THE RED PAGE.
lhat isW.K.’s valedictory rhyme : he definitely
.closed his riotous Australian chapter at the end of
last year, and returned to his father’s house oi
many scansions—but in German, not English,
hor a Deutscher born and bred (till nearly man-
hood) Yon Kotze wrote English verse remarkably
well, and with a fuller pulse than many indigenous
bards. Hot blood sent him early roving and husk-
eatmg : he came to North Queensland in search of
fortune, and almost before realising that he had
arrived lie found himself hoeing cane in a sugar-
plantation, with a kanaka on his right hand and
a low-caste Chinese on his left. The decade
during 1 winch lie drifted aliout New Guinea and
Northern Australia did not cool him, and memories
of Ins final departure from Sydney include a
red landscape flowing with many rivers. He met
others of the bardic sept, and read his own poems
in an ecstasy of emotion which prevented utter-
ance. You saw lips moving, but no voice issued.
W hat astounded him particularly was the ease
with which the Chief of the Bards composed
“ poetry while you wait.” “When I write a
poem, complained V.K., “I lie.on a sofa for
three days with the pains of a woman in childbed
and when 1 get up lam as weak as the baby.” Yet
he wrote; and often well. His farewell in N.Q.
Leinster held real affection for the country wherein
he was a sojourner.
VV hat loads of enthusiasm and virility have we not
carried out into the West and the North, among 1 the
plains and the ranges, and they all lie buried there, burnt
to cinders under the blazing sun, drowned in the endless
swamps of the Gulf rivers. Heigho ! What’s the good
of winning ? Has not our life been a free one, untram-
melled, clear of conventionalities and five-shilling din-
ners ? Have we not tasted the great, the glorious cup of
Freedom, that poets sing and patriots die for, to the very
dregs? Ah—the dregs ! There’s the rub ! Are there in
all this world chains of slavery that chafe the flesh more
cruelly than the invisible prison tethers of loneliness and
desolation ? We.lhv? poured our heart-blood out into
the wilderness, and the sand has sucked it up; we have
breathed out our soul into the desert, and it has dissolved
in the great stillness around us. And when we return to
humanity, we suddenly realise that this our soul is gone,
that we are only anaemic wrecks of former manhood, a
pale, brow-beaten, miserable band of convicts released
from 'the-torture dens of—Liberty. And so I return to
my refrain :I am out of it! lam out of it! And slowly
the coast line is sinking into the sea. There’s a bushfire
rising behind the brow of the last headland. It seems to
me I can smell the smoke from here—and it all comes
back, the camp, the glowing logs, the call of the curlew
down in the empty river-bed ! And once I remember,
when we had scrambled up that dry gully on the
Northern coastal range and came—but there ! I knew I
could smell the smoke ! It is biting my eyes, I feel it, for
they are full of tears. And—and—Goodbye ! God bless
you, Australia !
*
Wallace Henderson, who might be called the
English Prose Laureate, is an interesting func-
tionary in a newspaper age. It is his office
to concoct the touching little paragraphs
which wake the Great Heart of the British
People to proud astonishment that monarchs
are made of real flesh and blood—“ just the same
as we are ! ” Wallace is now a hale’ old fellow of
83 ; and the curious may meet him daily walking
along the Thames near Richmond—a hearty
“man frae Greenock,” who carries his years
nobly. The genesis of his office imparts a fine
moral lesson. Sixty years ago Wallace Hen-
derson was a budding poet lie is budding
still. The efflorescence of his faculty was
contemporaneous with the marriage - year of
Queen Victoria; and Henderson was so impressed
by the celebration of the marriage, which he saw
as a visitor to London, that he resolved to become
the Queen’s poet exclusively. He had a small in-
come, and could devote himself entirely to the
Work.
Thenceforward, at intervals of six months
or so, booklets of poetry arrived at Buckingham
Palace, Windsor, or wherever Queen Victoria
happened to be in residence. No one knows if the
poetry was good or bad, for only Queen Vic-
toria and the royal family have seen it. A
humble note with each booklet said that only
one copy had been printed, and the type
distributed. Persistent dripping will wear
away royalty, and after three or four years the
singularity of the.offering was noticed. Enquiries
were made, and the poet was permitted to see the
Queen, and was graciously, thanked. He re-
double;] his labours and produced a booklet every
three months, receiving continued tokens of royal
favour.
But about 1850, when Henderson had pub-
lished some forty volumes in editions of one,
he lost his income through the failure of a bank.’
This distress being made known, he was given
quarters at Hampton Court, with a pension of
£l5O a year; and tlie duty of People’s Paragraphs-
was assigned him. The choice proved excellent;
has * S S ' n office. Long experience
written 1 -”L an ac^e P t 5 an d his paragraphs are
becomes Vlth - a r skill which, once familiar,
Becomes unmistakable. His recent mra-
!KL about ** d « th » f vSEUK*
London K P a P er > then clipped and sent to
ai!:°r f the People,” or “A Loving
-l a®, n copied by every ‘‘loyal”
ran thus mt l 6 dominions. It originally
tbtf y ' i ."' w 4 itin B' afc the court vouches for the truth of
.. ,° °'' ln B affecting story. In thedark days preceding
old t n e^n Ullera1 ’ Prince Eddie > the little six-year-
, , -It. 11. the Duke of York, found his governess
rince Eddie is loved by all for his affectionate
posi ion , and he is particularly fond of his governess.
r tI P to her, and putting his arms round Miss ’s
fck, he said, “ Why are you crying, Dearest ? ” “ I am
crying because of a Great Sorrow,” answered the
governess. Prince Eddie thought for a moment, and then
asked, Dearest, what is Sorrow?” Miss . could not
rep y. Truly, many older and wiser heads than little
Prince Eddie’s might be puzzled by that which to us all
seems so simple—a Nation’s Sorrow !
wa ' s undoubtedly an impressive paragraph,
calculated to touch the hearts of all British
mothers. The assertion that Queen Victoria’s
last words were “ Albert ! Albert! Albert ! ”
was hardly so well invented : for many people it
slg ltly overshoots the mark. Henderson, who
has no objection to talking about his work, thinks
tiat his most successful paragraph was one which
followed the death of the Prince Consort, to this
effect :
It is not generally known that for three days after the
death of our noble Prince, Albert the Good, his little dog
“ Spot,” a faithful terrier, refused all nourishment. In
vain the most tempting - canine delicacies were put
before her : the poor animal turned away her head
with doleful howls which shocked everybody who
heard them. It was only at the close of the
third day "that a member of the household be-
thought herself to offer “Spot” a morsel of blood-
pudding—a favourite dish on the Royal table.
At the sight, a sudden train of recollection
seemed to flash through the little creature’s brain. She
walked round the blood-pudding mournfully, emitting
short, hoarse barks ; and finally “ ate it slowly,” says an
onlooker, “as if it were a sacrament.” When dumb
animals show such devotion, it is no wonder that
the nation has been rent to its depths by an unforgettable
grief.
Henderson calculates that this anecdote was re-
produced not less than 100,000 times, and it still
flourishes in biographies. Naturally, the old
gentleman is extravagantly proud of his office
and regards himself as one of the chief bonds
which unite Britons in devotion to the Crown.
Ihe legend of Royalty’s superhuman virtue
has been created, he contends, almost entirely by
his labours. Writing a really popular paragraph,
it appears, is the most difficult thing in
the world.; and Henderson rejects hundreds of
drafts before he is satisfied. The essential thing
is the homely reference—such as “blood-pudding”
—connected with some homely event. Royal
births, marriages, and deaths are Henderson’s
especial stimulants. A paragraph must be neither
too subtle nor too obvious ; and sometimes it is
very difficult to hit the proper medium. Hender-
son says his best things have flashed upon him at
early morning, after a sleepless night of invention.
His only fear is that no competent person will be
found to succeed him. Truly, an interesting pro-
duct of the age !

The request for a rhyme about Australian towns
brought nothing worth print. Doggerel like this
from “ Iris ” was the cream of receipts :
Others—from Led : C.H. ; W H • Bov •

P. P. G.
Oh, you with the wind of the plains in your hair,
And the scent of the bush in your breath,
I drink you a bumper, my wattle-buds fair
That bloom to the South of the Realm of Despair,
To the North of the Kingdom of Death !
Oh, girls of the bush ! In the West, in the West
I have built me a castle apart;
And when I am yearning and weary for rest,
I hearken me back as sweet memory's guest
And stay—for the good of my heart.
The desert encircles and holds you aloof,
Afar from the withering strife.
The bush is the wall and the sky is the roof,
And thus you are pure and your palace is proof
Against the defilement of life.
For, somehow, your kiss is a tenderer kiss
Than all the caresses I know j
And, somehow, you temper the passion of bliss
With peace, with content, with a something I miss,
Wherever, wherever I go.
Oh, girls of the bush, if you knew, if you knew
The dreams that we carry away !
When stages were long and oases were feiv,
One glimpse of your fluttering dresses would do
For many and many a day.
And you, with the stamp of the free on your brow
And the light of the sun in your eyes ;
You promised me once but what matters a voiv ?
I drink you farewell as I think of you notv,
And visions, old visions arise.
Berlin, If01.
VON KORZE.
A WESTRALIAN WAIL:FROM BUNBURY
First Effort —
I landed here a week ago,
M.v head is fairly staggered,
The nomenclature of this place
Would drive a nigger haggard.
There’s all the ’ups and ’ings and ’ya,
They spell confusion to mine eyes.
Quindalup, Darradup, Walkaway, Wandering,
Mourambine, Nullagine, Wagerup, Moojeebing,
Wongamine, Boyup Brook, Wonnerup, Daudabin,
Mean Mahn, Dwalginup, Oarrolup, and Jourokine.
Second Writhe —
There humpies are the favourite style,
With shingles red for roofing,
The walls are built of undressed slabs
And mud for weatherproofing.
Nabawah, Jumperding, Uduc and Quindanning,
Dardanup, Mornbekine, Coolup and Barljanning,
Wanneroo, Mogumber, Doodlekine, Marradong,’
Narra Tarra, Yardaring, Baliugup, Balladoiig.
N.S.W.
Country Press Ass.,
114a Pitt Street, Sydney.
There is sad humour all over this circular, and
most of it unconscious :
N.S.W. Country Press
Association.

Ars Artis Preservatrixi March U 'l9Ol
That rival newspaper man in your town ! You have a
poor opinion of him, haven’t you ? You have an idea that
he will take an advt. at any price, just' to get it from
you ! He plans and schemes to circumvent you in every
way ; and a thousand and one other little meannesses he
plays upon you.
Does it ever occur to you that much of his meanness is
due to the course you pursue? Both of you are pretty
good fellows, each eager to make money. Both could
insert Coy eminent advts. at the same price, instead of
one doing it at half rates, and the other for nothing and
apacket of cigarettes thrown in.
A set scale for advts. could be arranged and stuck to.
Fair prices for printing could be agreed upon, and in a
hundred and one ways each could help the other.
What would be the result ? You certainly would not
derive (sic) your competitor out of the town in a hurry.
Young Tom could get a bike, Cissy could take music
lessons, Dick could pursue his studies. The anxious
hard-working little wife at home would have an easier
time, a happier life, and a more hopeful future. While
for 3'ou there would be less work, more pleasure, more
money in your pocket, holiday-trip to Sydney now and
again, and a chance, of retiring from the frame or office
table in ample time to enjoy your declining years.
The N.S W. Country Press'Association is trying to, and
in fact is, lifting Cue Provincial press from the slough of
despond and lethargy into which it has drifted. Your
membership will greatly advance your chances of success.
If yon come in your rival will come in, and the thing is
done.
You have all the particulars ; send in your cheque.
George Wride, junr.,
Secretary.
Don’t forget the N.S.W. Country Press Conference at

FOR HTS GOOD.
“ I bring you the stately matron named Christendom,
returning bedraggled, besmirched, and dishonoured from
pirate raids in Kiao-Choil, Manchuria, South Africa, and
the Philippines, with her soul full of meanness, her pocket
full of ‘ boodle ’ and her mouth full of pious hypocrisies.
Give her soap and towel, but hide the looking-glass.”—
Mark Twain's Greeting to the Red Cross Society.
If you see an island shore
Which has not been grabbed before,
Lying in the track of trade as islands should,
With the simple native quite
Unprepared to make a fight,
Oh, yon just drop in and take it for his good.
Chorus.
Oh, you kindly stop and take it for his good,
Not for love of money, be it understood,
But you row yourself to land,
With a Bible in your hand,
And you pray for him, and rob him, for his good ;
If he hollers, then you shoot him—for his good.
There 've been sad and bloody seen,es
In the distant Philippines,
Where we’ve slaughtered thirty thousand for their good.
And, with bullet and with brand,
Desolated all the land :
But you know we only did it for their good.
Chorus (fortissimo, beginning with a howl) :
Ovv ! just club your gun and kill him, for his good ;
Don’t waste a cartridge, give him steel or wood.
When he’s wounded and he’s down
Brain him, ’cause his skin is brown,
But he careful that you do it for his good.
“Take no prisoners,” but kill them—for their good.
Yes, and still more far away,
Down in China, let us say,
Where the “ Christian ” robs the “ heathen ” for his good,
You may burn and you may shoot,
You may fill your sack with loot,
But be surq»you do it only for his good.
Chorus :
When you’re looting Chinese Buddhas for their good,
Picking opals from their eyeballs made of wood,
As you prise them out with care
Just repeat a little prayer,
To the purport that you do it for their good ;
Make your pocket-picking clearly understood.
Or this lesson I can shape
To campaigning at the Cape,
Where the Boer is being hunted for his good.
He would welcome British rule *
If he were n’t a blooming fool ;
Thus you see that it is only for his good.
Chorus (pianissimo) :
So they’re burning burghers’ houses for their good.
As they pour the kerosene upon the wood,
I can prove them, if I list,
Every man an altruist
Making helpless women homeless—for their good;
Leaving little children roofless- for their good.
MORAL.
There’s a moral to my song,
But it won’t detain you long.
For I could n’t make it plainer if I would.
If you dare commit a Wrong
On the weak becaus’e you ’re strong
You may do it—if you do it for his good.
You may rob him, if you do it for his good ;
You may kill him, if you do it for his good
You may forge and you may cheat;
You have only to repeat
This formula, “ I do it for your good.”
Crime is “ Christian ” when it’s really Understood.
Bertha’K!) Suadwell, in Chicago Record.

A quaint example of Dickens’s ebullient fancy
has been re-found by a London paper—a declara-
tion of his'“unhappy attachment ” to Queen
Victoria.
My dear Thompson.— Maelise and I are raving with love
for thS.yueen, with a hopeless passion whose extent no
tongue can tell, nor mind of man conceive. On Tuesday,
we sallied down to Windsor, prowled about the Castle'
saw the corridor and their private rooms- nay, the verv
bed chamber (which we know from having been there
twice), lighted up with such a ruddy, homely, brilliant
glow, bespeaking so much bliss and happiness, that I,
your humble servant, lay down in the mud at the top of
the Long Walk, and refused all comfort, to the immea-
sureable astonishment of a few straggling passengers who
had survived the drunkenness of the previous night.
After perpetrating some other extravagances, we returned
home in a postchaise, and now we wear marriage medals
next- our hearts, arid go about with pockets full of por-
traits, which we weep over in secret. Forster was with
us at Windsor, and (for the joke’s sake) counterfeits a
passion, too, but he does not love her.
Don’t mention this unhappy attachment. I am very
wretched, arid think of leaving my home. My wife
makes me miserable, arid when I hear the voice of my
infant children I burst into tears. I fear it' is too late to
ask you to take this house, now that you have made such
arrangements of comfort in Pall Mall, but if you will you
shall have it very cheap—furniture at a low valuation-
money not being so much an object as escaping from the
family. For God’s sake turn this matter over in your
mind, and please to ask Captain Kincaide what he asks—
his lowest terms in short for ready money—for that post
of Gentleman at Arms. I must he near her, and I see no
better way than that for the present.
I have in hand three numbers of “ Master Humphrey’s
Clock,” and the first two chapters of “ Bamaby.”
Would you like to buy them ? Writing- any more in my
present state of mind is out of the question. They are
written in a, pretty fair hand, and when I am in the Ser-
pentine may he considered curious. Name your own
terms.
I know you don’t like trouble, but I have ventured,
notwithstanding, to make you an executor of my will.
There won’t be a great deal to do, as there is no money.
There is a little bequest having reference to her which
you might like to execute. I have heard on the Lord
Chamberlain’s authority that she reads my books, and is
very fond of them. I think she will be sorry when lam
gone. I should wish to be embalmed, and to be kept (if
practicable) on the top of a triumphal arch at Bucking-
ham Palace when she is in town, and on the north-east
turret of the Round Tower when she is at Windsor.—
From your distracted and blighted friend, 0. D.
P.S.— If it comes to that don’t show this to the
coroner.

A piece of beautiful verse from the preface to
W. B. Yeats’s new play, “The Shadowy
Waters”—
How shall I name you, immortal, mild, proud Shadows?
I only know that all we know comes from you,
And that you come from Eden on flying feet.
Is Eden far away, or do you hide
From human thought, as hares and mice and coneys
That run before the reaping-hook and lie
In the last ridge of the barley ? Do our woods
And winds and ponds cover more quiet woods,
More shining winds, more star-glimmering ponds?
Is Eden out of time and out of space ?
And do you gather about us when pale light
Shining on water and fallen among leaves,
And winds blowing from flowers, and whirr of feathers
And the green quiet have uplifted the heart?
1 have made this-poem for you, that men may read it
Before they read of Forgael and Dectora,
As men in the old times, before the harps began,
Poured out wine for the high invisible ones.

the garbled quotations. The garbling seems character-
istic ; N.S.W. Railway Commissioners noted it years ago.
Evidently author was let off too lightly. But the attitude
of the unin telligen t Scotchman to Burns (B. never here dis.
praised, but admired for all his worth as a forcible writer
arid a splendid Man) will always remain askew. Super-
stition defies fact and argument. ..As Sydney Smith said,
“ If you take a. blowfly and look at it through a micro-
scope, you will see twenty or thirty ugly little insects
crawling on it. These insects think their blowfly is the
grandest and most important thing in the universe,
and they believe the whole world would come to an
end at once the moment their fly ceased to buzz.” Burns
was a fine fellow ; but he has damnable parasites... .Out-
law : “Why attribute to ‘Mr. Anonymous’ those
affecting verses ‘The Night Before Larry was Stretched’?’
Because J. S. Farmer rightly if ungrammatically says
(Note to “Musa Pfedestris, ’ 1896) that “Neither the
authorship nor the date of these inimitable verses is
definitely known. According to the best authorities,
Will. Maher, a shoemaker of Waterford, wrote the song.
Dr. Robert Burrowes, Dean of St. Finbar’s, Cork, to
whom it has been so often, attributed, certainly did not.”
Have read elsewhere that Burrowes was the last man in
the v orld to write such verses, being himself a supra-
solemn clerical joke, wherefore wicked “ Father Prout ”
nailed him as author in Fraser’s Maya. V.T. :

(9 t) Robert Buchanan writes in “ Julia Cytherea” (of
the Roman maiden dug up in the Appian Way)—
“ Golden her hair with light from heaven
As if she slept but yestereven.”
And Julia had been in the grave (say) a thousand years.
.... H.,; “ Kropotkin’s autobiog, as an anarchist leader, is
reprinted, in two vols., from Atlantic Monthly. The
first is much the better. The opening chapters would
splendidly lead off a romance about Old Russia, with the
description of Moscow and its society, when Kropotkin
was reared there, in the purple. From his own know-
ledge of them he gives the characteristics of Nicholas I,
Alexander 11, and Alexander 111, with much point.
Nicholas would walk \lone, with impunity, about
Petersburg at any time, and without an attempt
at disguise. Kropotkin was a page to Alexander 11. He
could have entered the Guards, but picked a Cossack
regiment on the Amur. This enables him to pourtray the
great Russian scientists who did so much for the
national advance into Asia, and Kropotkin himself won
considerable distinction among them. In analysing him-
self, he gives the first place to Goethe’s ‘ Faust’ as the
book which set him thinking. In every phase of his re-
cord he is vivid and incisive, especially with the angles
of his bits of description. He might have been a
novelist to rank with Gogol or Turgenieff. As a writer
he makes.one think a good deal of G. W. Steevens”
S.A. : There are British officers and “ offisahs ” ; and it is
the “ officah ” who calls A. G. Hales “ Australian cad.”
Sam Johnson remarked to effect that, when plain speech
knocks the wind out of noodles, their breath comes back
with abuse. Comparing Kipling’s experience in India, it
seems that a “cad” is sometimes a civilian with too
much brains for military environment W.J,: Em

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When “Bill the Smelter” (W. H. S. Os-
mand, Victorian M.L.C.) was a youth in Ply-
mouth, Eng., be had a sweetheart whom he called
“Cushie Doo,” and to whom he confided that, if
ever he were lucky, he intended to buy a steam-
yacht and call it by that name. Long years after-
wards, when the sweetheart was probably the
mother of nine, he bought a steam-yacht of 500
tons, and called it the “ Cushie Doo.” After two
years’ luxurious travelling, he resided on the
vessel in Hobson’s Bay till the barnacles on her
bottom were 4ft. thick. Then he tired of the
business altogether and sold her.
The Friend.
I had a vision terrible : I saw
My body lying stark upon a bed.
Its lips were pale ; its brow was bound with awe ;
I knew that it was dead.
Then through me, like a flame, there swept a gust
Of pity for that life outworn and done ;
For that poor body, soon to lie in dust,
And see no more the sun.
The eyes were sightless that had pierced tor me
The purple veils that hide the face of Sin.
And white as wax the ears that avidly
The song of Lust drank in.
The brow, that once was clear as cloudless day,
Was scored with crooked lines of grief and care ;
And round the temples clustered locks of gray,
Unvenerable hair.
The mouth was pale and cold, that once was red
With scarlet kisses and with crimson wine ;
And like a marble idol’s was the head
Whose evil thoughts were mine.
Still were the hands that plied the shuttle free,
And wove the web of wicked deeds and fell;
Still were the feet that swiftly trod for me
The Primrose Way to Hell.
Alas ! when first that tenement was mine,
That lay a sad, unlovely ruin there,
It was a Temple Beautiful, a Shrine
Stainless, and bright, and fair.
Swift judgment well I knew awaited me—
Swift judgment and a doom of dule and dread ;
But, filled with sorrow, I could only see
That form upon the bed.
Then suddenly, while I, with memories drear,
Gazed on that liody I had foully wronged—
I grew aware of Spectral Shapes of Fear
That swiftly round me thronged.
The brows of one with vine-leaves sere were
bound,
One who trod softly wore a poppy-tiar,
And one —a golden Woman-Shape—was crowned
With roses all of fire.
And all were vulture-faced ; yet, dire to tell,
I knew those faces and those eyes that burned ;
In seemed as if old friends, by some black spell,
Had been to demons turned.
Alas, alas, upon the Primrose Way,
Nor man nor woman of the future heeds ;
I knew them all too well again, for they
Were mine own evil deeds.
They came to claim me as their Chief and Head,
And drag me with them down the dolorous road
That leads, beyond the Kingdom of the Dead,
Unto the Drear Abode.
Methought I struggled madly to escape
The burning talons of that dreadful pack,
When forth there stood a shining Angel-Shape
That waved them sternly back.
“ And who art thou ?” I said, “ Good Friend to
me! ”
The Angel answered low :
“lama Deed of Mercy done by thee,
Many a year ago.”
I woke. Yet well I knew the sight I saw ;
It was not all a vision false and vain,
And I went forth, with spirit touched with awe,
Into the world again.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Nocturne.
On the mountain crest the shadow
Of victorious Morning clings;
And deep, moated gullies tremble
To the beat of ebon wings.
Through the still, expectant forest —
Faint and luminous, afar,
Down the pallid West slow sinking,
Sets a solitary star.
Yester-eve —my camp-fire over—
Glowed that lone star's ardent ray;
Now the dead, white ashes scatter
And the star sets cold and grey.
Oh, my heart, thou throne of Passion !
Yester-eve thou too wert fire,
But this Morn thou boldest only
Dead white ashes of Desire.
In the West a pale star, fading,
Flickers, trembles, fails—and dies.
In the East a red star burning,
Fair as Love, doth slowly rise.
N.S.W.
E. J. BRADY.

SATURDAY, APRIL 6, 1901.
Well, Australia! How Are You?
The Commonwealth elections are over ; ana
the result meets reasonable anticipation.
The Barton Ministry will have a substantial
majority in the House of Representatives,
and a sufficient majority in the Senate ; and
whether those majorities are maintained will
depend upon—the Barton Ministry. Not
upon the Ministry’s policy of Freetrade or
Protection : that was one of the issues at
the elections, and the newspapers of N.S.
Wales and Victoria, inheriting the disastrous
legacy of provincial politics, foisted it into
undeserved prominence. For, if an eight-
million tariff has to be raised at the Customs,
the difference between Freetrade and Pro-
tection will be practically little more than
the difference between Tweedle-dum and
Tweedle-dee. Presumably, had George
Reid headed the first Australian Ministry he
would have endeavoured to exclude the Pro-
tectionist tariff-tendency ; but he could not
have excluded it wholly—any more than he
excluded it when as N.S.W. Premier he
maintained the duty on sugar. Theoreti-
cally, as George Reid knew, the advent of
Freetrade meant the removal of N.S.W.
sugar duties. Practically, as George Reid
discovered and admitted, it is one thing to
talk theoretical Freetrade on a platform, and
quite another thing to destroy a productive
industry. So George Reid maintained
N.S.W. sugar duties—that is, in regard to
sugar duties, he was as much of a Freetrader
as the Facts permitted him to be. Barton
will be just as much of a Protectionist as the
Facts permit him to be. That is all the
difference between them in that respect; and
when the Eight-million tariff comes to be
drafted, it will be found that the Facts give
a very small margin of political play be-
tween Protection on the one hand and Free-
trade on the other. As both Reid and Bak-
ton very well know.
* • * *
The Bulletin is glad that the tendency of
the first Australian Parliament is towards
Protection, because it is quite clear that a
policy of Protection—as far as circumstances
permit—is the best policy for Australia to
follow. National progress should continue
® ver y possible point. The physically-
perfect man is not a cripple : he has not a
magnificent torso and shrunken limbs, or
good limbs with a contracted chest. So the
economically-perlect nation does not develop
its industries in one direction, while
neglecting other directions in which de-
velopment is just as necessary. The
most vital industries are always those
which actually afford national subsistence by
drawing wealth from the land—agricultural,
pastoral, mineral. But the farmer does not
live by bread alone ; the pastoralist does not
live by wool ; the miner does not live by
gold or coal. They all demand manufac-
tured products ; they demand an exchange
of products. Clothes and boots, furniture
and tools, are not essentially necessaries of
life ; but they are necessaries of civilisation
which have practically become necessaries of
life. The men who produce these things, by
an exchange of products, are practically as
necessary to primary producers as the
primary producers are to them. The aim of
Protection is to encourage national indus-
tries in all directions ; and a well-devised
system of Protection can do this. What
father of a family, because he has some
strong sons and some less strong or
weakly, would nourish the stronger at
the expense of the weaker who can
become strong and useful ? Every nation is
a family of industries, to be reared as a just
father rears his sons and daughters. And
just as a father’s protection is repaid by his
family, so a Nation’s Protection is repaid by
the Nation.
* * * m
N.S. Wales, under the influence of Syd-
ney’s daily papers—whose wealth and in-
fluence depend upon the aggrandisement of
the port of Sydney—does not yet under-
stand that. The N.S. Wales Federal vote
is clearly in favor of Freetrade. Australia
as a whole understands it; and the Austra-
lian Federal vote is clearly in favor of Pro-
tection. By'far the most important lesson
of the N.S. Wales vote is this: If Protec-
tionists wish to win that State they must at
any cost establish a Protectionist morn-
ing paper. The evening Star does not
count outside Sydney, and it counts for
very little inside Sydney. The Herald
and the Telegraph decided the vote
of Sydney for Freetrade ; and the vote
of Sydney decided the Senate election.
The country districts did not poll as they
polled in the Federal Convention elec-
tion ; but if they had the result would not
have been materially different. The Herald
and Telegraph flood N.S. Wales with argu-
ment and illustration as far as the N.S.
Wales border ; and there their influence
stops. If Barton neglects to put the estab-
lishment of a Sydney morning Protectionist
paper among the very first of Things to Do,
he will continue to lose, and the Protection-
ist policy will continue to lose, the support
of N.S. Wales. The Bulletin’s opinion is
that, as a newspaper enterprise, such a paper
would pay handsomely if conducted with
brains ; it would pay even if (and perhaps
because) a separate cable service had to be
established. The Commonwealth Premier
is counselled to paste “ Daily Paper ” on his
dinner-plate,
* * * *
Just as Freetrade heredity, and a Free-
trade press, cast the N.S. Wales vote for
Freetrade ; so a Protectionist heredity, and
a Protectionist press, cast the Victorian
vote for Protection. And “Victorian”
means Melbourne, just as Freetrade means
“Sydney.” Lacking the trade-advantages
of Sydney port, Melbourne has turned
especially to manufactures ; and the Age,
like the Telegraph, knows on which side
its bread is buttered. Melbourne will have
to learn that, in the interests of Australia,
Protection does not necessarily mean High
Tariff, just as Sydney has learned that
“ Freetrade ” does not mean, in the eyes of
its exponents, no tariff at all. Around his
tumbler Barton is counselled to engrave
“ Australia First.”
* * * *
In South Australia and in Tasmania, as
shown by the list of results, the fiscal issue
was not the chief issue. The electors voted
in accordance with their predilections, and
the tariff cry had little force. Tasmania re-
turns to the Senate three Freetraders and
three Protectionists. South Australia does
the same. Barton can remember, without
writing it on his serviette, that from South
Australia and Tasmania a reasonably honest
and enlightened democratic policy will meet
with consistent support.
* * * •
It was in Queensland, and perhaps in
Westralia, that the most significant results
were shown. In Queensland, Freetrade and
Protection practically did not count at all.
It was Black Man’s Country versus White
Man’s Country; and the vote for a White
Man’s Country was overwhelming. Despite
the continued distrust of a considerable body
of electors, theLaborParty “swept the polls.”
Two of their “ bunch ” of three topped the
Senate poll; Drake and Glassey are, the one
a strong Democrat, and the other an ex-
member of the Labour-party opposed to
black labour; and the sole candidate
elected on the Government side is a
colourless man of no significance. The third
Labour candidate, Stewart, came fourth
on the list in spite of consider-
able animosity roused by his per-
sistent denunciations of Australian inter-
ference in the Transvaal war. Thynne,
Bartholomew, Hamilton, Plant the
whole tribe of black-labour advocates—were
left gibbering in outer darkness. And the
Labour party secured five out of nine seats
in the House of Representatives. No clearer
manifestation of Queensland opinion could
be given. On his knife-handle Barton can
write “The Asiatic goes at once,” and on
his fork-handle, “The Kanaka goes as
quickly as possible.”
• • • *
Westralia showed an apparent preference
for Freetrade candidates, electing four
nominal Freetraders and one Protectionist
to the House of Representatives, and five
Freetraders and one Protectionist to the
Senate. In part, this was an accident of
politics : the electors preferred men, rather
than fiscal policies. In part, it was a
recoil from the Protectionist Forrest ad-
ministration. But in part also—and
here the significance lies—it was a
natural protest against the so-called
Protective duties which have helped to
make living in interior Westralia the most
expensive living in this country. In Wes-
tralia Freetrade has been connected, among
other ideas, with the idea of fresh meat free
of the Sand-groping squatter’s stock-tax.
And since Westralians have hardly any local
manufactures, they want their imported
goods with as little tariff as possible. So
the Westralian vote, in its fiscal aspect, was
a purely local vote. The moral which
Barton can wrap round his cigars is that
Protection is not synonymous with Customs-
duties ; and that duty-pressure on Austra-
lian primary-industries is only to be justified
by an equivalent expansion of secondary-
industries—or by sheer revenue-necessity.
* » * *
On the whole, The Bulletin thinks Aus-
tralia has done very well. She has struck a
keynote of national policy Protection
against the Cheap Local Alien and the
Foreign Cheap Laborer ; and it remains for
the first Commonwealth Ministry to play
the tune. In the Commonwealth Parlia-
ment they may not have the best possible in-
strument. but it is as good as could fairly be
expected : ably and honestly handled, it is
good enough to produce, with whatever flaws
and wheezes, a fit National Anthem for the
audience of Australian Democracy.
“ White Australia! ” —With a Black
Smudge.
Politics is a game, and politicians are gamblers.
Probably, nearly all politicians would prefer to
win with honest cards ; but the essential thing is
to win. So nearly all of them “stack” their
principles to suit the circumstances of the game.
* • * *
When all is said, George Reid is probably as
honest as most other politicians—that is to say,
he is as honest as he can afford to be, or thinks he
can afford to be. Confronted at the Federal elec-
tion with the general sentiment in favour of a
White Australia on the one hand, and the desira-
bility of collecting Queensland coloured - labor
votes for his supporters on the other hand, George
Reid decidedjto be a white politician with a black
smudge. He explicitly condemned Asiatic labor.
With regard to Kanaka labor he temporised.
“ He would go and see the thing for himself ”
after the elections.
* * * *
Therein George Reid blundered, as The
Bulletin could have warned him, and as the
Queensland election has proved. The Queensland
electors have given an overwhelming vote against
colored labor. They would have given a similar
vote at any time during Queensland’s history had
they been asked on a single-voting basis. It is
property-voting, “ cooked ” electoral-rolls, and the
corrupt influence of financial institutions upon
Parliament, which account for the maintenance
of colored labor in Queensland. So George Reid
“stacked ” his principle-cards to no purpose. He
gained nothing from Queensland ; and he showed •
to the other States that he was prepared to risk a
most vital principle of Australian welfare—the
Purity of the White Breed—for the purpose of
catching votes to aid him to power.
• * *' *
It has occurred to The Bulletin that the arena
of Federal politics, hedged by manhood suffrage
on a single-voting basis, offers a good opportunity
of success to any politician who will try the ex-
periment of being entirely honest. Even in State
politics, dishonesty rarely pays in the long run.
In the new National politics, it is not likely to
pay at all. There are too many eyes on the cards.
The temporary and local exigencies of any dis-
trict or State will not prevail over obvious
National Principles in the minds of all-Australia.
The State politician has been in the habit of
trusting himself. The Commonwealth politician
will have to trust the people.
* * * •
George Reid has not yet learned that. So he
will make up his mind in favor of a White Aus-
tralia —a Completely White Australia—after the
elections. The smudge will remain on his face
until he comes back from Queensland.
*■ * * *
When George Reid goes to Queensland to in-
quire into the question of Kanaka labor, what
will he find ? He will go to Bundaberg ; and as
Charles Powers (then leader of Q. Opposition in
Parliament; and many years resident in the Bun-
daberg district) said in 1895 : “A trip to Bunda-
berg will introduce you to a Queensland town in
which no woman or young girl dare venture about
at night without a protector for fear of being in-
decently assaulted by the walk-about kanakas.”
Enquiring of impartial people, he will find that
statement just as true to-day. The men who thrive
on Kanakas may tell him that it is an exaggeration;
but two questions by George Reid, that excellent
cross-examiner, will show him that the Kanakas’
advocates observe it more carefully than a reli-
gion, when applied to their own wives and
daui/hters. Interviewing the local sergeant of
police, George Reid will learn that as late as
last Christmas Eve, about one thousand Kanakas,
maddened by alcohol and opium, took possession
of Bundaberg township, brawling, thieving, riot-
ing, defying the police, and keeping the
white residents in a state of panic behind
locked doors. After his dinner, The Bul-
letin hopes George Reid, in the com-
pany of his friendly police-sergeant, will stroll
round Bundaberg’s houses of Kanaka prostitution.
There he will see the girl-daughters of white
working-men (no rich men’s children —no relatives
of Brisbane newspaper-proprietors, or of Parlia-
mentary black-labor advocates) who have been
borne in travail by some poor mother, nounshed
by some toiling father, and educated at Q State-
schools —to satisfy the natural lust of the wifeless
and womanless Kanakas.
* * * *
Proceeding up the Q. coast, with the black-
labor advocates’ affirmation that white men
cannot perform the labor of cultivating sugar-
cane in his mind, Geokge Reid will come to the
small farmers’ plantations which feed the Mackay
central mills. There he will learn with interest
that white men can and do perform all the
very labor which it has been proved by the Rule
of Imagination impossible for them to perform.
He will find that the labor is severe, though not
more severe than a dozen other occupations (min-
ing, stoking, scrub-clearing—for example) which
white men continuously perform in the tropics;
and that the man w r ho can afford to pay
a laborer naturally employs a laborer, colored
for preference. But he will learn, from the
evidence of his own eyesight, the great truth
that the sugar industry is in every detail as much
a white man’s industry as a black man’s industry—
if only it is carried on under white men's condi-
tions and pays white men’s wages. Should he
happen to travel by night the roads in the
Mackay district, George Raid’s keen in-
telligence will note something missing. All
over Australia at night you will see the red
dottels of swagmen’s fires. All over settled Aus-
tralia men lie down in the bush, under the sky,
and sleep in peace and safety till morning. All
over settled Australia—with the exception of the
Q. sugar districts. There the swagmen seek shel-
ter in houses and towns. On enquiry, George
Reid will learn that in the Kanaka a savage’s
murderous instincts are dormant ; and that he
gratifies them (as at Ayr, Q., the other day) by
hacking solitary white men into small pieces with
a cane-knife.
* * • *
Continuing his journey, George Reid will come
to Geraldton. When his steamer enters the
Johnstone, the first thing that will strike George
Reid is that the Johnstone is a river in China—
such a crowd of Chinese sampans will surround the
vessel. Watching the steerage passengers leave the
steamer, he will count probably three Japanese,
two Chinese, two Kanakas, and one white man;
and the white man will look very sorry for him-
self. On enquiry, George Reid will learn that
the steamer steerage-accommodation between
Townsville and Thursday Island is practically
monopolised by colored laborers. Reaching
Cairns, and travelling by rail to the Barron Falls,
he will learn that the second-class carriages are
entirely given over to Chinese—the very meanest
white being practically compelled to pay the heavy
first-class fare. Going along the course of the
Barron, he will be able to see the numerous huts
where the Chinese live with white wopien pros-
tituted to their service; and returning to Cairns
he will be told of the interesting Kanaka riot a
few months ago, when a mob of Kanakas chased
the police to the police-station with knives and
broken bottles—putting the town in terror —and
afterwards barricading themselves for a siege.
* * * *
Thursday Island is the place where George
Reid will look round for a white face, for Thursday
Island is a Japanese community—Japanese per-
forming nearly every service, and monopolising
nearly every trade. (Previously, in his Townsville
hotel, of course, George Reid will have noted
that the cook, the waiter, the housemaid, the
froom, are all Japanese.) John Douglas will tell
im all about the affray between the Thursday
Island Kanakas and Manila men at the beginning
of this year ; with the police powerless, a jewel-
ler’s shop raided, eight men badly injured, and
two deaths resultant.
* * * *
Coming back in the steamer, George Reid will
doubtless ponder all these things in the light of
the fact shown by the 1891 census that one in every
three adult males in Northern Queensland is a
coloured alien; and The Bulletin fancies that he
will begin to think very seriously about his
smudge. And as George Reid has brains, The
Bulletin is reasonably sure that by the time he
reaches Sydney he will add the weight of his
endorsement to the memorable declaration by
Samuel Walker Griffith (formerly Premier,
now Chief Justice of Queensland) —
“ After a very full consideration of the matter, I
am convinced that it is impossible for an inferior
servile class, and a free white laboring population,
to permanently exist side by side with one another.”
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Old Age Pensions.
It seems, as a rule, impossible to get human
sympathy, charity, or worldly common-sense out
of a paper run by a parson. Brisbane Telegraph
and Week are generally understood to be run by
a number of parsons, and, as a natural con-
sequence, presumably, the two rags mentioned
are always agin everything calculated to lift
humanity out of the particular ditch in which it
pleased “the Board ” to dump it. All improve-
ments in the social machine, all suggestions for
the welfare of anybody but us and ours are, in
the opinion of parsonically-bossed prints, a loath-
some and irreverent interference with the divine
regulation of things. Any attempts to abolish
poverty are especially irreverent, because the
average parsonical mind has been trained to see
in poverty the manifestation of the anger of an
outraged Deity. The belief that all human
wretchedness is the outcome and result and
proper punishment of Sin has become a fetish ;
and to battle with Poverty and try to do away
with it is derogatory to the fetish. "The Old Age
Pension system is looming large in the Australia’s.
Where it has arrived it has come to stay, and
where it has not already arrived it can be seen
approaching very fast. So Brisbane Week (the
weekly edition of the ignorant, hide-bound Tory
Telegraph) rises up and curses Old Age Pensions
with a loud and raucous curse. It screeches that
the bulk of those who require Old Age Pensions
are a bad lot. A hunk of its screech :
They comprise those whose lives have been one lone-
meteoric course of moral ineptitude, who are, and always
have been,-entirely beyond the reach of reformation, and
of help. For such there are souphouses, benevolent
asylums, and Dunwich. Those places are just as much the
natural habitat of such as mud is of the eel. The diffi-
culty always has been legislatively to separate and pro-
vide for the two extremes without laying the country
open to a charge of injustice in regard to the intermediate
denizens of pauperdom. Maugre all legislative expedients
those denizens will ever be what they are. It is useless
to put the country to any moral qualms regardin'-- them
They are happy only in the sphere in which their morai
ineptitude has fitted them. We cannotwaste sympathy
much less good State treasure, upon such. Souphouses’
benevolent asylums, and Dunwiches must still be main-
tained for them, but no more can be done. For the
others, much good can be done by a system of pensions


much sacred affection can be preserved, and much pood
citizenship properly recognised and acknowledged. The
Melbourne experience should be carefully borne in mind
by our own social reformers.
Summed up, it all amounts to this : No man
can honestly. say what brought another to
ruin, and it is practically impossible in
everyday life to divide the sheep from
the goats. The reason of 20 years ago
does not matter much, the community has to deal
with the result or the effect. The soup-kitchen,
the benevolent asylum, and Dunwich asylum
are all merely badly-organised efforts to do some-
thing the community recognises as absolutely
necessary. The gang that runs the Week might
have no objection to seeing people lying dead in
the gutter from the effects of starvation, but the
ordinary man is not built that way, and prefers
to feed those who cannot feed themselves, even
if it is their own fault they cannot do so, rather
than have his path blocked with emaciated corpses
and importuning beggars. The first results of
that impulse to do something were the soup-
houses, the benevolent asylums and the Dun-
wiches. But humanity cannot stand still: it must
either advance or recede, and just now, despite
papers like the Week, it is advancing. People
recognise that the vicissitudes of fortune are
beyond human control, and that all of us are pretty
well tarred with the same brush of wasted oppor-
tunity and dragged down by the same dreary load
of ventures that failed. We have all spent cash
on pet sins that we ought to have put in the hank,
and to-day Jones sees Brown go down through a
combination of circumstances over which he had
no control—uniting, perhaps, with a few sins that
have suddenly found him out; and Jones reflects
and shivers as it strikes him like a bullet that to-
morrow some of his pet sins may come home to
roost and weigh him down till some other com-
bination of circumstances suddenly grips him,
drags him from his little, brief affluence, and
sends him forth on a dreary world, a pauper in his
declining years. If he is a sensible man he recog-
nises that it is better and more humane to pro-
vide in a decent and dignified manner for
Brown and all others who may he wrecked
on the rocks of circumstance, because he him-
self may he wrecked some day. This is
the prompting of a higher, better - developed,
and more far-seeing sense of instinct of self-
preservation. When people vote for the
establishment of Old Age Pensions they are not
doing a charity to those who have gone under,
they are effecting a national endowment policy for
every man and woman in the community—insur-
ing themselves against starvation and pauperism
in their own sere and withered old age. The
well-fed parsons who run the Week and the Tele-
graph may yet, through the grace of God, be
flung down into the gutter of poverty to realise
the unholy uncharitableness of their present atti-
tude on the Old Age Pension question. The
drought in Queensland has ruined hundreds, if
not thousands, of the hardest-working and most
thrifty of her population, and those who are too
old to start again, if they have been in the habit
of taking a little der-rink, are, according to the
Satanic Week, fit cases for “soup-houses, benevo-
lent asylums—and Dunwich.”
♦
Socialism in Maoriland.
Maoriland still hums along in the vanguard of
democratic progress. In the old days of Fat
Man the constant argument was that every
individual must bow down to the great law of
Supply and Demand. If men required 50s. per
week wages to live properly, and the capitalist
could get men to work for 30s. per week, he was
not bound morally or legally to recognise the fact
that the 30s. was too little to live upon. He was
perfectly justified in buying in the cheapest
market and applying the irresistible suasion of
unlimited competition to beat down wages and
prices, and do the best for his own particular busi-
ness. To-day the exploiter is having his old-time
argument used against himself ruthlessly. There
has been an agitation for cheaper cable communi-
cation with Australia. M.L. Postmaster-General
Ward proposed that the co. should cable 12 words
for 6d. between the Islands and the Con-
tinent. The Eastern Extension Co., which con-
trols the cable, nearly fell down dead at such an
outrageous proposal, and as soon as it got
its breath indited a withering reply to the effect
that the co. was there to make cash and not to
foster the business relations of Maoriland and
Australia : it declined to reduce its rates on the
ground that any reduction would mean a loss.
The Postmaster-General has replied that he re-
grets they cannot make it pay at the rate of 12
words for sixpence, but that is the rate the people
of Maoriland would like to have the business done
at, and as the co. can’t do it at that rate, the
people of Maoriland will see about laying down a
cable of their own. A Bill is to be introduced
into the local Parliament to authorise Govt, to
buy the co. out, or if that is inconvenient or im-
possible, to lay down a State cable and simply run
the syndicate affair right out of the business. Then,
in the matter of coal, the State has taken
a hand. There has been a big demand for M.L.
fuel lately, and the prices to the local consumer
have gone up accordingly, except where the
colliery-owners were tied by contracts. One R. J.
Seddon, vulgarly known as “King Dick,” ob-
served, with pain, that while as Minister of Rail-
ways he obtained the best coal at 21s. per ton, he
was compelled to pay 40s. per ton for the fuel
with which Mrs. King Dick had the roast cooked.
This quite satisfied him that something was
wrong, and that the yell of the Wellington house-
holder was a justifiable and proper yell. The Goal
Co.’s got thehint that they were merely the tenants
of the people of Maoriland, and if they couldn’t
see their way to supply their landlords at a reason-
able rate the lease might be cancelled or some-
thing else might happen. Just now there
is a bitter cry going up from the colliery -
owuers to the effect that they do not
make very huge profits, and that these
threats were quite unjustifiable. However, King
Dick, still smarting under the 40s. per ton, and
his ears filled with the curses of the consumers,
sails serenely on. Men have been set to work at
Mokihinui and Cardiff in mines that were aban-
doned and declared no good, and thousands of
tons have been raised and sold under the market
price which had been artificially forced up. Hav-
ing started, the Government finds it cannot stand
still, and already the demand has been made that
the State shall ship and distribute the coal as well
as tear it out of the earth. Altogether, it looks as
though Maoriland is about to fall into the paradise
of State Socialism wholly.

A Judge in a Tantrum.
At the recent Ararat (Vic.) assizes Judge
Williams was obviously in a vile temper. A
lad of 16, convicted of seducing a girl under legal
age, was stated by the medical superintendent of
Ararat lunatic asylum and by a local doctor to he
mentally and physically not-fully developed, “ he
was what was recognised as a moral imbecile.”
Judge Williams ordered him five years imprison-
ment 1 The next case was precisely similar save
that the man was a few years older and there was
no imbecility in the case. The customary damsel
“about 15, but looking much older,” said she had
walked home with accused on the customary
Sunday evening and the customary result is
alleged to have ensued. She didn’t mention
the matter till some months later and
then she straightly charged accused , with
forcibly assaulting her. She said it hadn t oc-
curred to her to mention the circumstance to
her mother, or even to some friends they had met
on the way home, also that she parted from her
assaulter “fairly good friends.” The jurymen
bearing in mind the previous shocking sentence on
a semi-imbecile —debated long. Possibly they
reckoned the girl’s bare assertion that the prisoner
was the cause of her trouble insufficient. Possibly
they were afraid to trust his Honor should
give accused many years right off. They were
finally discharged without agreement. Then this
serene dispenser of justice up-ended himself and
froze on to them with great vigor. (Be it remem-
bered, the accused was remanded, and still awaits
another trial.) Said Judge Williams: “This
was a case in which he could not understand any
reasonable man having any reasonable doubt. . . .
He was surprised they had not been able to come
to a conclusion. . . . When a case was plain be-
yond reasonable doubt it was their duty to convict.
Their daughters were liable to be seduced, and the
man went free. . . There were cases where the jury
ought to trust the Judge, audit was well-known
that where the girl was well grown and a consent-
ing party lie always made the punishment as
lenient as he could. The probability now was
that the young man, if found guilty, would not
get a lenient sentence. He would have to take
his trial before another Judge at Ballarat, and
might get a severer sentence.” Also he may come
before the same Judge (at Ballarat, in June next),
and if so ! A more shameless attempt
to prejudge a case against a man then awaiting
trial is happily rare. It was Judge Williams
who, the previous week, sentenced a weak-
minded lad at Horsham to three years and a
flogging for a similar offence, and, considering
these two sentences and his attempt to procure a
conviction by reference to his “well-known leni-
ency ” (Williams’ well-known leniency !) it looks
as though his Honor was starting t*> blossom out
as a humorist.
A Seasonable Prayer.
The Australian Premiers should lose no time in
getting an assurance that the York bag of tricks
will not contain any baronetcies. If this country
draws a hard and fast line at baronetcies the
institution of knighthood for “distinguished
Australians” will eventually perish of ridicule.
Every honest thinker —“loyal” and otherwise —
recognises the fact that knighthood must always
be in the nature of a British Government degree
for grovellers, even though it were only conferred,
hereabouts, as a recognition of valuable public
service. An Australian Premier who gets the
“ Sir ” is (or ought to be) in his purely political
aspect as much entitled to titular distinction as a
doctor of medicine or of laws, or an army or navy
officer. The handle drops off his family name
when he dies, as with the other fellow’s.
If it didn’t reduce him in a sense to the
level of the bloated British soap-boiler
who is knighted because he happens to
be mayor of a town when the King comes
along to open a hospital, or what not, and who
would miss the “honor” if he were a notorious
first-class democrat, the K.C.M.G. degree for a
George Turner or George Dibbs might be
endorsed by the public opinion of the Common-
wealth. Under present conditions it excites con-
tempt, or at least dissatisfaction, in the best of
cases —indeed, the better the case, the greater the
reason why the “honor” should be snorted at.
Still we can afford to wait for common sense to
work its will as regards life titles. The alleged
bundle of knighthoods which the Duke of York is
bringing to Australia along with the “£20,000
worth of presents ” and other baggage should do
good, not harm, to the democratic cause by
attracting notice to the real purpose thereof,
which is the reward of snobbish “Loyalty.” The
Bulletin devoutly prays that many knights of
the Bingy Benjy and Matthew Davies order
may result from the poor little Duke of York’s
visit. But against the possibility of any more
baronetcies being introduced into Australia’s
social life this journal begs to enter a violent pro-
test. God save the Commonwealth from the
grovelsome influence of the “honor” that de-
scends from father to son, gaining a pestilent,
spurious dignity with age ! To parody the late
Laureate’s verse —
Knighthood is but for a time,
A folly that quickly blows,
But the baronet’s title sticks,
And the baronet’s title grows.
Human nature in this young country is much
about as weak as elsewhere, and it must be pro-
tected from the poisonous gas of hereditary dis-
tinctions. Melbourne already knows what this
means. S’Rupekt Clarke, son of S’William, is
an object lesson'on the insidious effects of Rank in
a strange atmosphere. The browless baronet
holds a place apart from all other local land-
owners. Fools may laugh at the respect, often
an involuntai-y respect, shown to this deplorable
“accident of birth,” but wise men pray, “Goo
save us ! ”
The Slave State of Wesfcralia.
The Bulletin receives this letter from the land
of the Groper slaveholder and his “indentured”
nigger :
I anxiously scanned the Federal debates but failed to
find that any attention had been given to the Native
trouble in this State. Why so grave a matter should
have been ignored is passing strange now that the dawn
of the century greets the brand-new Constitution, and
from henceforth a blot on any one unit will prejudice the
Commonwealth in the world’s eye.
Some years ago Government inoculated this State with
the social ulcer of slavery, for no other apparent object
than to provide low-priced labor to the greedy crowd,
who, not coatent with oceans of land at a nominal rent,
must needs get labor on similar terms. It might have
sufficed to rob the rightful owner of his country without
handing him over to the tender mercies of the slave-owner
and the nigger-driver.
The observation of.years proves that the everyday life
of the “ apprentice” native is physically worse than that
of the African slave of bygone years. True, those were
mere chattels, but even that grim fact gave them a
money-value, and consequent protection. In those days
man’s property in man was fairly well attended to,
simply because it paid. No such luck attends the slave
in disguise. He has no market value, because, when the
old nigger is dead, a new one can be captured gratis.
Employers, when in need to fill the gaps created by deser-
tion ordeath, just “run-in ” a supply. Then a J.P., or some
other equally reputable authority, witnesses the “ signing
of the contract,” and is supposed to make sure that the
captured nigger is acting under no compulsion. The
spectacle of two slave-owning J’sP. witnessing each other’s
contracts and making quite sure that each other’s nigger
is a perfectly free agent is most interesting.
It is only the brutality of a certain class of squatter
which makes the apprentice system necessary. The man
who treats his aboriginal servants kindly can get abund-
ance of voluntary black labor at a cheap enough rate.
Civilisation reproaches this State because it out-
Herods all its sisters in its treatment of the aboriginals.
It is the one Slave State of Australasia, and its slave
system is loathsome in its brutality and revolting in its
hypocrisy. The Catholic Missions furnish irrefragable
evidence that, with kind treatment and training, even
the wildest tribes are susceptible to a better life. I have
known such natives perfectly reliable , truthful, ana
honest— and what one Church has accomplished, why not
ot Mav ? God grant that the iniquitous enactment be
forthwith repealed, and natural rights restored to a race
fast ebbing awav, and entitled to more than barren sym-
pathy. Then will the young Commonwealth be rescued
from the indignity of wearing the badge of Slavery .
Then, too, accountable beings will no longer be hunted
down and driven in chains like beasts of prey, to be
scourged, as if malefactors, by brutish Australian slave-
owners in this land of alleged freedom.

Death from Chloroform.
Writes “Medicus ” from Victoria :
Dear Bulletin.—The use of Chloroform Anaesthesia ha
been condemned in your columns. I write in its defence
and enclose a cutting from the British Medical Journal
which, while proving not much, is a plea m its behalf.
Chloroform, if carefully used, is as safe as anj other
anaesthetic and more convenient than any other in its
administration. I have lately had two cases mmj quiet
country practice where I gave chloroform to two old men
over 70, the one being under its-influence for an hour and
three-quarters. Both came out of it with pulses as it
nothing had been done. . .
Single-handed (my medical neighbours being tar
distant) for reducing fractures and dislocations and m
refractory children needing surgical help, I have
no hesitation in using chloroform. During the tour
years that I was in Edinburgh there was not a
single death from chloroform recorded at the Koyai
Infirmary, and they use nothing else there. One
patient got very near it by the carelessness of the anaes-
thetist, a clinical clerk, who, instead of attending to his
work, became interested in the operation. If it had not
been for the prompt action of the operator Dr. Jas.
Bell, the Sherlock Holmes of Conan Doyle—who, on
noticing the darkening of blood, due to want of aeration,
pushed a remarkably long fore-finger, which he fortu-
nately possessed, through the rima glottidis, and stimu-
lated the respiratory nerve-centres, there would have
been a corpse, and chloroform would have been blamed.
(From a practical point of view, writes “ Puyyaka,” the
above brings out no new arguments in favor of chloro-
form. The assertion that it is as safe as any other
anaesthetic “ if carefully used ” is vitiated by the doctrine
of convenience set forth in the next line. No amount ot
superior convenience should be allowed as an argument.
A medico’s duty is to study the safety of his patient with-
out regard to convenience, and, anyhow, the odd few
inches of buggv-space taken up by an ether-inhaler is a
small matter in comparison to a possible coroner’s inquest.
The instances related of safe administration to men ot
advanced years are explainable by the fact that in early
vouth and advanced age (below 5 and over 60) the average
of deaths from chloroform does not exceed 2 per cent, of
all fatal cases. Ages of between 16 and 60 yield 61 per
cent, of all chloroform deaths, and it is here that ether
shows its superior safety. The instance given of a case
at Edinburgh Infirmary further bears out the contention
of the superior safety of ether, since it is almost impos-
sible for a careless administrator to give a dangerous
overdose with a Clover’s inhaler. ’The only cases in
which chloroform appears to be admissible where ether is
obtainable, consist of children of tender years, persons of
advanced age, and pregnant women. In the two former
the circulatory mechanism is not so liable to sudden
breakdown as'at other ages, and in the latter the physio-
logical hypertrophy strengthens the heart against a
possible collapse. The tendency to a possible bronchitis
also appears to be present in all three.]
_ ♦
“ Medi-Cuss ” re chloroform deaths :
Writer had hammered into him the code of ethics in-
stead of the catechism, but he fears that the law of caste
would have to go under were he a juryman at an inquest
where the victim had died under chloroform administered
for the extraction of teeth. There can be no shadow of
excuse for so using the drug, and the doctor who allows
himself to be persuaded into a course of action that he
must know is fraught with danger, ought to get three
years “hard.” “Medico” (B. 9/10/’01) states that the
considerations of less excitement and struggling may
favor the use of chloroform in preference to ether in
private practice, but not in public institutions, “ where
skilled assistants are usually procurable.” Are they ? I
know of a dozen public institutions in N.S.W. where the
surgeon has “ Buckley’s ” show of “skilled assistance,”
and it is my opinion that every country practitioner in
districts where there is but one doctor should be oom-
pelled—by force of public opinion, if not by law
—to possess a nitrous-oxide (laughing-gas) outfit. A
really good outfit, including a 163 ft. cylinder of gas, can
be had for less than £5, and the doctor would have thus
at hand an absolutely safe, pleasant, and cheap means of
producing comfortable anaesthesia in nearly all operations
of a minor nature Especially is laughing-gas the thing
for teeth-extraction. The patient can be placed in any
position—the raising of the patient to a sitting posture
being one of the great dangers in the use of chloroform —
and the operation over, the patient, in the great majority of
cases, after a few moments walks out of the room unaided.
Each patient will take, on an average, say, eight gallons of
gas, which means 12 sittings to the cylinder. When the
cylinder is exhausted it can be returned and refilled while
a fresh one is forwarded by parcel-post— another great
point in favor of this agent.
For the extraction of teeth, therefore, the insertion of
sutures, the probing for and removal of foreign bodies,
the opening up of painful abscesses, the removal of in-
growing toe-nails (a momentary but exquisitely painful
proceeding) and many other minor operations, every
country doctor should have a laughing-gas outfit—
especially as the apparatus can be easily carried from
house to house.
Public opinion will yet force the powers that be to sup-
ply it to every back-country hospital, when, in addition
to the saving of an immense amount of totally needless
suffering, there will be a marked decrease in the number
of deaths on the operating-table.

“No compulsion, but you must.” Circular issued
by authority of N.S.W. Education-Minister
Perry:—
Department of Public Instruction,
Sydney, 25th February, 1901.
MEMORANDUM TO TEACHER.
The Minister of Public Instruction has expressed his
approval of the movement initiated for hoisting the
“Union Jack” on all Schools in the Federal States, on
the occasion of the opening of the first Commonwealth
Parliament by H.R.H. the Duke of Cornwall and York,
accompanied by the Duchess of Cornwall and York.
2. It is desired that all requisite action in this matter
should be taken by the teachers and parents concerned.
The cost will be inconsiderable, and probably the forma-
tion of local Committees to obtain subscriptions and
make arrangements for the provision of flags and flag-
poles would afford the best means of carrying out the
desired object. Donations of the latter articles may per-
haps be invited from private citizens. It is to be under-
stood that everything must be done without cost to this
Department.
3. If it be intended to take action in your district in
the direction indicated, no time should be lost, in order to
have the flags and poles in position when the opening
ceremony takes place, on or about the 9th May next.
If practicable, it is proposed that H.R.H. the Duchess of
Cornwall and York should give a signal simultaneously
over all the telegraph wires the time for hoisting the
flags.
J. C. Maynard, Under Secretary.
If it be intended! The Lord may have mercy on
the miserable teacher who doesn’t “intend.”
And “everything must be done without cost to
the department ’’—and for the greater glory of the
Minister.

“ Royal Engineer ” to The Bulletin
The great “ expert ” question cropped-up again at the
Sydenham (Sydney) railway accident inquest. Loco.
Superintendent Tiiow said the accident was due to a com-
bination of ill-balanced engine and uneven line, whereas
the coroner’s amici curice, Engineering Prof. Warren
and Mr. Norman Selpe, said that unevenness of the line
had nothing to do with the accident, and that a fin. de-
pression on the rails would not tend to jerk even an ill-
balanced engine off the line. Mr. Tnow said it would, if
the engine were going fast —and he seems to have sound
sci'ence on his side. The same physical laws regulate the
rolling and pitching of a ship and the oscillation and
pitching of a locomotive. Both move round their centres
of gravity with a motion like that of an inverted
pendulum. If the centre of gravity, as in the
Sydenham engine, is “behind the centre of figure,
the front wheels will, if they get a sufficient im-
pulse, tend to rise above rail level, and then a very
slight impulse to one side, such as may b e pven by
a verv small inequality in the rail, will jerk the whole
mass off the line. No one but a very experienced railway
man—who is not only well up in the theory of mathema-
tical physics, but possesses extensive practical experience
and has also the rare faculty of combining theory and
practice into an effective concrete system-can rightly
estimate the value of each small circumstance. Prof.
Warren and Mr. Sklfe saw all that, Mr. Tiiow saw the
faulty engine, and the slight inequalities in the permanent
way; - but they postulated either a perfect engine for the
faulty road, or a perfect road for the fauity engine.
Something more than mere mathematical and mechanical
knowledge is needed to make a real engineer. . i e
must have a certain kind of brain, to enable him to matte
adequate combinational use of his acquired theory ana
personally-observed facts. University degrees prove
nothing. They are merely labels —the real value of whi .1
depends on the sort oi brain owned by the mail who Dears
one
An aggregation calling itself the North Australian
League met the other day in Melbourne, and de-
cided to urge the Federal Govt, to promptly finish
the transcontinental railway to Port Darwin, to
secure the assistance of Russia (Russia !) 111 estab-
lishing a line of steamers between Port Darwin
and Port Arthur, to agitate for telegraphic com-
munication between Darwin and Wyndham, and
finally to endeavor to get that portion of W .A.
ahd S.A. north of the 23th parallel constituted a
separate State. This wonderful league, which
appears to be mostly J. S. Butters, J. Brau-
shaw, P. B. Durack, M -ieas Gunn, and Go.,
gave its case away hopelessly by stating that the
development of the N.T. would make a White
Australia impracticable until an immense amount
of capital had been expended in pioneering
work.” Let’s see. “Pioneering work” mostly
means using and abusing black and yellow labor,
land-grabbing, concession-syndicating, and so
forth, doesn’t it ? And these lovely Leaguers
want the N.T. and the North of W.A. erected
into a State and practically placed under their
control. Sweet simplicity !

“Shellback” to The Bulletin
The Federal, about whose doom, with that of all her
jerew, there now can be little doubt, is described by the
press as a comparatively new boat, strong, well-found
and welPmanned. The same papers, in commenting on
the finding of hatches washed ashore, stated that in
accordance with usual collier routine these were duly
secured before leaving port. Perhaps so. But whose
business is it to see that any vessel, employed in any trade,
coasting or deep sea, is in fit and proper condition for a
battle with the elements that may be waiting her
immediately her nose smells the green water ? The
answer is—Nobody’s. Marine Board surveyors don t
go poking round the coal and other wharves at
all hours of the night, when steamers usually
manage to finish up, and personally see that they
are not overloaded, and are in other ways ready for sea.
Why, not more than six months ago a couple of colliers
belonging to a big firm arrived at an Australian port with
the centre of their load-line discs submerged, and this
after a four-days’ passage plus consumption of at least 40
tons, and the decision of the local Marine Board was to
theeffect that no action could be taken because the vessels
loaded in another State ! Also, present writer has heard
it stated that not long ago the coal-trimmers on the ‘ 60-
milers” —Newcastle to Sydney—used generally to finish
up work somewhere in the vicinity of Barranjoey,
just outside Sydney. Another point—why cannot
actual and verified crew-lists, signed by some re-
sponsible person, say master or purser, be always
available at owners’ or agents’ offices ? The prac-
tice in London is for the skipper to make out his list
whilst proceeding down the river, and this is handed
to the pilot previous to his going ashore at Dover, he in
turn handing it over on arrival to the higher authorities.
Also, the Victorian Ports and Harbors Dept, don’t show up
very well in their refusal to send the Lady Loch to investi-
gate re the Federal when urgent telegrams were being re-
ceived from Gabo. The Lady Loch’s crew, from their close
acquaintance with almost every foot of the coast from
Gabo westwards, would have been just the people to send
on an errand of mercy, which was only tardily attended-to
by the despatch of 'the Tagliaferro to the scene of sup-
posed disaster. The Lady Loch’s “ regular programme ”
is, apparently, such an important affair that the whole
business of the department would stand still were it
interfered with in the smallest degree.

The Jingo newspapers which strive to excuse the
devastation of the Transvaal by referring to the
doings of Sherman and of the Prussians in 1870,
always forget to add that the English papers of
that time were loud in their protests against what
they called the “ barbarities ” of the American
and German generals, and that the harsh measures
of “ the brutal Prussians” did much to enlist Eng-
lish sympathy with the French. A favorite
example with the Jingo newspaper is the order
given in 1870 that French hostages were to be
placed on the trains in districts where it had been
attempted to damage the lines. On this Hall re-
marks in his “International Law” (p. 475):
“The order was universally and justly repro-
bated.” Should the war go on much longer, we
shall doubtless have the devastation of the Palati-
nate pointed out as the model the troops in
S’Africa should follow, “if it were not for the
misplaced magnanimity of our commanders.”
+
With reference to Boer General Botha’s recent
mention of “his Government,” London Times
sneeringly asks “What fs his Government?” Well,
the fact that—after repeated proclamations to
the Boers had failed to secure the submission of
any considerable number of them—substantially
the same proposals were made to Botha, was
a recognition of his position as head of the Boer
State. Those who know the real history of the
American War of Independence (not the
Washington and Lafayette legends, but as
written in the memoirs and correspondence of
men like Jefferson and Governor Hutchinson)
.can find many parallels to the events of the Boer
war. Even as late as 1782, the 7th year of the
Amer can struggle, numbers of American patriots
despaired, and in 1779, the Frenchman De
Portain, one of Washington’s brigadier-generals,
wrote that, if England could keep 30,000
men in America, she must win—and 30,000 men
would be but a drop in the S’African bucket !

“Ex-R.E.” writes re Broderick’s new British
army scheme: —
Can’t see how the thing is going to work. Six army-
corps to begin with, and three of these to be kept con-
stantly ready for foreign service. Why, 20 years ago they
only professed to have two army corps, and only one was
supposed to be ready, hut when they wanted to send that
one to Egypt it wasn’t to be found, and they had to
throw together a lot of fragments of regiments to take its
place. Also, the army that went to S'Africa was a thing
of shreds and patches, and would have, made an awful
exhibition of itself opposed to a regularly - organised
European army. The men were brave enough;
but they could neither march nor manoeuvre, and
they had no leaders who could handle them. Who
can say that Broderick’s six army-corps will
he any better than the two of 20 years ago ?
The War Minister proposes, but the recruit disposes, and
if men won’t enlist there are no soldiers. Even in time
of peace recruits are scarce, and all that can be got are
wanted to make up the drafts for foreign stations, re-
ducing regiments at home to skeletons. So where is
Broderick going to get his “ 155,000 additional regulars,”
his “ eight regiments for garrison purposes ” in the Medi-
terranean, and the 5000 or so extra marines the Admiralty
will want to guard coaling stations ? The competition of
the recruiters for the marines with those for the regulars
is sufficiently severe already, and the former get the pick

strength. Where is the new large army to come from ?
