“Why Smith Left Home,” at Sydney
Palace, with its last act labelled “The Count
makes a mistake, and why Smith leaves home,”
calls up a melancholy picture of a distracted hus-
band flying from his disgraced wife and her per-
fidious deceiver. But it turns out that Smith
fled suddenly simply because all his friends and
his wife’s relatives and acquaintances persisted in
turning his home into a hostelry where they gob
accommodation on the cheap. The lydy who does
the cooking, an up-to-date person well-versed in
the economic side of the servant-girl problem,
occasionally paralyses domestic life with a wave
of her soup-ladle, and helps to induce Smith to
flee by providing him with a cake to which it was
necessary to use a hammer. Ere vanishing,
Smith tries to persuade the cook to turn
the deadly art of cake-making to good purpose
by using it to clear his wife’s relatives
off the premises. But the compact, and the cake,
and everything else fade out of the comedy more
or less till Smith himself also melts out of sight.
The dialogue scintillates like the cinematograph ;
and, unlike most plays of this sort, which generally
contain one large warning to married men—usu-
ally not to go out o’ nights—this one contains
several warnings to married men not to do a great
many things.
♦ * * *
Charles Arnold, as Count Yon Guggen-
heim, the foreigner with the twisted English,
makes the third act something that the most sated
plav-goer would want to see all over again, and
perhaps once or twice again after that. It is not
often the stage Outlander knows how to
break his English in the right place. Arnold’s
is scientifically correct. He should remember,
however, that when a German kisses a man
friend, which he does on small provocation, he
salutes both cheeks as a symbol that his friendship
is the real Mackay. A Berlin rail way-station
resounds with the crack of farewell salutes like a
S’African donga with the bark of a pom-pom.
Elderly Miss Smith, who wants to get married, is
an old idea well worked out; and Miss Sally
Booth, as the lydy who does the cooking, and upon
the slightest pretence threatens to go on strike
and call out the milkmen and other trades amal-
gamated with the Servants’ Union, is distinctly up-
to-date. There are also some fine touches about
the slow, ice-cold tones of the housemaid who is
kissed in mistake for her mistress. The humilia-
tion of being kissed in mistake for her mistress
and not “on her own” is a thing that only good
red gold can wipe out.
** * *
On Wednesday, April 10th, the ever-
welcome Mdlle. Dolores (hitherto known as
Trebelli) will return to Sydney Centennial Hall
and delight the hearts of all lovers of good music.
It is 16 months since Sydney heard the great
singer and that fact will no doubt whet the en-
joyment. Since then she has been through
Canada and the U.S., and spent a few months at
Paris Exhibition, where she secured some songs
of the far-away twelfth century and the dead-and-
gone Crusaders. Most of them are troubadour
ditties of once-famous knights, and are spoken of
by Mdlle. as “ very quaint, very simple in form,
and of wonderful harmony.” These, or some of
them, will be sung to the Sydney audiences. The
Cornstalks will surely have something to ruminate
about when that musical resurrection takes place
in Sydney Centennial Hall after about 700 years,
and the man in a dress-suit leans back in
his comfortable chair and listens to the
notes and words to which some crusader
humped his weary swag of armour over the
burning sands of Syria after the mobile Paynim.
The modern man will, no doubt, appreciate the
music of the Crusader ; but what sort of an effect
it would have had on that steel-clad gentleman if
he had had the good luck to. hear Mdlle. Dolores
sing “Home, Sweet Home,” or the “Laughing
Song,” is an interesting musical problem. At
present, negotiations for Mdlle. to sing in connec-
tion with the arrival of the Yorks are in progress,
but barring some such arrangement the melodious
lady will go to Adelaide from Sydney, and thence
to Melbourne. The Centennial Hall box-plan
will be opened on the morning of Wednesday,
April 10.
** * *
The only comparatively recent arrivals at
Sydney Tivoli are the Sisters Pine, two nice little
girls, who warble in character about the advan-
tages of following “the lady guide.” They have
a pleasant way about them, and as the two little
Quakeresses are distinctly charming. Unfortu-
nately, their voices do not impress this paper as
being up to their other attractions. That Lizzie
Kirk is in her last week is regrettable. Mr.
1) Albert has broken out in a fresh line. lie now
sings a song about sundry gory doings in S’Africa,
including the “spiking” of Long Tom on Bul-
wana Hill. And, while he sings, pictures of the
doings, presumably taken on the spot, flit across
the screen. The fact that they don’t spike modern
guns, but steal their breech-blocks instead,
doesn’t concern the song. Next week Marie
Lloyd and Alec Hurley, late of London, will
make their first appearance at the matinee.
** * #
If the book and the songs of Musgrove’s
panto, were only on a par with the gorgeous loveli-
ness of the setting and dressing, What a panto, it
would be ! ’Twould stand as the great time-mark
from which writers and actors would count backward
and forward across the eternity of Xmas shows.
But book and songs are not so, and, with all its
beauty, “Cinderella” is a moulting bird ina golden
cage—a priceless casket containing paste jewels. It
is now ‘‘ up to ” the great George to break away
from the silly old custom by which panto, writers
have been restricted to a dozen or so weary old
fairy-tales that have long ago had all the fragrance
squeezed out of them, and get a real up-to-date
panto, in which the characters shall indulge in the
sparkling dialogue of “ The Tyranny of Tears ”
and “Lady Windermere’s Fan.” There might
not be more money in it than in the present show—
the spectacular effects alone are good value for the
public’s money—but there would be whole stacks
of fame ; and, according to popular report, Mus-
grove is now rich enough to indulge in the luxury
of Fame. Meanwhile, apart from the setting, the
flying feet of the Dartos are the brightest spots
in the show.
** * *
The Villain with the ballet-girl walk anti
the slab of hair over his forehead is still being in-
formed, at Sydney Lyceum, that he who would kill
a woman’s soul would not hesitate to murder her
body. This sounds impressive, but is not true —
for reasons temporal and connected with the
gallows. Also, when a body is killed it can be
certified to; but the alleged homicide of a soul
has to be taken on the word of its owner,
who is generally soulful after she has lost,
according to her own account, that which makes
her so. The fight between the good little drum-
mer-boy and the bad little ditto still continues,
but when one very round drummer-boy tells
another rather feminine boy that he is as good a
man as he (the other round boy) is, the audience
smiles. The bad ’un is a convincing little
larrikin, but his larrikinism is more like an
inheritance from a costermonger and a washer-
woman than from an officer and a lady
whose manner of being unsober is strictly high-
class and grammatical. Bert. Bailey is especially
convincing when he flies out at the wicked officer;
and the servant-girl who has two men waiting 12
years or so to marry her, while the Vicar’s
daughter has only one man, who is anxious not to
marry her, is a striking exemplification of the
advantage of being born in a lowly condition and
taught to cook well.
** * *
At Sydney Criterion Alfred Dampier filled
in the first part of the week with the “Black
Flag ’’—the old, old story of the two brothers —one
of whom went to London to study theology, and
only got as far as the chapter on the Seven Scarlet
Sins, and stuck there evermore ; while the other
brother went about in moles, and mended fisher-
men’s boats and did odd jobs in the charity line
till his irate and money-grubbing old step-father
heaved him out and decided to leave all the cash
to the son of the Seven Sins. The unfortunate
impatience of the vermilion-souled son caused the
old gentleman to get a nasty crack about the
head, and Alfred Dampier Glyndon, the chari table
vagabond, got into Portland Gaol for the theolo-
gian’s crime. Eventually he got out of Portland
at great risk, to life and limb just at the precise
moment his innocence was being proved up to the
hilt. Things always happen that way in melo-
drama. Next week Alfred will revive “ Robbery
Under Arms,” and then he departs from Sydney
for awhile.
** * *
Wirth’s Circus got ashore at Sydney about
7 a.m. on Saturday morning, and by 8 p.m. was
in full swing to a crowded house. A performing
bear and his lady bear gave a sketch that is
a beautiful caricature of humanity going along
King-street to supper. John W. Cooke and
Carl Wirth figured in a clever, gentle-
manly turn as Mashers on Horseback, and won
wild applause. Then the fine-looking Miss
Marizles flew round the ring standing upright on
a white horse ; and juggled with balls, knives,
plates, and fire in great style. The cleverest item
in the show, perhaps, is the hand-walking of
Fred. El Zobedie, who, with his feet pointed to-
wards the zenith or hanging down round his
shoulders, sprang from bar to bar with his
hands. He walked upstairs on his palms and
slid down the handrails, and finally sang
and danced “ Ta-ra-ra-boom-de-ay ” in a re-
versed position. Horizontal - bar exercise,
horses waltzing to music, a leaping grey-
hound, and a grey stallion that seemed to have a
perfect knowledge of colors and picked out red
and white handkerchiefs according to various in-
structions, filled in the time till two tiny ponies
came out with gloves on their forefeet and boxed.
When time was called the little equine pugs
s f a RS' ere d back to their chairs and were sprayed
and fanned in the most approved modern style.
The tiger-trainer went into the cage and used his
whip with great vehemence. The old man tiger
was in a great rage; and the people waited
breathlessly to see him pat the trainer across the
jugular. But he didn’t; and the audience had a
first-cla,ss set of thrills, but no blood. All the
same, it’s only a Question of Time. The wit of
the clowns is the weak point of the show.
Napoleon was routed for the last time
at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, last Saturday ; and the
big theatre closed for a week. To-night (Satur-
day) the story of Gyrus Gilfain’s strange scented
island and the doings of Widow Palotta and Prof.
Lauri will be warbled —in other words, the Opera
co. opens in “Plorodora”; and as this musical
comedy ran for over 100 nights in Melbourne, and
seats for the last nights were booked nearly a
week in advance, there is evidently something
startlingly original or delightfully sinful about it.
Originality and pleasant sinfulness always fill the
house; and when they are combined the manage-
ment has to turn money away by the bushel.
*# * *
John Fuller’scompany, with green and blue
sashes round their Arcadian waists, still sit round
the Cornfields in the Empire Theatre and carol
songs both old and new. The good old Gallants
of England who are going to fight for the King
come in very appropriately at the present epoch ;
while John Fuller, with Amy Blackie, gives a
pretty rendering of the “ Sailor Sighs.” Miss
May Marlow, with plenty of spirit and go
about her, still possesses the same sort of
voice and uses it in the same sort of way.
The farce on the difficulties of an insurance-
agent, wasn’t quite up to some that have been
staged by Fuller ; but that is saying no great
harm about it. The feature of .the evening, of
course, was the appearance of Mexican Jack, got
up to kill, with beads that tumbled off his neck
in his agonised efforts to shoot backwards while
half upside-down. Mexican Jack, who apologised
for not having a Californian accent, affirming,
nevertheless, that so-called sharpshooters furnished
with this were generally frauds, managed by
means of two rifles to shoot a ball off his own
head, besides shooting backwards by means of a
mirror, and doing various other things ; and at the
conclusion of every trick the orchestra said
“ Bom ! ”
** * *
The most remarkable Sydney show last
week was decidedly “ Billie Barlow’s ” perform-
ance in the Supreme Court theatre—for two days
only. Miss Sweet-William is a song-and-
dance “artistq” of considerable notoriety in
London and Australian music-halls ; and her per-
formance consisted in suing The Bulletin for
£6OOO damages for “libel” because this paper
on Jan. 12th last, after seeing her romping
about the stage of the Tivoli by-and-large
and front-and-rear, made some remarks about her
“costume” which, when you come to read them
calmly, seemed likely to bring a cloud of eager
spectators. “Miss Barlow’s” legal name, it ap-
peared, was Mrs. Edward Menzies-Stuart ; and
the jurymen had the unusual pleasure of witness-
ing a special “turn” at the Tivoli so that Miss
Barlow could exhibit the “costume” complained of.
There appeared to be considerably more “costume”
than is worn by the Hottentot Venus—and this
time Miss Barlow did not turn round. Next day
Mrs. Stuart, with still more costume, appeared as
a witness to tell how a reference to her naked
soul (blush !) had lacerated her feelings. Cross-
examined by George Reid, K.C., she ad-
mitted singing songs which counsel suggested were
over-frisky; but alleged that her “profession”
didn’t particularly consider “the virtue” of
songs. The Bulletin’s counsel began so nicely
that Miss Barlow apparently thought he was on her
side, and threw him sweet, confiding smiles. Later
on, when the drift of the guileful George Reid’s
remarks became evident to the meanest music-hall
understanding, the witness scowled as if her cross-
examiner was “quite horrid.” The husband,
Edward Menzies-Stuart, told proudly how he
came to Australia with the Duke of Edinburgh,
and said he “managed” for his wife; but what
he was put in the box for, except to scin-
tillate in the now-faded glory of the Edin-
burgh visit, did not clearly appear. Pilcher,
K.C., who appeared for plaintiff, snowed such an
intimate knowledge of trunks, tights, and other
details of theatrical costume that Reid, K.C.,
expressed the opinion that he must follow these
troupes round. “I don’t follow you round, any-
how !” retorted Pilcher. “No,” chuckled Reid,
“ I don’t wear tights.” The jury, after an hour’s
consideration of the case, gave a verdict for The
Bulletin. Miss Barlow having given notice of
appeal, no more can be said about the matter at
present.
** * #
“Tannhauser,” as performed at Melb.
Princess’s two nights last week, comes rather
hard on anyone who wasn’t made in Germany.
The more manageable “Lohengrin” is more
easily admired, because the music has a little
sugar in it, and the action tells the story in an
interesting way, and the background is impressive;
while the opera is so symmetrical that its produc-
tion on a comparatively small scale seems big
enough in all respects. The “Lohengrin” that
Musgrove supplies is never absolutely wearisome
outside of the second act, when the Queen of
Spades and her degraded Frederick hang around
a doorstep in the cold grey morning and sing the
present writer to sleep for about 15 minutes. A
larger orchestra and a finer pair of voices would
doubtless make even that bit of “Lohengrin”
worth listening to.
• * * *
“Tannhauser” has more than one cold, grey
period when a man and a woman, or two men,
sing to one another concerning matters which
Wagner can shed no new light upon. There is
naught save the music and the voices to interest
one at these times. The music is heavy with the
sighs of German philosophy, and the singers’ in-
tonement of the Wagnerian theory is nothing out
of the common. “Tannhauser” (but this a blas-
phemous truth) lives mainly by the success of its
second act —the gaudy crowd, the .noon)nr tnnwfi.
and the incidental spectacular effects. Yet the
“Tournament of Song” is very heavy. Wagnerites,
whose religion runs to bigotry, and quidnuncs in
general, pretend to a keen delight in that Song
Fight as it is fought at the Princess’s. They even
declare that the unexciting “love” music in
the opening scene is voluptuous and passionate.
According to the Cult everything that Wagner
wrote is unimpeachably true to its purpose, for
the mere reason that Wagner’s intentions were
honest. “ Tannhauser,” as some other people
hear it, is a pedantic composition of the highest
moral character. It appeals to them with the
force of a respectable Sunday sermon, no more
and no less. Performed on a larger scale it would
give them a larger measure of mild satisfaction.
Present scribe reckons that the opera is an ac-
quired taste, and he hopes it will never develops
into a violent craving.
* * * ♦
Miss Crichton sings conscientiously the
part of Venus, a lady who reclines upon a
hard ambrosial couch in the blissful Realms of
Space. Miss Crichton’s method is clearly not
voluptuous, whatever the music may be in German
estimation, nor is her voice soft and round. She
is a rather angular Venus, with bones in her
warble. Max Eugene, as Wolfram, doesn’t sug-
gest the spirit of Platonic Love and Pagan
Endeavour. Madame Slapoffski is an efficient
Elisabeth ; looks well, acts well, sings well, but
lacks beauty in her voice ; and the same may be
said of Louis Arens. Arens, who is returning to
Europe, has filled his two Wagner parts very
capably. If the new tenor is famous, as the
advt. states, he will give a prettier complexion
to the music that he takes over from the depart-
ing “Lohengrin.”
••* * *
“ Mignon ” was repeated three times last
week, likewise on Monday, and it is anxiously
expected to appear again. Versatile Madame
Slapoffski has improved her picture of the little
egg-dancer since the first night—greatly improved
it. Her acting in various scenes and sundry cos-
tumes, including a knickerbocker suit and a
nightdress, is very natural to theyoung-girl char-
acter. Seeing that the brilliant French opera was
wanted, and an ideal Mignon is not obtainable, a
clever artist who can get near to the ideal in this
casual Slapoff style deserves a vote of thanks.
And she gets it. Her light comedy scene is rap-
turously received. But why turn a searchlight on
the kneeling figure of Madame in the hist act ?
Miss Lorraine, the frequently indisposed member
of the co., was missing from the cast on Saturday,
when Miss Crichton, with a little assistance from
the prompter, played the giddy Filina in an airy
manner.

Barron Barthold, the new tenor imported
by George Musgrove, was to make his first appear-
ance at the Princess’s on Wednesday as the Yes-
No hero of “Tannhauser.”
*# # #
The record reign of “ Florodora ” ended
at Her Majesty’s last (Wednesday) night with the
106th performance, unless Melbourne took out an
injunction to restrain the company’s departure.
“Florodora” was something more than a
phenomenal success for J. C. Williamson. It was
a matrimonial agency for the dear girls who
made it bright and beautiful. The Bulle-
tin can’t say exactly how many of them
got married or engaged to smitten capitalists
during the long run of luck, but their number was
so great that the management has been rushed
with applications for chorus-billets from ardent
daughters of Toorak, and the idea of cele-
brating “Florodora” weddings on the stage
will be seriously considered when the show
gets going in Sydney. Tweedlepunch could
start a new Denomination and qualify himself
as a marriage-pastor, and the ceremony might
be introduced into the second act with Salvation
Harmy effects, all the guests and dependants of
Cyrus Gilfain bringing tambourines and singing
“ What joy to be there ! ” But this by the way.
Nance O’Neil is announced to reappear at Her
Majesty’s on Saturday, and the admirers of that
commanding lady are booking seats for “La
Tosca.”
** * *
Bland Holt has arranged to celebrate the
return of Easter, at Melb. Royal, with a mighty
production of “The World,” for the first time in
12 years. The intention of Rickards, at the Bijou,
is to add Austin Rudd and Lottie Collins to his
programme, in honor of the moveable Bun Feast.
** * *
At the Athenaeum Hall the best of the
biographs, which has now settled down to the
title of Genuine British Biograph, will reappear
after a long-lamented absence on Easter Saturday.
Manager Wyld has just come back from England
with new pictures ; and if these are as clear and
flickerless (or almost flickerless) as the pictures of
the first G. 8.8. season, a repetition of the old
boomsome business may be looked for.
*# * *
Good Friday concerts are advertised from
three influential quarters. George Musgrove’s
Opera Co. will give “ Stabat Mater” and extras
at the Princess’s ; the Philharmonic Society has
prepared Gounod’s “Redemption” on a broad
vocal scale ; and a Sacred Festival is to happen at
the Bijou, under the direction of the Napoleonic
Boffard.
John Black, who built Melbourne Theatre Royal, put
into it a fortune he made as a carrier to the Goldfields.
“ Lost Chord ” Sullivan left £54,529, the bulk of which
goes to his nephew.

J Acetylene Gas Patents.
The infringement of patent case, brought by the
Acetylene Gas Co., of Australasia, Ltd., against Mr.
Henry Markwald, a Melbourne merchant, was advanced
a stage by Mr. Justice Hood, of the Victorian Bench
on February sth. Plantiffs claim to be the patentees of
a process for producing acetylene gas, and allege that
defendant has infringed their rights by selling calcium
carbide manufactured according to their invention.
Defendant denies any infringement, and asserts that the
alleged invention is not new, having regard to the
common and public knowledge at the time the letters
patent were issued. On the date mentioned plaintiffs
applied for a commission to examine various expert
witnesses in New South Wales, England and Canada.
His Honor met their wishes, remarking that he believed
the application to be bona fide, and though its grant
might occasion delay, its refusal might amount to a
denial of justice. On the same day, and by the same
-company, application was made to restrain James Ross,
master of the steamer “Melbourne,” from landing any
carbide of calcium manufactured according to their
patent. His Honor granted the order, with costs.*

* * * *
Most readers of The Bulletin will have heard
that Luhrig Vanners have proved a phenomenal success
on Broken Hill sulphides as also on low grade gold and
copper ores. All Barrier Mines remodelling their mills on
up-to-date lines include a number of them. The South
and Junction companies each have about a dozen, no
slime treatment plant being now considered complete
without these excellent machines. In Queensland tin
concentration is the latest accomplishment, the Irvine-
bank Mill having introduced Luhrig Vanners with success-
ful results, the example having been followed by the well-
known Ooolgarra Tin Mining Co., who are installing a
number of Luhrig Vanners in connection with their new
Tin Dressing Mill. Messrs. W. &J. Lempriere, Melbourne,
are the general agents for the Luhrig Appliances.*
* * * *
Norman Godkin, of 31 Queen-street, Melbourne,
is prepared to purchase or develope, with option of
purchase, “ big prospecting shows ” (either gold or
mineral) in any part of Australia. Properties offered
must be subject to inspection. Cable address: “Option,”
Melbourne.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Footscray
works, and he is nowin a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, andall sorts of iron-work.*

“ Wireless electric lighting,” writes The Bul-
letin’s London correspondent, “is to be the first ‘boss’
invention to flare-out upon the new century. If all that
is claimed for it be true, copper speculation will receive
another very nasty set-back. Up to now the disclosed
details are not over-definite, though an official and tech-
nical pronouncement is expected daily. Roughly, it may,
however, be said that in the cellar—as with the gas-meter
is placed a powerful induction coil of particular con-
struction, which, by the aid of a battery, generates and
disperses throughout the house, through wood and
brick, electrical waves of a new sort. Empty glass
bulbs and tubes, from which the air has been
exhausted, lie loose in rooms, &c., and under the
influence of the waves fill with flame that gives
a beautiful glow-light. If you are chasing a spider in the
ceiling-corner you can throw up a ‘ bulb ’ to direct the
broom-handle’s prods. Tesla has got something near
this, but a celebrated English scientist claims success.”

The Bulletin
The S.M. Herald recently held its annual
curse at the sound and prosperous Maoriland Government
Life Assurance Department. The S.M. Herald loathes
State enterprise with a great loathing, and once a year it
rises up to compare the M.L. State Department, not with
privately-managed institutions of similar size and agc»
every one of which it can beat badly, but with the A.M.P.
Society only. The Herald’s latest effort is given here-
under : „ .
S.M. Herald curse (26th March, 1901).
M.L. Govt.
Insurance A.M.P.
Income— Hept.
New Premiums ». ». £23,925 ..
Assuring .. .. .. 748,428 .. 614,325
Total premiums .. .. 277,355 .. 223,972
Assuring .. .. •- 9,558,166 .. 7,140,042
Interest on Funds : Amount 133,465 .. I*V®

“ The Government Department,” the Herald goes on,
“is badly beaten in every line by the Australian
Mutual Provident Society except in those relating to
the amount of assurance. Certain influences still
enable it to obtain a larger amount of new busi-
ness, £23,925 being the amount of its new premium
revenue, as against £20,100, the amount of the private
office. It also has a total premium revenue of £277,355,
against £223,972 in the A.M.P. Society. Still further, in
proportion to its income, it assures a larger amount, as its
premiums are rather lower. Still, looking at the income,
we find that the A.M.P. earned i per cent, more interest
on its funds, and there does not appear to have been any
necessity to write off more than £737 for depreciation of
securities, while the Government Insurance Department
has written off £14,400, which would have to go practi-
callv in further reduction of interest. If the Government
had earned the same rate of interest as the private office,
it would have had £9973 more to lay by, and it has
deemed it necessary to write off £13,663 more than I® re-
quired for a like purpose by the directors of the A.M.P.
from their investments.”
The Herald ought to be heartily ashamed of
this mean little fake. It cooks the revenue account in
the first place by omitting to mention that the M.L.
State office had an income of £10,746 from consideration
for annuities, against the A.M. P.’s £46loonly. This, being
an item where the State department is far ahead, care-
fully left out. It omits to mention that the A.M.P. s pile
of £3 082 807 invested in Maoriland was not nearly all
made there, as the pile of the State office was, and is no
evidence of the solidity of the M.L. business , £43,150
was sent to Maoriland in 1899 from other branches of the
AMP for investment; over £263,000 was similarly sent
there in 1898 ; about £44,000 in 1897 ; £46,000 in 1896 ;
and so on. Similarly, the fact that the A3I.P. earns
more interest in Maoriland than the State office doesn t
show that it is beating the State office on its own ground ;
it only shows that the interest on hundreds of thou-
sands of pounds of the A.M.P.’s Australian funds
appears among the Maoriland receipts. The alleged
total funds are wrongly stated in both cases -; accord-
ing to the return from which the Herald quotes the
real figures are :—M.L. State Office, £3,0/1,581 ; A.M.P.,
£3,102,628. This makes the A.M.P. £31,000 ahead
instead of £85,000, and considering that it was doing
business in Maoriland for eight years before the State
Office was founded, and further considering how its
funds are swelled by hug*e remittances from Australia,
this is rather a poor showing. The fact that the State
Office in 1899 wrote off £14,400 of losses, while the private
one only wrote off £737. is partly counterbalanced by the
fact that in the preceding year the positions were re-
versed. And the difference in expense rates is mostly
owing to the fact that the head office expenses of the
State Office appear in the Maoriland accounts, while
those of the A.M.P. appear in the Australian accounts
—though the Maoriland policy-holder has to pay his share
of them just the same.
But the overwhelming discrepancy is this:
There are nine large life offices doing business in Aus-
tralasia, and the Maoriland State Office is the most
cheaply managed of them all save one. And every year,
regularly, the S.M. Herald rises to declare that the fact
of one big private office —the oldest Australian office
being more cheaply managed than the State office
proves the principle of State assurance to be rotten.
Yet the Herald has never been known to say a
word against any one of the seven large private
offices which are managed far more expensively than
the State office. The worst of them all has never evoked
a comment or a complaint. It has never even warned
the public, so far as The Bulletin can remember, against
cronk and dilapidated little concerns whose expenses are
50 or 60 or 70 per cent, of their earnings, though its
warnings against the absolutely reliable State office whose
expenses are only 20 per cent, are something tremendous.
The security of the Maoriland State Life Department,
with the guarantee of the whole nation behind it, is
worth that of all the Australian and English and Ameri-
can offices doing business here a dozen times oyer. The
only possible objection is its expenses, and they are lower
than those of the Mutual Life Association, City
Mutual, National Mutual, Australian Widows’ Fund,
Colonial Mutual, Australasian Temperance and Genbral,
Equitable, New York Life, Mutual of New York—m fact
that any office bar one. And while the Herald de-
nounces the Maoriland State Life office for spending 20
per cent, of its premiums in expenses it never even draws
attention to the fact that the above institutions spend
from 23 to over 40 per cent. The Herald can’t profess
that it is toiling unselfishly in the public interest when it
persistently denounces a single institution the °
Australasia, and the cheapest save one—and neve r exposes
the notoriously shaky concerns (Tub Bulletin d
refer to those mentioned above) whose balance-sheets
wild joke and their obviously bogus assets a . mo< jery.
Obviously it is the principle of State enterprise whic
rouses this usually mild and lady-like paper to such a y
of anger and muddle ; but it isn’t used to b? 1 . n B’, vl £ or ’
and its struggles to make up a case are pitiably uncon-
vincing. >
“Nonplussed” : We are perfectly aware of the
fact. That beautiful assurance office has no assets in the
State you mention barring a few stamps and a little petty
cash.
* * * *
John Norton’s Truth states with a loud cry of
surprise that the People’s Prudential Assurance Co. of
Svdnev (a small proprietary concern with a paid-up capi-
tal of £4llO, of which £6lO is cash and £3500 is repre-
sented by the alleged goodwill of the co.’s own business)
has been approved by the N.S.W. Public Service Board as
an office for civil-servants, to insure their lives in. J-UE
Bulletin doesn’t see why John should be_ so surprised.
So far as it knows, the N.S.W. Public Service Board has
formally approved in its sleep of every office in this coun-
try, from the A.M.P., with its sixteen or seventeen
millions, downward, as a perfectly safe place .for civil-
servants to insure in. A fine, useful, discriminating body
is the N.S.W. Public Service Board.

The Squatting Investment 00. has a paid-up capital of
£292,005, all buried out in Western Queensland where the
dead men lie. Its endless perspectives of freehold dust
or herbage are valued in the balance-sheet at £277,771,
and on them are £51,550 worth of living animals and the
skeletons of many years’ unpaid dividends. And the co.
still hopes that something will turn up.

“Adelaide”: Under Federation, should a, £1
note, issued in Melbourne by a bank that does business
here also, be worth only 19s 6d in Adelaide ? A £lOO
cheque, received from Tasmania t’other day, was worth
only £99 5s when it got here. This J per cent, is per-
haps justifiable (though the old argument, about its
costing more than that to personally carry your sack of
sovereigns from Hobart to Adelaide, is now exploded).
But 24 per cent, from one office to another of the same
bank seems steep. Why, even Tiie Bulletin’s cheque
for a pound, owing to the necessity for re-stamping, has
a commercial value of only 19s sd, away from N.S.W.
Under Federation, should these things be ?
* • * *
The White Rock (N.S.W.) has fallen into new
hands. The old lease was cancelled—labor-conditions the
cause —and re-pegged by Dan Creed. “ The mine has had
no end of capital spent on it during the last 15 years, but
from one cause or another shareholders have not reaped
any return.” True—it once had a capital of a million,
and tons of money were wasted on one trip there, with a
champagne guzzle. The writer was one of the party, and
endangered his life by saying he didn’t feel enthusiastic.
“ From one cause or another?” Only cause was want of
value in the ore. It could not be made to pay when
silver was nearly double its present value, and what
chance has it now ?
* * * *
Great Central Freehold, Mt. Hope (N.S.W.):
Directors estimate 700,000 tons of ore available for
treatment, and that the money in hand and the 100,000
shares in reserve will be sufficient to make the mine a
regular copper-producer and div. -payer. It wants a rail-
way more than anything ; a doubtful proposition without.
Copper-mines cannot remain permanent profit-producers
without railway communication. Lloyd’s (Burraga) mine
is the only exception in Australia ; but there the ore runs
from 7 to 12 per cent., and there’s plenty of it.
* * * »
Witney-Green, N.S.W. : Suspension granted
for six months. Applicant says £2500. has already been
spent, and he wishes to raise more capital.
r * * * *
Jennings Gold-Dredging Co. Tenders invited
for the purchase of the mining property and complete
dredging-plant on Adelong Creek, N.S.W. This is the
first N.S.W. dredging-concern offered for sale since the
industry started ; but, alas ! there are others to follow. In
most cases the machinery on dredges is so situated that
it can’t be dismantled without great difficulty and ex-
pense. , .
Webb’s Consols, N.S.W., and other adjoining
leases, have been purchased by Mr. Issell Perrott, who
seems to be a great goer. Worked for lead and silver 10
years ago, and after long silence the yarn is that Che ore-
bodies are large, even yielding as high as 83 per cent,
lead and 40oz. silver per ton.
* » * *
As expected, the mining-apprentice proposal,
shvly put forward by the Broken Hill mine managers,
has been dropped on the miners showing their teeth.
* * * *
Most of the shares in Broken Hill’s richest and
biggest mines are held by absentees, in England princi-
pally • and the absence of any proposal to reach these
people by tax made it hard work to get support for even
the democratic Barton Govt, there. There was the same
feeling on the Westralian fields also.
* * * *
“ Working Miner ” is indignant:—“The height
of despotism was reached t’other day by the underground
manager of Peak Hill (W.A.) mine, who forbade the
miners (under penalty of going up the ladder) to carry
their watches below, the reason given for this restriction
being that the miners consulted their watches at knock-
off time, instead of awaiting the “call” from the shift-
boss. On the management being interviewed by a depu-
tation of the miners, however, another reason was given
—viz., that gold was being taken away in the watch-cases
—this in face of the fact that a smart army of gold-
samplers and searchers are constantly on the job. The
system of searching on that mine is so revolting to the
better class of practical miners that they seldom remain
after the first pay. The fish-hawking and bartering class
never finds the constant espionage irritating or humili-
ating. The attempted restriction was cancelled, however,
when the men bodily knocked off work.”
» * • *
Northern Associateds: The last consignment of
100 tons sent to the Fremantle Smelting Works returned
soz. to 2dwt. per ton. This makes about £50,000 worth
of ore sent in three lots, deducting railage and smelting
charges. The ore reserve at the end of the esti-
mated by the manager at 84,117 tons, valued at £761,902.
So far most of the ore has come from development work,
and it is expected that with ordinary stoping an oz. per
ton will cover mining and shipping charges, so it is pro-
posed to send away all ore of 4oz. per ton and over, and
to keep the lower grade for local treatment. Treatment
plant not yet determined ; but, if the success of the Diehl
process is permanently established, that will probably be
adopted.
* * * *
According to the official report to the Mines
Dept., Greenbushes, the Westralian tinfield which has
been just going to boom for so long, once more failed to
get much way on last year ; in fact, the population fell
back from 932 to 736. Still, whereas in ’99 654 miners won
278 tons of black tin, in 1900 306 miners won 436 tons. A
better water-supply is much needed ; there are two
schemes afoot for pumping up from the Blackwood River.
* * * •
Westralian Mine Managers’ Union has joined
with the miners in protesting against the issue of any
dredging leases over Hannan’s Lake or other untried
auriferous ground ; so that one nice little scheme is
apparently knocked out.
North Boulder: A misnamed property—not by
accident, but by design. The Boulder mine is on the
western side of the Golden Mile, the North Boulder is on
the eastern, and there are the Perseverance, Associated and
other mines between. Two or three years ago it found
some good ore, which gave out in depth, and the mine has
been passing through tribulation for over a year. The press
still befriends it: “ The North Boulder has been quietly
pegging away, and although no sensational developments
have been chronicled of late those in charge of the
property have by no means lost faith in its value and
possibilities, and the management is sanguine of some
day picking up the run of ore from the Oroya north
block.” Writer has observed in other places instances of
paid officers of a mine sticking to their faith and remain-
ing sanguine to the last while shareholders were losing
their money.
* * * •
Cabled that the Mt. Deddick Syndicators have
at last got certain underwriters to deposit £lO,OOO as a
guarantee that they will provide £1,500,000 capital necM-
sary to build the line and open the alleged mines. The
Syndicate wants a free grant of the land for the line and
a mining lease of 1000 acres, and, as it has taken nine
months to get somebody else to lend the Syn. a poor
£lO,OOO, it is pretty evident the whole scheme is simply a
grab at concession, to be floated in London with the cus-
tomary results —these being the filling of the pockets of
the first sharks, then the monotonous extension of time,
then the weariful wail of the shareholders, next the
attempted sweating of the Victorian Treasury, and finally
the repurchase of the “ concession.” This country has,
in various spots, experienced the whole painful process
before, and if Premier Peacock —who is said to be await-
ing written information—wants a reason for rejecting the
attempted grab right-off he has one to hand, viz. : the
local railway lea ;ue, representing all the shire councils
of the district, unanimously protested against the pro-
posal. Surely that ought to kill any such scheme.
* * * *
A proposal for improving the mining industry
was laid before Vic. Govt, recently by some frantic icono-
clast. The suggestion was that Govt, should appoint a
board of experts to advise mining investors as to the
prospects of ventures placed on the market. It was re-
ceived with a howl of execration, and for awhile ’Change
wore the worried look that is so familiar in the face 3 of
its clients. It seemed for a moment that the “Wild
Oat’s” occupation was gone; but the Govt., headed by
a prosperous mining boomster, refused to consider an
idea that displayed so little respect for Private Enter-
prise.

Rio-de-Oro Gold and Diamond Dredging Co.,
of Brazil, was discovered by one of the New Australian
settlers in Paraguay, who brought it under the notice of
some N.Q. mining men last year. They sent there one
Wall, who found another man in possession, but, satisfying
himself of its value, made an arrangement with the owner ;
and this is an offshoot of the concession got for dredging
purposes. The parent co. was taken up in Charters
Towers in a few hours. In the prospectus, it is said of
Mr. Wall, that he was one of the dredging founders of the
Shoalhaven River (N.S.W.) Also that he left the sub-
management of the Ohillagoe Mines and Railway' Co. to
take the management of the Chillagoe-Bedford mines.
Why on earth do people print such things in prospec-
tuses ?
* • » •
North Chillagoe Mines : This has appeared
again with the modest capital of £30,000, and if every-
thing is subscribed there will be less than £lO,OOO for the
development of a big group of mines. Such a sum is, of
course, not half enough. This time geologist Dunstan’s
report is published in full. He says the mines are full of
promise, and it lies entirely with the management, not
with the mines themselves, whether the result is failure or
success. To attempt with £lO,OOO to develop a group of
such mines, isolated in N. Queensland, seems a childish
proposition.
* • * *
“ Pelican ” : Surely the whole trouble re Mt.
Morgan water-supply might have been avoided in the
first place if the directorate had unlimited faith in the
mine. The reducing-works should have been on the
Fitzroy river, the ore being railed from the Mount. This
would have necessitated the building of a 25-miles railway
at a cost of £120,000 or so to run through 5000 tons a week
—with a small staff of railway employes. But a good set-
off would have been the saving on dam-construction and
having coal landed at the works water-borne.
• * * *
Wallaroo and Moonta, the one big mine of
S.A., made a profit of £47,008 for the year, from which
£14,550 was deducted on account of depreciation, leaving
£32,457 net. The Wallaroo section of the mines gave
22,700 tons dressed ore as against 23,354 tons in the year
before ; the product of fine copper was 2382 tons against
2413 tons, so the percentage went from 10’33 to 10'44.
Moonta yielded 12,321 tons ore against 12,390 tons, and
the value rose from 16‘78 per cent, to 17T9 per cent.—
2118 tons fine copper instead of 2080 tons. But it is to be
observed that the percentages refer to dressed ore. As a
matter of fact, Moonta lodes steadily deteriorate as they
go down ; and though Wallaroo holds up its end, costs
there are high. For every ton of copper produced by the
co costs averaged £6S 13s.—mining £5O 19s. Bd. and
smelting £l5 11s. lid. being the principal items. The co.
is still adding to its already huge plant; the latest, now
in progress, is a “ cementation ” plant to deal with the
vast heaps of old tailings. This will cost £20,000. The
co.’s financial position is satisfactory ; debiting it with
the £BO,OOO of debentures, there is still a surplus of
£119,329. But as the figures include £198,010 for “ore
furnace-products and bluestone on hand,” the position is
largely a matter of estimate.
* » • *
The Barn Bluff, Tas., “ has been placed under
offer to Mr. Harcourt Palmer ; the capital is to be
£150,000, and shareholders receive one-fourth paid-up
interest. A deposit has already been paid to insure bona-
fides, and a further sum is to be put up within four
months, and £37,500 within 16 months.” This is the
mine that is going to make the proposed railway from
Hobart to Mt. Lyell pay.

Dr. Peters, the first big scientific
who reported on Mt. Lyell, declares in Harper st;hat he
has located the rich mines of Ophir, the place where
Solomon got his gold when he was in the heightiofh 3
srlorv. Peters’ find is some distance from the
River, the natives of which country, he says, have been
at war with the Portuguese for centuries, He makes out
a good case, but his conclusion befits the mining-boomer
more than the cultured scientist. I have made friends
with the great chief, and large territories in this country
belong to me and my friends. Thus I hope I shall ha
the satisfaction of turning the most ancient goldfield in
S’Africa into the most modern one there. Many oth
gold-fields have been named Ophir, which in itself has
been enough to damn them. There is a N -S-W. Ophir,
somewhere near the spot that Hargreaves made his first
discovery. There are still a few diggers working there
but never a permanent mine. There is an Ophir l
Tasmania which also belies its name, and any indmd, . ia *
mine named Ophir gets what it deserves, a very wide
berth.

George Jenkins, of the beard, Clerk of
Vr'. ba.rllaments, has been appointed Master
ot Ceremonies to the Federal Govt, during Vic-
toria s great grovel. He already shows signs of
worry, and the messengers and attendants at
arhament House begin to wear a hunted
look. Among his duties Jenkins has to
arrange for a Commonwealth concert. On his
return from Sydney, whither he went to
receive lus instructions, he found awaiting him at
Ins office 140 shock-headed, wild-eyed men and
women singers ; 113-instriunentalists with piles of
violins, harps, cornets, trombones and violincellos ;
and about 3000 people who wanted to recite to
him. The singers all wanted to sing, and the
players to play to him. They wished to
start away on the spot, and it was half-
an-liour before Jenkins was able to explain that
Govt, had appointed a committee of experts in
•Sydney to -select the performers who are to enter-
tain the weary Yorks. This was only the begin-
ning of Jenkins’s troubles. Most of his time since
has been taken-up in dodging persons who want in-
vitations. Those who know say that, in handing
all the social arrangements over to Jenkins, Barton
has cleverly put him out of the running for the
position of Clerk to the Federal Parliament. For
the sake of the Federal Parliament it is to be
hoped this view of the matter is correct.
£50,000, mainly from Govt., has been
promised for the erection of a North Sydney hos-
pital. Another large appropriation goes to the
new Sydney Children’s Hospital, out Centennial
Park way, and now comes a request from Sydney
Prince Alfred Hospital for the completion of the
original design, which with endowment, &c.,
would approximate to £lOO,OOO. A suggestion
has been made that the Duke of York
should lay the foundation - stone of the
new block instead of tiiat of the sense-
less Dead House. Accommodation in the city
hospitals is now over-taxed ; serious and urgent
cases are turned away daily. It is by'no means
uncommon for a nearly-convalescent patient in
the Sydney hospitals to have to hui'riedly vacate
his bed to make room for a desperate case. It
would, therefore, be a good thing, if the visiting
Koyalties must lay a foundation-stone, that they
proceed to start a building intended to be of some
use to Sydney sick and suffering. Then the Govt,
might be excused for going on with the work. The
Head House idea may be ornamental, but it is
perfectly useless, and born of the moment only ;
to call it a Commonwealth conception is pure
cheek. If Australia is to have such a luxury it
should be in the Federal capital ; and, anyhow, to
spend thousands in hollow Jingo show while a
great public hospital absolutely groans for want of
means to do its duty to the suffering would be a
cold-blooded crime.
• * * *
Vic. Government is still prospecting for a
ne .v house for the State Governor, as the huge
place on St. Kilda-road is to be kept for the
Gov.-General when he is in Melbourne. Parlia-
ment has not been asked to sanction the buying
of a new Govt. House, nor is one wanted ; but
unless vigorous steps are taken to stop the job it
will be perpetrated ere the public are aware of it.
Ihe Gov.-General will not occupy Govt. House
more than a few months in the year, and might
take the State Governor in as a boarder, or the
latter might have his own rooms on the premises.
The purchase of such a house as is con tern plated,
and its fitting up, will mean about £3o,ooo—an
iniquitous and useless expenditure.
• * * *
Defaulting lawyer Grey, it is now stated,
has been enjoying a luxurious time at Fiji since a
recent outbreak of legal proceedings made his
continued presence in Victoria a somewhat too
glaring joke. The true story of Grey’s life for the
past year would provide an interesting study in
Crown Lawdepartmental tactics re “distinguished
rascals. Did Grey clear out of Victoria when
the C.L.D. uttered its first threat—some 12
months ago—to arrest him for crimes which were
then already old-established facts ? And if he
why didn’t he ? How came it that Attor-
ney-General Isaacs refrained a year ago from
issuing the threatened warrant ? Has anything,
bar public scandal, happened to convince the
Attorney-General that the warrant which was not
issued when he first threatened to issue it ought
t° be put into execution now? And if public
scandal is the sole reason for the Crown Law
Department’s recent activity, what excuse can
Attorney-General Isaacs offer for the department’s
previous lethargy ? The Bulletin • has had its
eye on the Lawyer Grey business for many weary
months, and in the light of its experience this
journal sees reason to suspect the Vic. Crown
Law Department of being accessory after the fact
to Grey’s criminal offences. In short, to put the
matter into plain English the C.L.D. has appa-
rently done its best to enable an impudent male-
factor to laugh at his victims.

“ What a tantalizing woman y’ are! I've been

The Aucklanders having announced that,
following Roberts’ advice, they weren’t going to
give the Imperial Tommies any beer, Premier
Seddon promptly wired that Tommy Must Have
his Beer, and the Govt, would see that he got it.
The Mayor had to telegraph submission. More
recently, the people who are supposed to manage
things at Auckland intimated that they weren’t
buying any fireworks for the Royal visit,'and now
Seddon says that the .Took must and shall
have fireworks, and plenty of ’em, and if local
bodies don’t make suitable preparations accord-
ingly lie’ll take the job out of their hands and run
the whole show himself. Which is exactly how
they manufacture public opinion in Russia.
* * ♦ *
When the Indian troops were in Maori-
land Jingo-Premier Seddon, who, of course,
organised everything, went with them by rail
from New Plymouth to Wellington on a Sunday,
and addresses of welcome with light refreshment
had to be provided all along the line. Observing
which things, Rev. J. Kennedy Elliott, Scotchby-
terian Moderator, wrote Seddon a letter about the
Sabbath and had it published in the papers. It
was like Elijah going for Aliab. Mr. Elliott
drew the attention of Seddon as a maker of new
holidays and working man’s friend to the fact
that on that particular day he had robbed a good
many working men of part of the oldest of holi-
days. Finally, he called upon Ahab to acknow-
ledge his transgression. Instead of which :
“ Premier’s office, Wellington, 7th March, 1901.
Rev. Sir, —Respect for the office you hold and
ordinary courtesy demand that I should acknow-
ledge receipt of your letter of the 27th ult., and I
accordingly do so. I am yours faithfully, R. J.
Seddon.”

King Ned has contributed 1000 guineas
towards the British public fund for a memorial to
his late mother, and subscriptions are to be invited

ing to see whether the “ colonial ” bauble-hunters,
who have so emphatically hung back from
seriously subscribing to similar local funds will
now shell out to make a splash in the Place where
the Titles come from.

The Melbourne police laid hands on a
lovely villain the other day. Armed with red
collecting-books with balance-sheets of various
well-known charities pasted on the inside of the
covers, and forged letters of authority to collect,
he wandered diligently among the business people,
doing “pretty fair.” In Court he mentioned in a
pained voice that he succeeded in getting Lady
and some others to promise donations of a
few pounds, but that instead of handing the
money to him, they forwarded direct to the
institutions; “Some and my partners was done
out of it! ” He has given the names of some of
his “partners.”
* * * *
Lane Cove, the Nepean, and up to Parra-
matta, the “country” of the half-caste Locke,
who abducted the little girl the other day, were
the worst spots for aboriginal outrages in the early
days. The dense scrub, irregularity of the
country, and the ease with which rivers could be
crossed, gave the blacks many advantages.
Murders were of weekly occurrence, and some-
times even pitched battles were waged. The
black blood of these parts has still a wild strain in
it, and an infusion of white blood often makes it
wilder. But, after all, the little girl has been re-
covered, quite uninjured, and her abductor has
given himself up.
Justice is a quaintly
ramshackle affair in the
gilded West. As a case
in point: A man was re-
cently charged in Perth
with taking down an
alleged It.C. priest for £7
10s. The priest was think-
ing so hard of holy things
that he couldn’t remember
whether the accused
snatched his £7 10s. or
whether he handed it
oyer; and the only other
direct witness was a pub-
lican, who said that the
alleged priest and the
alleged spieler had sundry
drinks on his premises,
and that the latter paid for
them, though whether he
paid with his own money
or with the priest’s money
there was no evidence
whatever. Both parties
were unknown to the
bench, and there was no
proof that prosecutor had
owned any money, or
that defendant wasn’t
merely a benevolent per-
son irrigating a dry
fellow-creature at his
own expense. But just
when things were at a
deadlock a detective
offered the perfectly
irrelevant evidence that
defendant had been
previously convicted
in Melbourne, and
the court, hearing
this, promptly con-
victed him again in Perth
on the off-chance. By this
kind of justice a man may
be convicted the second time
simply because he was convicted the first time,
and the third time because he was convicted the
second time, and after that, because he is a har-
dened sinner with three convictions, he may be
convicted again to an indefine extenite.
• * * »
Another specimen of the justice meted out
by Victoria’s, cheap justices. Present law is
that no female shall be employed in any
shop more than 52 hours a week. In an action
(under the factories Act) against a baker and
pastrycook a young girl swore that she began
work on Monday at 1 o’clock and left off at 11
p. m. On Tuesday she started at 4 a.m. and ceased
at 11 p.m. On Wednesday she worked from 4
a.m. to 4 p.m. On Thursday from 5 a.m. till 8
p.m. On Friday from 4 a.m. till 10 p.m. And
on Saturday from 4 a.m. till one o’clock on Sun-
day morning. (It all sounds like a recountal of
the hours during which Judge Windeyer forced
the court to sit during the Mount Rennie trial.)
She had only about 10 minutes at meal times.
Defendant in his evidence swore that he did not
work her all those hours, “she ‘only ’ worked 85
or 80 hours that week.” “ Only ”33 hours more
than the law allows. Then this'miserable bench
of beaks : “We find that this girl did not work
all the time; defendant is fined five shillings.”
Why is there no Society for Lynching Jay pees ?
The Government which unprotestingly stands by
and sees such pigs practically abrogate a vitally-
democratic law should be flung out at once.
The inhabitants of Buninyong (Vic ) have
for some time been receiving letters filled with
all the obscenity that the imagination of the most
filthy lirute could devise. Scarcely a man, woman,
or young girl in the township escaped ; and now
has come the biggest shock of all, in the discovery
that those unspeakably filthy epistles emanated
from a 16-year-old damsel in long hair and short
skirts. The Australian school-girl isn’t always
as sweet as she looks-and she doesn’t always
look sweet, either..
Another interesting young lady was in the ser-
vice of a Ballarat resident, who, one morning,
found a pair of boots on the fire, and, as there
was nobody in the room but his two-year-old son,
the child got an admonitory whacking. Some
days later, another pair of boots was burnt, and
the child caught it hot again. Then that youngster
apparently developed a destructive mania. Hats,
boots, stockings, &c., were sacrificed, and finally
a valuable umbrella was found on the fire. The
father was distracted to think he was the owner
of such a son, but was somewhat gratified by the
sympathy the young servant expressed for him.
She shortly afterwards left; and, though it was
then noticed that no more cremations took place;
the windows of the house—as of others in the
neighborhood—began to be mysteriously smashed.
This the detectives eventually found to be the
work of the damsel aforesaid, and, when arrested,
she confessed that it was she who had been burn-
ing the articles and getting the unfortunate kid
whacked therefor. »

was obtained from a Melbourne institution for
“ reclaimed ” children. One evening she ran to a

* * * *
Horsham (Vic.) Assizes, last month, a
diminutive 11-year-old lad “of apparently weak
intellect,” pleaded guilty to an assault with intent
on a little girl, whose pluck alone had ‘ ‘ prevented
the commission of a more serious crime. ” Judge
Williams said he didn’t know quite what he ought
to do with the prisoner. The miserable specimen
of humanity was very ignorant and lacking in will
power. There was also a doubt as to his age.
Therefore, leaving the Attorney-General, or some-
body, to transfer the creature to a reformatory if
the authorities thought fit, Judge Williams sen-
tenced him to three years’ imprisonment with
hard labour, and a private birching of 20 strokes.
As a tonic for weak intellects the birch ought to
be hard to beat. Interesting to know how those
20 strokes struck the warder who had the job of
inflicting them on the diminutive ninny.
» * * *
The British Admiralty’s order that “in
future ” colonial, instead of foreign, wines shall be
broken on ships’ bows at baptisms in the navy,” is
proof, according to Australia’s Agents-General, of
the affection of Britain for Australia. After
years of abortive struggling to induce the Ad-
miralty to drink Australian wine the A.-G.’s’
transport at that party’s order to break it on
ships’ noses looks high burlesque.
* * * *
Parson Welch of Ballan (Vic.) avers that
The Bulletin hurts people’s morals and moves
that it be excluded from the local public library.
The Bulletin cordially seconds the motion, and
tenders its thanks to Parson Welch. This paper,
as has here been frequently remarked, is not run
for the good of its proprietors’ health —but for the
brutal, selfish purpose of providing them with
food and drink, not to mention money to put into
the plate. The Bulletin is so pleased with
Parson Welch’s notion of kicking this paper out
of a place where it is read by the public on the
cheap, that it will, on his application, mail him a
free copy (“packed so as to be securefrom ob-
servation”) regularly for six months. This offer
is open to all other parsons who can show that, at
their instance, The Bulletin has been excluded
from a public reading-room.

Not generally known that, if a British
military pensioner becomes deranged in mind, his
miserable pittance is withdrawn. In a recent case
at Boulder City, W.A., the veteran’s wife and six
children were thus left penniless. The fag-end of
Glory is a ghastly thing.
HOW MAN REPENTS.
[For The Bulletin].
I called to the Prodigal Man to return
From the regions of gay-painted sin ;
I told him the ring and the robe were prepared,
And the feast was laid ready within.
Oh, swift seemed the feet of that Prodigal Man
To speed from his error and shame,
Yet the mouth that seemed moving in penitent prayer
Was chewing the husks as he came.'
Constance Clyde.
# * * *
A crowd that gradually swelled till it
nearly blocked Swanstqn-st., Melb., had a quarter-
hour of exquisite thrill the other day. Some
workmen, standing on a plank suspended at the
fifth storey, were painting the face of a high
building. It was knock-off time. To leave work
they had to swarm up 12ft. of rope, climb over the
eaves and descend through the building. Two
successfully accomplished the feat, but the third
mounted steadily hand over hand, grabbed the
eaves, then slipped down to the plank again.
Twice more he tried, with like result; then he
struck an idea. Putting a loop in the rope just
above his head he swarmed up once more, caught
the eaves, and had just stuck his foot through the
loop when his hands slipped and he toppled over
and hung suspended 60ft. above the street! Men
gasped, women looked round for a clean spot to
faint on, and everybody shrieked for somebody
to do something. By this time a constable, who
had sized-up the situation, had mounted inside,
opened a window, and hauled the man in just as
he was getting black in the face. As he was
drawn in he gave an expiring kick at the plank
and upset a pot of cream paint, which landed on
the verandah and sent a splashed and disappointed
crowd home swearing.

On St. Patrick's day last a North Mel-
bourne publican had on his bar-counter a lot of
shamrocks in a soup-plate of water. An old Ger-
man came in for his daily pint, and gathering a
fist-full of the “chosen leaf,” swallowed it with
relish. Bung didn’t notice anything till the Ger-
man was departing. Then, observing the deple-
tion, he said, “Here draw it mild, old man !
One shamrock is enough to take.” “Shamrogs !
said the German—“ vos dose shamrogs ? I tought
d y vos vater-gresses ! ”

Victoria has got into such a habit of
promptly arresting any ancient and dilapidated-
looking person that a Melbourne constable actually
ran-in on of the State’s pensioners the other day as
a vagrant, and had him washed and combed and
put on a hominy diet for a week ere it was dis-

ago a party of
male Australian
tourists examin-
ing a picture gal-
lery in Rome
with their guide,
got mixed up
with an English
party similarly
engaged. The
guide, seeing the

excellent picture
which was hung
in a bad light
remarked, “Ah,
yes, thatis Yosef
and the Pootifar.”
An English lady
said to a young
Cornstalk who was
next to her, “What
is the subject of
the picture?” The
youth lifted his hat
with a very bad
imitation of the
manner of a
Frenchman, and,
blushing a little in
spite of himself, and
trying to hide his
confusion by affect-
ing a foreign ae-
cent, replied,
“ Mademoiselle,
it is the ancient
story of a young
man who was
singularly defi-
cient in the
quality of tact
and who was de-
servedly punish-
ed for his gross
want of gallantry on
the occasion of a memorable
interview with a lady who has
prominent mention in sacred his-
tory.” Then his new acquaintance saw the
situation. She fled, and gat her out, and was
seen no more in the gallery that day. A discussion
arose among the Australians as to whether their
good-natured companion had behaved rightly on
this occasion. It was resolved by a narrow
majority that he had given the only explanation
of the picture which a man of any delicacy of
feeling could offer to one of the opposite sex.
One M‘Guiness, of Melbourne, fell in
through trying to take down the State Treasurer
for an old-age pension while he had £l6O in the
keeping of the Ballarat Banking Cos. He had,
when applying for his pension, informed the
magistrate that all his worldly wealth amounted to
three solitary sovereigns. The court didn’t im-
prison him ; they did worse {to a man like that) —
they grabbed £2O of his £l6O and turned him out
lamenting.

The Chapter of St. Paul’s Cathedral,
Melb., take a simple commercial view of the build-
ing’s situation. Years ago, when it was in course
of erection, the Chapter nearly decided to sell the
land at an enormous price to a boom syndicate.
The other week they rented the Swanston-street
frontage for £2OO to “ responsible persons ” who
are now running up a consecrated grand-stand for
Jookoyork purposes. And, by the way, the
responsible persons seem likely to make a pretty
holy profit on the transaction.

* * * *
Occasionally little spurts of enthusiasm
about the glory due to “our soldiers” who have
returned from Africa start up like the fading hic-
coughs of a worn-out drunk. Victoria lias given
a feeble “ hie ” over her “boys,”and inspired War
Minister M'Culloch to show a little cheap hys-
teria. “They have done splendid work,” he
gushes, “ and why should they not receive their
reward?” And the reward! “Mr. M'Culloch
says he does not think it will be possible to form
the returned men into a guard of honor for the
Duke of York, but he says they should certainly
be singled out for social favor during the parades.
If they survive all this glory, and do not burst
with emotion when the Royal eye lights upon
them, they may even be allowed to stand round
the distinguished visitors when the animals are
fed, to keep off the dogs and the democracy.
■* * * *
From Adelaide Advertiser :
The concert which took place in Adelaide Town
Hall in aid of unemployed members of the first S.A.
Contingent resulted in a benefit to 19 men
of five and sixpence each. The City Treasurer
paid over the money to all but six of the beneficiares on
Tuesday evening." There were several undesirable
features in connection with the concert. For in-
stance, 91 persons occupied seats in the dress-circle on
complimentary tickets, although Lord Tennyson, who
received complimentary tickets, set a good example by
paying for them. The receipts were £27 17s. 6d., and
expenses swallowed up £22 13s. 9d., including £4 for re-
freshments, leaving a balance for distribution of £5 3s.
9d. The Town Hall was given free.
How Australia does love and honor its Heroes !
Its heart bleeds for them—but not its purse.
First contingent, five and sixpence apiece. What
will the last contingent get !
* * * *
Longstaff was given the job of turning
out the Vic. Government’s invitation-card for the
ceremonies connected with the opening of the
Federal Parliament and the Jookoyork shivoos;
but, being too full of work, handed the under-
taking over to young Norman Lindsay after put-
ting in a little preliminary sketching. The result
is a creditable piece of work which has excited
the wrath of the local artistic Little Pe.dling-
ton. The Australasian printed a criticism of
quite uncalled-for ferocity; but, by way of ap-
praisement, its own artist’s card has since been
accepted by the Commonwealth Govt, for one
function, and has been published. It proves to be
a melancholy re-hash of the hideous cartoon that
was hung out on Parliament House when Mel-
bourne went mafick-mad —a few commonplace,
lackadaisical female figures, an impossible horse and
a profusion of shields, streamers, and furbelows
illustrating Kipling’s pie-dish chant about the
Young Queen and the Old.
* * * •
Despite the visit of the Yorks, Mayoral
candidates come forward slowly in Maoriland.
The majority are probably ’cute enough to see
that they would personally have to foot very heavy
expenses. In Auckland it looks like a walk-over
for the 83-year-old Dr. Campbell—one of the
country’s wealthiest men and oldest settlers. His
big office now stands in the place where his tent
was pitched 60 years ago. In Wellington the
retiring Mayor, Aitken, a strong Scotehbytenan
merchant, is to be opposed by the sporting hams-
ter Tom Wilford. The contest between Sanctity
and Sport is expected to be a very willing go.

What promised to be a beautiful romance
finished up with a loud crash in Melb. City Court
recently, when the hero got twelve months
“hard.” He was a plain, ordinary John Hum-
phreys, but had passed himself off as a baronet,
and so captured the affections of a seasoned widow-,
whom he actually married under his title, xne
protests of a grown-up daughter were of no avail,
though the latter is now revenged, as it was
through pawning her watch that step-papa go
into trouble.

* * * *
The sin of the reel-headed father is visited
upon him, so to speak, by his illegitimate child.
In a recent Melbourne libel case the question
arose as to whether the auburn defendant was
really the father of a ver-y dark young woman’s
baby. Other gentlemen friends of the damsel
who had appeared in court were also very dark,
so when the infant was found to be the colour of
The Bulletin’s wrapper there was nothing left
for defendant but a suggestion that the mother
had probably coloured the interesting event by an
inordinate craving for tomatoes during her time
of travail.
* * * *
Adelaide City Council was lately in-
vited by the Holder Govt, to spend £lOOO in
erecting a triumphal arch for the Yorks’ visit,
but declined unless Govt, guaranteed to indem-
nify it by Act for thus illegally spending rate-
payers’ money. This is meritorious, but the same
council is engineering the unspeakable Snow
tramway monopoly —a scheme which pledges the
citizens’ tramway rights for 21 years, unless Par-
liament shuts down on the awful job.

Adelaide telephone operators, all ladies,
follow Melb. lady operators in refusing to adopt
the head-gear now universal in Sydney. All
the efforts of Widower Todd, who is very sus-
ceptible to the female glance, are unavailing ; and
the ladies remain adamant as the Melb. beauties
who are credited with having successfully resisted
several Postmasters-General.

There has been much feeling in England
and in S’Africa about the appointments made to
responsible posts in the Transvaal ; the friends
and dependents of the capitalist and Jewish gang
having been pitchforked into offices. The single
appointment which no party seems to find fault
with is that of an Australian—Major MTnerney
—to the chief magistracy of Pretoria. And this
is the only good billet given to an Australian,
despite all that was said about the value of the
services of the Australians in the war. By the
way, “ Tim ” MTnerney richly deserved that ap-
pointment ; was it not he who dragged into the
trial of Hans Cardua that odoriferous precedent
from the—hold your nose !'—Vic. Dungey case.

“Bung A.” • A “ contingenter ” recently
offered a friend of present writer a “ second-
return ” railway-ticket, Sydney to Bourke and
back, for 255. (full price, 84s. 6d.). Explained
that he wanted a few days off duty and a few bob
to spend; and, by securing a free ticket for the
purpose of saying farewell to a “sick sister” in
Bourke, he had killed both birds with one lie.
This blessed country is sweating cash for the
Ghamberlain-Rhodes-Rothschild gang.

Lord Rosebery and Miss Rothschild were
married in a workhouse. The Registrar, to whom
they went in the first instance, had his office at
the establishment, as secretary to the Board of
Guardians.
Re the power of modern firearms. Other
day at Warwick (Q.) rifle-range, a bullet pene-
trated 3ft. of earth and a foot of hardwood, and
glanced off a hard wood support into the marker’s
chest. Result—military funeral.
Shocking par from Melb. Outpost :
J. G. Barrett, formerly member for Carlton South, was
unseated by Moloney in 1897, and last year, on Moloney’s
retirement, he was unsuccessful against D. V. Hennessey,
in spite of the fact that Brennan, a co-religionist of D. V.’s,
was also standing.
Now, is the election for Carlton South a sectarian
contest or does the Outpost merely assume that it
ought to be so ? And if the Outpost can take
R.C. bigotry for granted without raising a protest,
why does it complain, re the Exhibition Secretary-
ship, that “ Masonic and Orange considerations
enter largely into many appointments ? ”
The most important preparations being
made for the reception of the “deah Duke” are
those for giving the highest artistic expression to
Australian female beauty. Picture the delight of
“ deah York ” when he discovers that the P.D.
figure, which is the delight of the “ best ”
European circles, has been adopted by all classes
in Australia, He will recognize the famous
curves at a first glance and exclaim “P.D. Bai
jove ! ”*
Entertaining the “deah Duke” will en-
tail much work for function-attending citizens.
They will soon be staggering under the weight of
the severest razzle they have ever known, and it
behoves them to take care that they stagger
decorously. Even the most experienced drinkers
can’t afford to mix their whiskies at such times.
Therefore the advice to one and all is D.C.L., and
don’t you forget it. No demon of Undoing lurks
in the D.C.L. ’Tis the “ Whisky of the Wise.”*
