A WOMAN’S LETTER.
_ , _ Sydney, April 2, 1901.
Mt Dear Myee,—
The Empire Hotel was the scene of the liveliest
doings in last Friday’s very serious-minded elec-
tions. At the Empire, Mr. Barton’s dinner
crowded the dining-hall with the Protectionist
party, who are nearly all—let me say it in a whis-
per—distinctly ugly men. For that matter, our
politician mostly is. Australian politics haven’t
yet turned out anything after the pattern of Rose-
bery, Chamberlain, Balfour—the brand that
brushes its hair, w""”s straight collars, and culti-
vates a profile. ' • urs ” tend to baggy chins and
trousers, bad boots and a hang-dog carriage of the
head, and in spi te ' being a rabid patriot I feel
that it is about ..t. possible to indulge in any
athletic sentiment over an Australian politician as
—to borrow an impossibility of Rhoda Broughton’s
—to be sentimental in a bad smell.
You will not be surprised, therefore, to hear
that women deeply regret that the only smart-
looking man in our politics didn’t get a seat.
Mind you, this doesn’t apply to the wives of our
politicians. Some of them are among the
brightest-looking women in Sydney—which, by
the way, is singularly empty of anything ap-
proaching the beautiful just now. Even the
downy-looking debutantes who all came out toge-
ther a couple of years ago, and shed a lovely com-
plexion over Sydney Society, are fading fast after
two seasons’ wear and tear.
As we waited, we heard some rosy stories about
our Senators. There was one man who was going
to put up for the Senate on the ground that his
name began with Z, and would be placed where
voters couldn’t miss it. Another man was trying
for a seat because his name was the same name as
the name of another man whom everybody knew.
I had a chat with Mdlle. Trebelli this morning,
and found her bearing up briskly under her
melancholy new name—Antonia Dolores. The
fascinating warbler—she looked ever so charming
in a black brocade robe a la princense, her brown
hair brushed severely off her forehead, and a lot
of black glittering things hanging from her cream
lace yoke—changed her name over in America
because the ’Murkans insisted on mistaking her
for her mamma. Old Trebelli is a household word
there, and it was considered that she, our genuine
“ little Trebelli,” would be a little too antiquated
to be worth hearing. This confusion was naturally
unsatisfactory to little Trebelli, and after much
consultation she determined to alter her title and
fall back on a family name. In time she will drop
the Trebelli altogether. In fact she doesn’t want
to be called Trebelli any longer, and will feel
obliged if everybody starts Doloring her as soon as
possible.
The Dolores Cbncert Co. now consists of herself
and a pianist, a young American man with a
reputation, who will open with her at Sydney
Town Hall, Wednesday, April 10th.
Mdlle. tells me that in America everybody is
for foreign songs. It must be French, or it must
be German, to please a ’Murkan audience. The
charming English songs—Purcell’s, for instance, or
Shakespeare’s songs that Dolores makes so much
of—don’t go there at all. It was through this
pronounced demand that Mdlle. went over to
Paris lately and looked up a lot of old French
songs, 15th century folk-songs, simple as simple
can be, quaint and vivid, and she will sing these
for us, in her own inimitable way, shortly.
Another concert on the tapis is that of Miss
Kate Rooney, our popular contralto with a voice
full of tears, who takes farewell of Sydney on
Easter Monday night, Centenary Hall. Miss
Rooney leaves for London —to study under
Pant ey—next week, and is to be seen on view in
Nicholson’s window—but only in a charming pastel
sketch by Mr. Fred. Leist.
Sydney Liedertafel set the new season’s music
going with a concert last Wednesday, the first of
their twentieth season. The large audience con-
sisted of the, usual mixture of opera-cloaks from
the suburbs and women in hats, with an inky
sprinkling of Society in the front row. Mrs.
Burdekin was there in black, short-sleeved, with
her youngest daughter, a pretty, fair-haired girl,
who will make her debut next season. Mrs. John
See was in black with a string-coloured fringe out-
lining her apron-skirt, short sleeves, and a black
note in her hair. By the way, the Sees are now
grand-parents, and at a public luncheon lately
they say that our Acting-Premier was very much
affronted because gran’pa Dibbs congratulated
him on being grand-parent to a bouncing boy, as
if everyone didn’t know that the Sam-Hordem
baby was a girl !
To return to the Liedertafel—this society has
arrived at the point of being able to do extremely
good work when it likes. But some pieces were
not carefully rehearsed on Wednesday; the
orchestra wandered about in strange tempos occa-
sionally, and many different sharps and flats made
themselves disagreeably felt now and then. The
Weber Overture and the Tyrol-Part Song for
Men only had an air of dress-rehearsal about them
that would be sure to annoy people who didn’t
pay for their seats. Otherwise the concert was
agreeable. The large complement of women-
fiddlers— mere girls, many of them—are proving
themselves worthy a fiddle and a chair. They all
wear white frocks and short sleeves, and, as
the Australian foot will hold its own with
anyone’s, they certainly heighten effects. Hetty
Holroyd warbled in a pale blue silk, with a
turquoise sash, and turquoise sleeves spangled
in silver, and wore the indispensable rosette of
black tulle in her corsage, She sang a scena from
the Vesperi Siciliani with great taste, and quite a
noticeable improvement both in art and feeling.
Her voice is silvery-clear; she doesn’t overload
with expression, but hints at more warmth than a
year or two ago when her singing seemed to me
delicious m timbre but cold. She also sang
Somervell’s “Shepherd’s Cradle Song,” “Poor
Vandering One,” and a few exclamations from
Elisabeth in Wagner's “Pilgrims’ Chorus,” with
tne .Liedertafel singing the Pilgrims’ music in the
background.
Speaking of singers, Nellie Stewart has been
for the State Concert to sing before their
x orknesses.
The lady with a foreign accent who doesn’t
always get things right in English and speaks of
body-very important these days—as Capt.
Wallaby, is also a songstress.
Something like a sensation in the way of publi-
cation is now in the hands of the printers for
publication at the conclusion of the Coninghatn
divorce-case, with a view of enabling the public
to be its own expert in handwriting. It is to be
illustrated, I am told, by photo.-reproductions of
all the documents, admitted and denied by the
petitioner and the respondent in the suit; and by
many which did not see the light of Court during
the progress of the trial. Likewise the public is
to be permitted to gaze upon the counterfeit pre-
sentment of the mysterious “Zero,” while all the
mysteries of the case are to be exposed to the
glimpses of the moon.
** Why Smith Left Home” was the reason of
Charles Arnold’s re-appearance at the Palace on
Saturday, with a crowded, jolly-looking dress-
circle to welcome back the popular laughter-
makers. Great changes among the Joneses. He
who used to be Bishop of Ballarat—item, George
Willoughby, husband of pretty little Ada W.—is
now the notorious Smith who left home. The
chubby face and blue eye of this domestic hero,
and ascertain infantile stolidity of expression,
make Smith a riotously funny creature in carpet
slippers—and Charles Arnold cuts-in the most
perfect little silhouette of a German.
“The farce—American, and full of Yankee fun
that goes ‘over’ a local audience—doesn’t call
for much dressing, but gets all it asks for. Willowy
Agnes Knights, as Mrs. Smith, is a well-dressed
woman in a snappy mauve voile frock over apple-
green glace silk, with a tall green-swathed waist,
and glim; vies of green underskirt. Miss Knights’
hair is worn in a rich sunset-color, to add to Mrs.
Smith’s charms. Even more fetching is a white
serge costume, with bolero of heavy cream
lace, falling in ends down the front of
the skirt. I liked less her white ball-
dress, fixed up with white chiffon and pink
roses. Hope Mayne plays a runaway bride in
white flounced muslin, which exactly matches her
unaffected style of prettiness ; and Frank Denton
runs away with her in a cadet suit, brass buttons,
a brown make-up, and that same young smile.
As for Miss H illy Booth, who was the real reason
Why Smith left Home, you will be surprised to
hear that she wears every color she oughtn't to,
beginning with a green-and-yellow speckled blouse
toned up with a purple throat-ribbon.
By the way, Sydney pays Smith the compli-
ment of considering lie leaves home too early, and
to lengthen the programme Mr. Arnold is getting
ready a new curtain-raiser for next week. The
name of the little play is “The Grey Parrot,” a
dramatised s ort story of that rollicking writer,
W. W. Jacobs.
A certain very high dignitary probably doesn’t
know that the carriage in which he went to the
railway station t’other day was hired from an
undertaker. There can’t be much superstition
about Sydneyites, considering the open manner
in which certain big undertaking firms are com-
peting for the vehicle-hiring business.
Die fact that a house which stands upon a hill
cannot be hid is apparently deeply borne in on the
mind of the master-architect of the R.C. Church.
Go where you will about the country, if you ad-
mire some prominently-situated edifice you are
sure to be told upon inquiry that it is “ the Con-
vent ”or “ the Chapel.” From Bondi the eye is
caught by a large building on the side of Waverley
Hill; from the Centennial Park the two most
conspicuous objects are the Loretto Convent and
the home of the Little Sisters of the Poor standing
side by side on the Randwick Hill ; while away
towards Kensington, on the crest of one of the
hills, stands the training college. St. Ignatius’
College occupies the finest position of any school
around Sydney; you couldn’t better the site of St.
Mary s Cathedral, and the Cardinal’s Palace at
Manly can lie seen for miles. All splendid adver-
tisements, if nothing else; and then, too, religion
would naturally be more at home in an atmos-
phere of beauty.
One of the nicest things about Queen Vic-
toria was the way she cared for her dogs. She
had them nursed till they died, and never would
have a dog destroyed. I think a picture that
would show the old, grey, homely-looking lady in a
more becoming setting than any Court grandeur
would depict her in her summer-house at Windsor,
wearing a big apron, and sitting down holding
one of her famous Dog Receptions. Eighty or
90 dogs—distinguished, well-bred personages—
used to come in one by one, and get patted and
petted by the royal hand, and rub an ear against
the homely apron. I haven’t heard whether they
were announced, but expect they’d announce
themselves under such circumstances. Add to
that a mushroom hat on the queenly head, and
you would have a true and pleasant picture of the
woman who always considered a dog’s feelings.
By the way, I wish some other humanitarian
would improve the “ benches in the park.” In our
Gardens you find seats so impossible that they
seem constructed with the sole idea of keeping
people from sitting on them. Some slant at an
angle that requires a pair of long legs, firmly
heeled into the grass, to stop the occupier
from slipping off. And some are shaped like
benches in a fit, with contorted backs, and seats
that writhe up in the middle, and defy you to
imagine you are taking a rest. And some rise up
at the edges, and leap forward in the small of the
back. And one that outclasses all other malfor-
mations is so complicated in its architecture that
I saw a man put his legs through the back and
turn his back to the front of the seat, and lean his
arms on the top of the back before he could
honestly say he was in a sitting position.
Apropos, a nationality that is generally regarded
as phlegmatic, and considerate, and slow in
making up its large fat mind, is responsible for
the following love letter. The woman who re-
ceived it, and sends it to me, says she had never
spoken to the foreigner who sent it:—
Dear Miss J.—l muse use my pen and ink of writing
you a few lines hoping you exquise me—if I am on the
side, my a few lines, might not give you—as much plea-
eure as a kind feaverable letter from you will give me.
Dear J.—l must give you a little sheck to your feelings
whil one single word gut and true and true I gut and
short till long, from all my heart and mind is my song.
Dear Miss J., I am in neet of a friend a Dear Friend I
might have found in you and such a friend is allways gut
to me—or to you—must exquise me considder over it as
follows Dear Miss J. would you like to own me as a friend
I would be alwais goot and true to you intill I die. Dear
Miss J., Yours very affectionately— Hans Blank. Kiss
me Quick.
The influence of back-block air on German sen-
timent is evidently productive of something in-
describable.
I don’t mean to say that sentiment in Germany
needs any fostering. It grows like weeds over
everyone, even little boys in socks. Some English
and German schoolboys were lately asked to write
down what they most wanted to be, and out of
hundreds of declarations there was just one boy
—German—who wanted to be a woman ! and his
reason was given with solemn fervor that would
have driven an English boy to hide his little
wooden head for ever. “/ would like to be a
woman so that / could love ! ”
Of course, there are some seats in the Gardens
that accommodate themselves to the human form ;
but it has always been my hard lot to find this kind
of accommodation blistering alone in the glaring
sun, as far as possible from a sheltering tree.
Also, there are not enough seats in the Gardens.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“Every day for some weeks past there has
been a worshipping procession of women to Govt.
House to inspect the apartments which are being
decorated for the Yorks. The whole building is
in the hands of artisans, who have been simply
pestered by the prying brigade. Some of the
workmen have rude ways of resenting these in-
trusions. A scientifically-directed extra-hard
dab with the brush will send a splash of paint in
the desired direction, and would on ordinary
occasions be resented ; but there is a charm about
a green paint-spot on the front of a new veil, or
a red splash on a bonnet, when the owner can
boast that it came from ‘ the dear Duke’3 bed-
room.’ However, the visits are now to be
stopped. The nuisance became so great that the
Minister for Works had to intervene.
“ A story which Tourguenief, the Russian nove-
list, told, will be repeated in substance many
times over in Australia on the occasion of the'
visits of the Yorks. The Czar was to pass through
the village in which Tourguenief lived, but the
novelist was ill and could not go out. After the
Monarch’s carriage passed a group of villagers
rushed in to tell him about it. They said they
saw a beautiful man in glittering white armour,
covered with jewels, who stood up in his gorgeous
chariot—drawn by four lovely horses—and dazzled
them so that they all fell with their faces in the
dust. When Tourguenief got well he inquired
what had really happened, and found that the Czar
had passed quietly through in a simple frock-coat
and top-hat. Many people in Melbourne are just
ais superstitious as those Russian serfs. They will
actually see a small, undersized, ordinary-looking
young man and his stout wife. They are prepared
to belive they have seen something more than a
demigod, accompanied by his archangel.
“Prof. Peterson, the new musical authority of
Melb. University, was not consulted in the pre-
paration of the programme for the State concert
to be given while the Yorks are in Melbourne.
Musgrove, Caron, and Slapoffski ivere consulted ;
but the official academic, Presbyterian, orthodox,
Westminster-confession-of-faith professor was
quite ignored by the authorities. Musicians are
also angered at the efforts being made to oust an
organist from a leading kirk, in order that the
new-comer may be offered the post.
“Seems that the Metrop. Liedertafel’s dinner
to Prof. Peterson, the other week, had no ulterior
motive whatever. Someone who knows the
circumstances of the little shivoo says it was
arranged by Vice-president Byron Moore as a
fraternal welcome to the new-chum Prof, from
the senior musical society (for the Met. considers
itself senior to Melb. Liedertafel) and as an assur-
ance that he is not to be shut out in the cold be-
cause he was the successful applicant for Marshall-
Hall’s billet. Also it gave the genial promoter a
chance to compose a humorous musical menu-card
on a plan of his own. There was no partisan feel-
ing in the matter; in fact, the welcome had the
sympathy of the rival Professor’s warmest sup-
porters. Mr. Peterson doesn’t intend to wield
the baton for any Liedertafel or orchestra.
He has come to Melb. to take over the Chair of
Music and the University Conservatorium; and
people who have met the dry, matter-of-fact
Scotch gentleman and his charming Hungarian
wife, seem to agree in expecting good results.
“Government House is neat as a new pin, in
readiness for Royalty. Outside, it’s clean but
bilious-looking. Inside, there are passable patches
in a sea of gilt and garishness. No one on the
premises can be induced to give away the culprit
responsible for certain color-orgies. ‘ The people
at ’Ome,’ seems to be the prescribed reply to all
inquiries—‘which I don’t believe there’s no such
pussons ! ’
“The Gov.-General’s den, with thick crimson
carpet and old-rose satin hangings, is warm and
snug. Two other smallish rooms, generally used
as a morning snuggery by the ‘Missus’ of Govt.
House, will also be temporarily given up to York.
With their China-blue and white papers go white
wood-work, and Empire hangings and furniture,
and Persian rugs in red, cream, and a suspicion of
blue. The private drawing-room has dull-red and
gold walls, peacock-blue carpet, bordered with
chocolate and deep-blue, faint-blue hangings
strewn with pink posies, under-curtains of Swiss
net.
“In the dining-room there’s impressive maho*
gany furniture ; and another oasis is the main
staircase —ivory-white with rich crimson carpet.
It’s no use my attempting to describe the ball-
room color scheme. You’d accuse me of dream-
ing rainbows and seeing kaleidoscopes. Experts
say it will light up well and prove becoming.
We ll see ! Those blue hangings and plush settees
are trying. Haven’t you noticed how pasty all the
inmates of the blue d.c. at the Princess’s appear
from the stalls, unless they’ve laid on the make-up
liberally? Near at hand they show to more ad-
vantage.
“ The Duchess’s bedroom is decidedly pink, in
spite of much counteractive grey - green carpet
with rose-wreathed border, green-striped hangings
of satin damask, and flowered cretonne furniture-
slips. The hangings, as a rule, are blends of silk
and wool. In the Hopetoun suite the colors are
blue and cream. Some of the other rooms have
curtains strewn thick with natural-color hyacinths;
and a blue bower is assigned to the ladies-in-wait-
iag.
“York’s retinue—don’t forget—includes among
other grandees a Prince, a Duke, and a Viscount
—game almost worth the candle of smart local
spinsters, who, unless their chums belie them, are
already groaning under the yoke of hygienic
beauty-process which combines the most drastic
features of fresh-air cure and milk-and-fruit diet
with vapour-bath treatment.
“At present the Maddens spend most of their
time at Macedon inhaling all the mountain air
they can ere handing over the keys of ‘ Govern-
ment Cottage.’ The Head of the House has not
been well; and official mourning further excuses
the clan’s seclusion.
“ The girls of the Waxellency Madden family,
with the connivance of mamma, have pre-
served all The Bulletin’s wicked tributes to His
Casual Excellency in ascrap-book. Caricaturedraw-
ings, scornful criticisms, and the most awful allu-
sions to his vice regal moustache are pasted in the
volume, so that when his nearest and dearest feel
nauseated by a Madden picture or paragraph in
poor old Paunch they can get an immediate cor-
rective. Papa runs no risk of ever being too
much of a hero to his own family. In the ordi-
nary way they divide him into three persons:—lst,
The nice, well-barbered, amiable, voluble Pa who
keeps the wolf from the door. 2nd, The Trans-
cendental Pa depicted in Paunch and the
Respectable Press. 3rd, His Waxellency of The
Bulletin. Then they shake hina into a united
trinity and worship him with a grain of salt.
“A huge crowd rolled up for Arens’ last night.
Local critics, Marshall Hall, &c., are raving over
the fareweller’s Tannhauser. Musically, mere
women admire his version ; but he is not ecstatic-
ally beautiful, and why on earth don’t they let him
sing in his ovvn language ? His English, if it can
be called so, is English as it might be spoken by a
talking Russian bear.
“The woman who lives in terror of the man
who is never under the bed has, after all, a com-
paratively easy time compared with a politician in
an Australian State, who, when his wife leaves
him, has an abiding horror of the possibility of
some scheming hussy invading the sanctuary of
his dormitory, and taking advantage of his unpro-
tected state to demand £lOOO down. The poor
man has good reason, too. The story goes that
he had the misfortune to find a calculating parlor-
maid under his bed one evening years ago, who
insisted that she would ‘ tell all ’ if money were
not forthcoming. The cash was found, but the
innocent victim’s confidence in womankind is
shattered. Now in the absence of the ‘missus’
lie absolutely refuses to sleep in the same house
with the designing sex, but betakes himself to
another establishment, which is something in the
nature of a safe-deposit, and where he can snore
untroubled by blackmail possibilities. Some
night a bold, bad damsel will burgle that retreat,
and then a prominent politician will be a candi-
date for the lunatic asylum.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULL.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Hie gist of John Norton’s open letter to
Barton in last Sunday’s Truth is—“ I’m a better
y gentleman than you, anyhow ! ”
B. R. Wise has two sets of admirers, one
political, the other personal. The former deplore

A sight in Sydney suburban train—an
Australian ex-Premierandhislady, sitting opposite
each other with a paper of prawns between them,
munching away and calmly throwing heads and
tails out of the window.
Both Isaacs, who “passed” the Yic.
State Premiership, and Peacock, who accepted it,
are sons of tailors. Mr. Isaacs’ father used to
wield the shears at Beechworth, Yic., while Pea-
cock’s is still in business at Creswick (Yic.).
John See has reached N.S.W. Premier-
ship in his 55th year. Like ex-Premier Reid, he
arrived in Australia in very early boyhood. He
has been 21 years in Parliament and is what is
known as “self-made.” He used to be one of
Henry Parkes’ favorite butts.
Eighteen members of N.S.W. State Par-
liament have been returned to the House of Re-
presentatives. Five —Barton, M'Millan, Bruce
Smith, F uller and Sydney Smith—are ex-members;
while three—Barrister A. H. Conroy, Jam-manu-
facturer Edwards and H. Willis —are new to
politics.
Sydney breathed contentedly when it was
announced that president J. Randal Carey, of
Sydney Daily Telegraph, had got a substantial
Govt, billet. It was clear for months that, until
president Carey was disposed of, there would be
no peace in the N.S.W. Cabinet, and no certainty
as to Lyne’s political doings. As the billet to
Carey straightened Cabinet and Premier, the
public feeling was, “ Let her go, Gallagher ! ”
After 62 years in Devonshire-street ceme-
tery, the bones of Allan Cunningham are to be
transferred to the vicinity of his monument in
Sydney Botanic Gardens. Cunningham was not
only a botanist but an explorer, and though of
delicate physique, nerved himself to numerous
desperate privations in the early Australian bush.
He died under 50 yeais, but into his short life lie
crowded a vast amount of valuable, some of it im-
perishable, public work.

DINGLEHOFEN: “ How it vas, [?]key, dot you never
see Yiddishers keep hotels?”
SCHLOSSBEIMER : “ Dere vas no excitement about
it; efferybody knows de price of a drink. So
if you serve a man mit a beer und ask him
a shillin' 1 for it, vat does he do?”
DINGLEHOFEN: “He beats you down to sixpence,
ain’t it? ”
SCHLOSSHEIMER : “ Not by a jugful. He drinks
half of it und throws de rest all over your
viskers, und calls you a dam schvindler. A
bisness vere efferybody knows de price of de
goods is only fit for blackfellers.”

ing t’other night, wore a thistle in his button-
hole. Interjector : “What’s that you’ve got in

Yet another centenarian. John Bond, at
present an inmate of the hospital ward of Mel-
bourne Immigrants’ Home, was born in England
in 1800. He arrived in Sydney in 1809 in the
troopship Alert, and went to Melb. with Batman
from Tasmania in ’35. One of his early occupa-
tions was whaling in Hobson’s Bay.
“What’s in a name?” One unknown
Denis Roger O’Sullivan, who put up for N.S.W.
Senate, polled about 13,600 votes —many electors
apparently thinking him identical with State
Works Minister E. W. O’Sullivan. In the crowd
outside the city newspaper-offices exclamations
were common at the poor show the O’Sullivan
was making.

There’s a man in Sydney who makes quite
£4OOO a year by pulling teeth —does nothing else
all day. He uses only a local anaesthetic, the
exact nature of which seems to be a secret. He
took out 14 teeth right away for a Bulletin man
t’other day, who, though perfectly conscious all
the time, felt no pain whatsoever. This isn’t an
advt. ; it is a fact.
Could W. B. Dailey have looked across
from his resting-place, at Waverley, and seen the
first three men who asked the electorate named
after him to send them to the Federal Parliament,
he would have quivered with disgust. Member
for Dailey—a Wilks, a Law, or an I. R. Cohen !
Pity that young barrister “Jack” Dailey was
not in Sydney and ready for the Federal polling !
Still another of Melb.’s better-known
woman-doctors goes out of practice. Few, very
few, risk matrimony and medicine together. And
The Bulletin is sorry to hear that one of the
earliest who did, the philanthropic Dr. Constance
Stone, wife of Dr. Egryn Jones, has for
months past been compelled to virtually cease
practice through a most serious illness.
According to a recent article by John
Ferguson, of Parramatta :
G. H. Reid was a competitor for a prize announced
to fie awarded to the best ode on the opening of the great
Sydney Exhibition in what was called the Garden Palace.
The prize was awarded to Henry Kendall, not to Reid ;
whereat Reid was so disgusted with the shortsighted in-
capacity of the judges that he had his wretched stuff
printed on superior paper and circulated amongst his
admiring friends. We think, however, the judges will
not be held to have been blameable when we quote the
final couplet of Reid’s rhyming atrocity which he calls a
poem. It runs thus :
“ Co-operation was clearly understood
By that best ’mong modern princes, Albert the Good.”
Hence, perhaps, the legend that Georgey Porgey
published a book of poems in his younger days.
Adelaide Anglican Bishop Harmer de-
pends for his screw upon an endowment by the
Baroness Burdett-Coutts, made 50
years ago in Bishop Short’s time.
The original amount, £20,000,
which was badly invested, only
yields a miserable £7OO a year, and
the Church is now passing round
the hat to keep His Gaiters out
of the Destitute asylum.
Melbourne hasn’t a bene-
factor to its name just now. Fif-
teen twenty years ago William
Clar\e was writing big cheques
for good causes ; Francis Ormond
followed suit; even “Money”
Miller, the stingy man, gave
£5OOO towards the building of St.
Paul’s Cathedral because he
thought the appearance of the
city would be improved by it.
And there was Joe Clarke, who
distributed tens of thousands of
pounds among nice little widows
and other deserving institutions
of the same kind. These men
have left no successors. The only
Melbourne citizen suspected of an
intention to shell out handsomely
some day is David Syme, the
millionaire, and he will probably
wait till he is dead, which, bar
accidents, will be a long wait.

Authoress Charlotte Yonge, who died
lately in England, notwithstanding that she de-
voted m-ieh of her early life to missionary work in
Australia and the South Seas, found herself prac-
tically forgotten by Australians when a couple of
years ago an illuminated address was presented to
her at Winchester, at the foundation of the Char-
lotte Yonge scholarship. A few S. Australians
alone remembered her.

James Howard, of Edi(Vic ), who attained
his 100th year on 9th March, danced a hornpipe
at a gathering of friends who presented -him with
a silver-headed walking-stick in honor of the
occasion. He arrived in Victoria from England
in ’29, afterwards went to Sydney, and returned
to Vic. overland in ’45, in the employ of Messrs.
Faithfull, squatters in Oxley district. His chief
occupations were those of-shepherd and miner.
N.S.W. Full Court takes a long time to
make up its mind about the case of that very
eminent Gent One, which looks to The Bulletin
one of the most simple matters ever submitted to
any tribunal. The whole point at issue, so far as
one can see, is—Do two (or forty) blacks make one
white ?
A Bulletin correspondent meets a
veteran :
Genial, honest old Beyers, ex-M.L.A. for Mudgee,
N.S.W., who with his partner (Holtermann) took a ton or
two of gold out of Tambaroora in the early 70’s, is here
(Mount Higgins, W.A.) working a show which looks well,
and yielded at last crushing 31dwt. per ton. It was a
godsend to the poor old man, who had been battling
desperately with evil days. Had a long yarn with him ;
he says he made a great mistake when he gave up poli-
tics. He still reckons on returning to Hill E»d and open-
ing up Hawkins’ Hill. Hope burns eternal.
Owen Cunliffe, who knows a good deal
about Edward VII, writes in New York Inde-
pendent :
It is true that he has been connected with a divorce
case, to which I only refer here because it is so frequently
cited by his critics as constituting an illustration of his
moral shortcomings. But those who know King Edward
best, and are acquainted with the ins and outs of London
society, are aware that the sole basis of the charge against
him in connection with Lady Mordaunt’s divorce was the
fact that no one else was admitted to her apartments
when on three occasions he went to pay her afternoon
calls at the Alexandra Hotel in London, where she was
openly staying at the time. The majority of people
fail to remember that the laws of etiquette governing
European Courts and society provide that during a royal
call the doors of the person thus honoured shall be kept
closed to all visitors of minor rank. When falsely accused,
he could have availed himself of his prerogatives as heir
to the throne to decline to take any notice of the affair.
But, moved by a sense of chivalrous sympathy for
the poor woman whose brain had given way before the
case came before the court, he went of his own accord
into the witness-box in order to swear to her innocence.
Even a bishop can’t please everyone.
“ Anti-Humbug ” to The Bulletin
The first Pastoral letter of the R.C. Bishop of Goul-
burn is remarkable for the slobber with which he
belauds his defunct predecessor. The new
Bishop has a penchant for hyperbole and for
turning on the oratorical tap, which goes
far with the common crowd. Hence little
reliance can be placed on his biographical
or historical statements. Everyone who
knew the late Bishop is perfectly aware
that he had little brains, but a keen con-
sciousness of his power as Bishop, often
using it unscrupulously and with a ven-
geance against his priests. A glance at
the “ Ecclesiastical Directory ” for the
years of his Episcopate amply
proves this. It is said “Nero
fiddled whilst Rome
burned;” the old Bishop
could eat his dinner
with a gusto after
sending some priest
out on the cold
world by his man-
date, and without
assigning a reason.
His whole Canon
Law was summed up
in the dictum, Sic
volo , sic jubeo. I)r.
Lanigan had a fond-
ness for f "!sh faces,
and to be a favorite
for a time meant
ruin in the hereafter.
No one knows this
better than the pre-
sent Bishop, who at
one time got a touch
of the episcopal staff.
Dr. Gallagher would
do more good to the
soul of his departed
friend if he set about
repairing some of the
wrongs his predeces-
sor committed, or
sought out the lost
sheep he had driven
from the fold. Such
action would show
that the Bishop
realised his respons-
ibilities and obliga-
tions, and would be
more in harmony
with his position
than indulging- in
unseemly laudation
of a man whom, at one
period of his life, he
surely must not have
worshipped.
Queen Victoria,
though the English
ac facto monarch
with the longest reign,
does not hold the re-
rd for titular monarchs.
The Old Pretender was, by right of birth, James
111. of England from 1701 to 17GG.
The fact that J. T. Walker topped the poll
for the Senate in N.S.W., thus winning the blue
riband of the Australian election, was one of the
surprises of the contest. As a politician and
party man he is unknown in comparison with
men like R. E. O’Connor and E. F. Milieu. The
reason of his success is, first, his daily-press
booming ; and, second, the fact that the Bank of
N.S.W. throughout the State (and probably also
the A.M.P. Society; worked steadily and solidly
for him.
At his final meeting, when the amusing
T. Eldridge Healey, who opposed Treasurer
Turner for a week, bade a regretful farewell to his
proposed constituents —he had been thrown over
by both the Argus and the Freetrade League—
the most pained spectator of the proceedings was
the pet-dog of this last of the Victorian Free-
traders. The animal—probably a twin-brother
of Reid’s tyke—sat on a chair and howled, no
doubt because he wasn’t to be thrown into the
water to swim, or sink.
George Reid, who handled the Billie
Barlow libel case for The Bulletin with so much
humour and such consummate tact, was “dis-
covered ” as an advocate late in life—he is now
nearer 60 than 50. He has built up, or begun to
build up, a decent little Bar practice just when he
is called away to Melbourne to fight the battle of
Calico Jemmy and his other admirers. If he
stuck to his law business, which is now attaining
considerable dimensions, he might possibly gather
a little money for his old age—the world knows,
on his own authority, that so far his purse is
slender indeed. Reid is Calico Jemmy’s one effec-
tive champion—without him the Freetrade cause
would indeed be a dismal one. So if Calico
Jemmy has any gratitude (of the kind which not
only recognises past favors but is ready to pay for
favors to come) he will put up a purse of £lO,OOO
or so with trustees for the benefit of Georgey-
Porgey instead of waiting for the inevitable time
when' the man of jokes and wondrously-distorted
figures falls down exhausted in his tracks, as the
other revenue-tariff champions have fallen before
him.
A melancholy example of malicious page
is the attack made by John Norton—squirming in
the agony of his defeat for the Senate —on Edmund
Barton in Sunday’s Truth. Barton, in warning
the Federal electors to exclude “ vileness ” from
the Senate, pointed at Norton (so the latter de-
clares) and no doubt deprived him of many votes.
As things stand, Norton, standing solely “ on his
own,” and without a single good word for him in
any daily paper, polled the enormous total of (say)
61,559 votes as against (say) 65,622 votes scored
by the lowest successful candidate. For many of
these votes Norton’s vigorous, not to say reckless
oratory in his extensive personal campaign is re-
sponsible ; but most of them were doubtless the
outcome of the gigantic and far-reaching personal
advts. gained per medium of his widely-circulated
weekly paper. A fine large moral here protrudes
for the benefit of the N.S.W. Federal-Protection-
ists, who have no daily paper. The Bulletin
doesn’t count the skin-shedding Star, which is
controlled by certain obvious non-public influences,
and so disgraced itself during the Federal cam-
paign that no sane person now ever takes any
serious notice of its alleged opinions.

Aboriginalities.
A curious death ! On Woodford station,
Vic., the boss had a fine Newfoundland dog, of
apparently very small appetite, yet always sleek
and fat. He was an animal of many privileges—
boasting a handsome silver-mounted collar.
Sheep were getting killed by a dog or dogs on
various parts of the run, and it was suggested that
the Newfoundland “ had a tooth in it,” but the
idea was scouted by the boss. One morning,
however, the dog was missing, and nothing was
seen of him till some months afterwards, when he
was found with a ram’s horns firmly locked in his
collar —both animals dead.

“Cuss” : A kangaroo-shooter on Whifc-
tarranah Station (N.S.W.) has a moke that, during
present awful drought, turns up regularly three
times a day for a feed of , the boiled ’roo
that the shooter keeps himself and family
fat and hearty on. Also, years ago, at the same
place, a carrier named Dickens— not “ dicken ”
had a mare that he fed regularly on boiled mutton,
as being cheaper than chaff —at the high carriage-
rates then prevailing.

“Frank Hennidon ’’ sends to The Bulle-
tin from Wangaratta, Vic., this queer story
Travelling in a buggy from a place called M'Mahon’s
Heef in N.S.W. with an erstwhile telegraph operator
named B ,we were forced by a storm to take refuge
in an old house on the road-side. While seated on a box
in the house my companion dozed off but presently sprang
to his feet and excitedly declared that he could hear a
telegraph instrument clicking in the room. He was
deadly pale and I felt not a little dismayed at such a
strange statement. Several minutes passed without any-
thing more being said, then he slowly drawled out :
“ Telegram to B. B , Benalla, Victoria: You have
drawn in Newmarket Handicap. Accept con-
gratulations. (Signed) E. B , Hay.” After this
B—— seemed to wake out of the trance or sleep into
which he had fallen and had no knowledge of the
incident until I acquainted him with it, whereupon it re-
turned to his memory like a flash. He said it must have
been a dream and added that he had never been in
Benalla in his life, nor was he ever likely to be. The inci-
dent was forgotten by us both until the latter end of last
month. B had been visiting friends in Melbourne,
and on his return to N.S.W. he missed the train at
Benalla. Wiring to his mother advising her of the fact he
in return received a telegram from her couched in exact’y
the same words as that which he had received from the
Unknown on the occasion above described two years
before. It seems he had, before leaving home, bought a
ticket in the sweep, and the result coming to hand before
his return, his mother telegraphed him as stated. B
regards the occurrence as almost too uncanny to talk
about.

“ H.S.” : A peculiar people are the
Badjus who occasionally visit the northern shores
SELF-EVIDENT.
THE DOG: “ What’s that! Lucky dog? Well, I am not
Reid’s Dry Dog, anyhow."
Photo, by T. H. Stoward, Adelaide.
of Australia (about Port Essington and Bowen
Straits) in March and April. They have appa-
rently no fixed abode—living almost entirely on
their prahus, and come to Australia for tortoise-
shell. Their food, which they carry with them,
consists chiefly of rice, sugar, and poultry. They
leave about May. Not nearly so many of those
people, however, come over as of the trepang-
seeking Malays, who have often on the coast as
many as 30 prahus at a time—some nearly of 100
tons capacity —manned in all by probably 1000
men. These take away annually nearly 1000 tons
of trepang (the edible sea-slug), worth from £9O to
£lOO per ton at first-hand, but very much more
valuable in the ultimate market—China.
At a Q. bush shanty years
ago, where preparations for a
big dance were well advanced,
a teamster got jammed between
a tree and the wheel of his own
dray, and was carried into his
shanty in terrible agony.
Shanty - keeper regarded his
writhing form with anxiety.
The dance was coming off that
night, and he couldn’t accom-
modate “ the cove” with a room
just to die in if he was going to
take long. The kindly S.K.
went and brought the J.P.
(neighbouring squatter) to look
at the invalid and hurry him on.
The poor fellow’s breathing
alone would have softened the
heart of the devil himself.
“Oh, get the ink and paper,”
said the justice. Procured
eagerly, and in five minutes the
inquest was finished, and the
J.P. off again after some rams.
Next couple of hours the dealer
in poison occupied himself pay-
ing short and frequent visits to
the bedside. “.Right you are,
Billy,” he yelled at last, and
a gravy-eyed groom brought a
horse and cart, which lmd been
in readiness some time, and re-
moved the corpse to its hole-
just in time.
Early in ’99 a company of
mining men bought a battery at
Croydon (N.Q.) for use at their
mine at far-distant Woolgar.
They started to move it per
horse-team, and it is still in pro-
cess of moving. Three times it
has been abandoned, and thrice the whole team
has died of thirst. Nice place, Queensland !

“W.O.W, Apropos losses by drought
on N.W. Queensland cattle stations (B. 2/3/’01).
On one big station in W.Q. two years ago, 250,000
sheep were mustered for shearing ; now they can
only find the living skeletons of about 60,000.
Another station has about 100,000 left out of
300,000, and yet another shore last shearing 80,000
remaining out of about 450,000. The drought,
too, has apparently come to stay.

* * * •
There was a learned dissertation t’other
day in a country court by an interpreter on the
virtue of various Chinese oaths. Blowing out a
match, it appears, simply means that John will
have no light in the dim Hereafter. Breaking a
saucer counts for nothing. Cutting the head off a
rooster, either in court or in the cemetery, how-
ever, is a method of recognised efficacy. Perjury
after this ceremony entails misfortune in this life
and a dreadful time in the next. By-the-way,
writer once heard three Chinamen deliberately
swear to the most damnable lies by way of putting
a countryman, to whom they owed money, out of
the way—on a charge of murder. And there was
a rooster hopping about outside with his head
chopped off, nevertheless.
THE GREAT SYDNEY DIVORGE-CASE.
Mr. A. CONINGHAM Mrs. A. C. CONINGHAM.
Photos. from Sydney NEWSLETTER.
A selector at Grenfell (N.S.W.) recently
trapped on 240 acres, in 18 nights, 18,791 rabbits.
On Eurabba have been slain 100,000 bunnies, and
the local stench of defunct rabbit is overpowering.
“ Deeby ”: In many bush
towns there exists a conspiracy
of cruelty to dumb animals.
As I write, a waggon is pulled
up opposite, harnessed to which
is a team of more-or-less crip-
pled horses. Three have raw
shoulders; these are mares with
foals, the latter all lame
through walking over metalled
roads. The near-leader has a
chafed side, in which the hot
chains rub all day; also, one
of the shatters has a sore back.
These animals have been stand-
ing in the hot sun, tormented
by swarms of flies, while their
owner sits skiting on the local
pub. verandah. Yet this pic-
ture of misery excites no pity
or comment from an) one.
Further down the street I
heard a man laying the whip-
handle on to a_ worn-out,
starved-looking horse, har-
nessed to a heavily-loaded
dray. This man systematically
ill-treats all his horses ; no one
dreams of remonstrating. An-
other local carrier has a habit
of “touching up” his horses
with a long-handled shovel ;
hacking them sometimes in a
barbarous manner. In this in-
stance a man once laid an in-
formation with the local con-
stable. He was a stranger and
unaware of the conspiracy, and
liothing resulted from it be-
yond the fact that he was so

In another instance of gross
cruelty I myself ventured to
mildly rebuke the biute. Re-
garding me with blank aston-
ishment, he curtly told me to
mind my own blanky business.
They were his horses. As the
fellow was in a position to
deprive me of my employment, I let the matter
drop at that. But some day, when able to
quit this elysian retreat, I will give some of
these callous brutes the greatest surprise they
probably ever received by laying an information
and seeing the case through—when the Police
Magistrate is on the Bench.

Down to date 93 bores for artesian water
have been sunk on Govt, account in N.S. W. The
most expensive was the Dolgelly bore, on the
Moree-Boggabilla road ; it was sunk 4086 ft., and
cost £10,630. The flow is equal to 745,200 gallons
a day ; the temperature of the water when it
reaches surface is 130 deg. Fahr. Next most ex-
pensive was Tulloona bore, same track, 3537 ft.,
£9768 ; 783,000ga15. But the department’s ex-
perience hasn’t always been so satisfactory. The
Bancannia bore, on the Silverton-Cobham track,
cost £8535 (depth 3615 ft.), and then failed utterly.
The biggest flow is from the Kenmare bore,
between Bourke and Hungerford ; though it is
only 1540 ft. deep, the daily yield is 2,050,000 gal-
lons—or would be if it weren’t controlled. The
highest temperature of water is at the Clifton and
Tinaroo bores, between Milparinka and Wanaar-
ing ; it is normally 139 deg., and varies but little.

8., 2/2/’l). Some years ago, when Richard

“ C.A..J.” : Be par. (2/2/’01) about trading
off blind horses. I was had myself once with an
apparently good seeing moke. Prior to my buy-
ing him the owner had ridden him hard across a
paddock, and then jumped a 4ft. fence with him.
This I saw myself, and, as lie was a decent-look-
ing beast otherwise, I bought him for £7. Took
him home and hitched him to a post outside my
gate. I had just got inside when the wind banged
the gate, which I had left open ; and hearing a
galloping noise, I ran out, and saw my new steed
careering down the road, until he belted fair into
a horse and dray. Then, when he made a bee-line
for a steep gully, I knew he must be blind, and I
closed my eyes and thought of my £7. But he
survived the fall, and the next time I saw him he
was leaning up against an old traction-engine that
no other horse in the district would go near.

“ T.G.F.” : Be A.C.H.’s cat-sagacity yarn
(B. 16/2/’01) knew an old hut-keeper on the Paroo
who told me that he had a cat once that was
afflicted with a most unquenchable thirst, and was
always helping herself to the water out of his
bucket. No matter how often he filled the vessel
the cat would empty it. This so enraged the old
man that at last he put the cat into a bag, with
half-a-dozen bricks, tied the neck of the bag
securely, dumped the lot into the creek, which
was about 10ft. deep, and went to work. On re-
turning to the hut he found the dear old cat
drinking from the bucket as usual. Then the old
chap filled a tub to the brim with water, plunged
puss in, put a cover over the tub and sat on it for
a couple of hours. But when he lifted the cover
he found the tub empty and puss fast asleep at
the bottom ! There is no water in the dams or
creeks thereabouts now. People say it is the
drought, but a demented old hut-keeper says he
knows better —its the cat.

The people of Bowen (N.Q.) have
been lately much exercised over the Beck-
man-Anderson case. Beckman (often
called the Fisherman) was mates with An-
derson on a prospecting tour, and Ander-
son disappeared. After a while, Beck-
man, who seems to have been none too
strong in the head, showed up again and
told a rambling story of how Anderson
had been killed by a gun-accident as the
pair were going down a gully. Beckman
further wrote a wild, incoherent letter to
the papers explaining matters. I inally
the police took the matter up, and were
guided by Beckman, after many devia-
tions, to the spot where Anderson was
buried. The body was exhumed, and it
was discovered that the head had been-
severed from the body and broken into 16
pieces by some sharp instrument. No
sign of any gunshot wound. Beckman was
tried and found guilty. He presented a.
piteous and abject spectacle in court, and
gave a decided impression of semi-imbe-
cility to the spectators.

A goat persisted in trespassing on
the garden adjoining Northam (W.A.)
post-office, until one of the clerks, after
officially drawing the goat’s attention to
the regulations dealing with trespassers,
decided to shoot the animal. In order
to proceed regularly, he brought a friend
to witness the dead, and the goat was duly
immolated. Two days later the I.M..vx.
wired for an explanation, to which he got
reply “ I shot the goat within the mean-
ing of the Act.” The clerk was at once
banished to Lake Way, one of the red-
hottest places you can get to.

The R.T.Y.C. schooner Ariadne—recently
at Sydney, and now wrecked at Waitaki, M.L. —
was the largest (300 tons register) sailing yacht
afloat, and possibly also the speediest —with an
adequately powerful breeze. She once made the
trip from Southampton to London in a week, and
on another run was credited (with a favouring
current) with an average of 18 knots. Mr. T. C.
Kerry, the owner, states that the £lO,OOO insur-
ance on the vessel does not half cover her value.
She was beautifully built, mainly of oak and
teak, and, barring accident, would have been a
good ship half-a-century hence.
“DO A GIT.”
(Off the Land.)
When putting in your residence, way back upon
and the Blues, of course,
The drought is at its height and for years there’s
been no rains, „
You often feel inclined to Do a git.
When the tanks have all gone empty and the
creek is ten months dry,
Your only moke for better lands has quit,
Forced to carry muddy water, tasting strong of
alkali, . „
It’s very nearly time to Bo a git.
When you have to tick up mutton that has been
a week in soak, ...
Not fat enough to keep your slush-lamp lit,
You can’t afford the papers and your only pipe is
broke, _ .. „
It’s very nearly time to Bo a git.
When “rent-day’s” drawing nearer, on your
O.P. and C.L., ...
And the mortgagees for interest want their bit,
You haven’t got a red cent and not a hoof to sell,
It’s very nearly time to “ Bo a git.
When the ants are in the sugar-bag and you re
running short of tea,
The flour is full of weevils, dirt and grit ,
Your dog has gone and left you, and you start to
yarn to trees, .. „
The best thing you can do is Bo a git.
SANDY.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

More Colliery Trouble.
A MINER COMPELLED TO LEAVE.
“ This is the true story of how I came to leave
the old Bulli colliery,” said Mr. Robert Spinks,
timber contractor and orchardist, Bulli, to a re-
porter who wished for some particulars of trouble,
hitherto unreported, for insertion in the columns
of the Australian Star. “ I had been a miner
there for years, had done my work well, and had
given no trouble to men or masters. But I
became Afflicted with sciatica. Hot, shooting
pains darted through my hip, I could not
sleep at night, and had to lay up until the
attack eased off. The disease often numbed my
left leg, and it pained me intensely to stretch
it. I also suffered so severely from indigestion,
sick headaches, biliousness, and heart palpitation
that I was obliged to leave the colliery. Subse-
quently I became a timber contractor and carter,
but this occupation caused the sciatica to spread
to my wrist joint and right knee; sometimes I
was unable to work for days. Four years ago I
read how beneficial Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills for
Pale People were in such cases, and I began taking
them. I soon improved, the pain grew less, and
I ate and slept better. By continuing their use
the sciatica, indigestion, and heart palpitation all
left me, and I can now work as vigorously at fell-
ing trees and carting timber as I could 20 years
ago. Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills also cured my wife
of heart and kidney troubles.”
Coal miners and manual laborers are specially
liable to sciatica, which may be caused by ex-
posure, overwork, or nerve injury. Impoverished
and vitiated blood also assists in its development.
Therefore, to cure sciatica the nerves must be
toned up and the blood enriched. This is just
what Dr. Williams’ pink pills do. Thus, too,
they have cured not only sciatica, but such
nervous disorders St. Vitus’ dance, epilepsy,
hysterical fits, nervous exhaustion, paralysis,
locomotor ataxia, etq. ; whilst by enriching and
purifying the blood they cure anaemia, debility,
indigestion, skin diseases, consumption, colds,
etc. Sold by chemists and storekeepers, and by
the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s
Place, Sydney, two and nine per box, six boxes
fifteen and three, post free. “How to cure
Sciatica ; ” write for special information.*

SYDNEY.


One of the text-books set for second-class
N.S.W teachers is not obtainable in Sydney and
inquirers are referred by authority to a firm which
expects early arrival of said book by English mail.
Complaints are numerous amongst intending
examinees and the fact that all are referred to the
same firm is much commented on.
Writes “ Veteran ” *
“A Mon Prae Scotland,” in taking up the cudgels (B.
17/3/01) for the Highlanders is both right and wrong ;
but not nearly so wrong as his opponent. It is not the
courage of the individual men in the ranks that deter-
mines the fighting value of regiments ; but the discipline,
training, and traditions of the regiments as fighting
machines. The winning of the V.C’s. by men of a cer-
tain regiment is no criterion of the regiment’s collective
fighting value. The colonel of a rifle-battalion in the
Crimea, when asked to recommend 16 or 20 men for the
V.C., said, “I cannot single out individuals. No officer
or man of the battalion ever failed to do all that could
he expected of him.” No soldier, as Gordon said, can do
more than his duty, and to single out one among many
for special recognition implies that all the rest have
failed to do their duty. Highlanders, Guards, Marines
are regarded as corps d’ Hite, and they try, as most men
with reputations do, to live up thereto. Also any com-
mander who knows his business can turn any set of
men into a corps d’ Hite.
Melb. R.C. authorities have bought 30
acres of land near Broadmeadows (Vie.), and.are
sending the hat round for a Foundling Hospital
to place thereon.
The House of Commons has borrowed one
practice from the Leg. Assembly of Victoria.
Formerly if a Commons member wanted to reserve
a seat * for himself he used to put his
hat on it early. Some members used to keep
hats at the House for the purpose. The Victorian
practice has always been to pin a card or ticket on
the seat reserved. A Vic. Parliamentarian
Duffy ?—suggested the ticket _ system to tne
Speaker of the Commons, and it is now an estab-
lished custom.
“H.V.D.” : A grimy little boy about six
was howling at a terrible rate the other day in
Pitt-street, Sydney. As I passed, a woman in
front of me stopped and asked him what was tne
matter. He was standing in the gutter and said
lie had dropped his penny down a grating. »ne
told him not to mind, and gave him another
penny. As I saw the same child, a. day or two
later, howling over a grating in King-street, I
have no doubt he found it a paying game. It is a
very old game, too.

POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S.W. Lands Dept, has on its hands
over 5£ million acres of country once held under
lease but now abandoned and yielding no revenue
whatever.
Melbourne unemployed recently passed
resolutions of confidence in the Hekald, of half-
ditto in the Age, and of no confidence at all in the
Arcus. Which sums up the situation to a nicety.
If Federation does naught else, it will
pare down ruthlessly the verbose O.H.M.S. wires
that have hitherto blocked the paid business in
the various States. Each dept, will now have to
pay for its own messages. In Q. alone the “ free”
business amounted to about £50,000 a year.
Hoyle, of the swollen head, by dividing
the Protectionist vote at South Sydney, thus de-
feating M‘Gowan and securing the seat for Free-
trader Edwards, who otherwise would have been
simply dog-licked, has earned for himself the un-
dying gratitude of the Protectionist party.
One thing is clear : the Commonwealth
must adopt the English ballot-paper, and enjoin
voters to affix crosses to the names of selected
candidates. To cross out 54 names (as in voting
for N.S.W. senators) with a blunt stub of blue
pencil is a waste of time, temper, and pencils.
In the N.S.W. Federal country-electorates
the producers were with the Protectionists all the
time ; the middlemen and their dependents in the
chief town of a constituency often decided the
contest in favor of Freetrade. As witness Goul-
burn, which beat Rose and practically elected
Conroy.
It is a great pity (from the Democratic
standpoint) that “Billy” Browne, leader of the
y. Labor Opposition, was not induced to run
either for the House of Representatives or for the
Senate. Browne is a shrewd, able man, respected
by the public at large, and very popular in the
ranks of his own party.
Barton’s note to Chamberlain on the New
Hebrides question is the first Commonwealth

hardly any more recruits for S’Africa are coming

Thursday Island was powerful enough to put in a
little brown man at the head of the hospital com-
mittee. A fortnight ago a certain local election
in a small northern town was carried by an en-
terprising candidate who, in place of advertising
and addressing the electors, simply went round

A census of State and Sunday scholars
by the Vic. Govt, shows the bogus-
ness of the cry for the Bible in State schools. Of
children under seven, 73 per cent., and of those
®,° pe f. cen h regularly attend Sunday.
too And r as the °, dd 2 ,° per cent - probably live
too far away from a church of their own particular
denomination to go regularly, it is pretty evident
that the parsonical cry about “Godless education”
is mostly moonshine.
Several M.L. M.'sP. declare that they are
against federation, “because it will take away
our right of self-government.” These same
gentlemen a few months back, declared that they
were proud to belong to the first State Parliament
that passed the Colonial Securities Act, a measure
expressly giving the British Government most ex-
tensive powers to veto Maoriland legislation in
tne interests or supposed interests of- British
bond-holders.
The duel between Melb. Age and Akgus
in their appeals for particular candidates resulted
in favor of David Syme. Of the six successful
Senators, two (Simon Praser and Zeal) were
selected by both papers, and naturally polled well
Sargood was an Akgus selection. But three of
the Ages men were returned, while Dow its
other candidate, was seventh. The Akgus
triumph was the return of Knox, for Kooyong a
suburban constituency.
A clause was inserted in the Q. Amended
Sugar Works Guarantee Act, prohibiting any
coloured alien from working in a State Guarantee
sugar-mill. It now turns out that, owing to a
treaty previously arranged through the British
D° Vt - Wlth Japan, clause is invalid. How
p blip Govt, must have chuckled when they
allowed the Labor-party to insert it! The Q.
Centra] Mills, established by Govt, money in the
interest of small cane-farmers, are importing
coloured labor right and left.
The election resolved itself into another
fight between Sydney and Australia. Of thelsanti-
Bartonites returned in N.S.W. 11 come from the
county of Cumberland, while B. R. Wise’s defeat
in Canoblas was mainly due to the virulent spite
and opposition fostered in Sydney. Only three
other seats—Macquarie, "Robertson'; and Barrier—
were won in the country by Reidites, and the
last-named was merely a local affair, in which the
fact that Josiah Thomas has freetrade leanings
was pure bad luck for the Barton Govt.
Some of the Canoblas electors no doubt
still consider they did a smart thing in rejecting
Wise and electing Brown. But human nature
ever asserts itself, and you can’t now expect the
Barton lans, smarting under the defeat of their
nominee, the most ready-witted debater in all
Australasia, to be particularly enthusiastic about
the claims of Canoblas as a site for the Federal
capital. To say nothing about Bombala, Yass
will suit the Bartoniahs just as well as Canobla.s—
and it will suit the Melbourneites, Tasmanians
and S. Australians a good deal better

POLITICS IN KIDDUM-STREET.
THE RAT: 'Now then, Sparrow, crawl off. Lammel let’s ’ave a White
Govt. To have had any chancfe at all Reid must
have carried his own State from end to end, and
have entered the House of Representatives with
a thumping N.S.W. majority instead of a bare
four or so. With Holder provided for as
Speaker, there is no one from the smaller States
returned as an oppositionist capable of taking an
Australian portfolio. As a matter of fact most of
the low-tariffists are anything but Reidites. Reid,
Bruce Smith, M'Millan, and the rest of the
Sydney softgoods party can’t run an Australian
Ministry, even with the help of Brown
Edwards, M‘Lean, and Whelks—or is it Wilks ?’
The Victorian I ederal campaign has adver-
tised a flaw in the suffrage, and convinced the
electors that, under a perfectly democratic law,
the voter would be bound down to one constitu-
ency. Whilst a man has the choice of voting in
two constituencies, the one-man-one-vote principle
is often infringed. In the case of Melbourne
1 orts, for instance, the candidate was returned
without opposition, and scores of electors, having
practically elected one member, were left free to
carry their votes into the city, and do their best
to elect another. When a candidate is given a
walk-over, it must be assumed that every elector
in the constituency has voted for him, and to
permit any of them to vote again in another con-
stituency is to uphold dual voting plain and
simple.
Now that the Federal vote has been cast
and the people’s hands are tied for three years,
whispers of things to come are beginning to clothe
themselves in loud speech. Barton is counted
safe on the titles question, and the Duke’s visit is
looked-to to put handles to scores of names. In
his Federal address Barton said : “The time has
passed when the approval of the Sovereign and
the bestowal of a decoration were considered in
our democratic community as the mark of the
beast. ’ Hitherto Barton has kept himself apart
from pseudo-dignities ; but those words are taken
to be his recognition of the right of Australians
who nlay at politics to Imperial gewgaws. The
trouble is that to win Imperial favors Imperial
services must be rendered and an Imperial spirit
cultivated. This is where Australian interests go
to the wall.
In the whirlwind that is presently going
to knock the N.S.W. State Ministrv off its perch
Lands Minister Hassall may possibly have as bad
a time as tiie rest. Some remarkable things are
alleged regarding the administration of his depart-
ment. In one case (the facts were sworn to before
the Pastoral Lands Commission) an application
was made that land which had been “attached ”
to a pastoral lease should be made available for
selection, according to the conditions on which it
was “attached.” The Minister sent the matter to
the local Land Board for report; and, after hear-
ing the evidence of intending selectors, the board
recommended that the land should be thrown
open. The departmental officers also recom-
mended that course. That was nearly two years
ago, and the squatter still holds the country.
Meantime, the intending selectors, sick of waiting,
have gone elsewhere—some of them to Victoria.
Alex. Peacock, the democratic Victorian
Premier, and Bill Trenwith, the socialistic Chief
Secretary, must be feeling 1 awkward re the Govt,
celebrations committee for the reception of the
Yorks. How ever are they going to justify to
the people the squandering of £lOO,OOO on the
entertainment of a couple of Royal nonentities ?
Think of the gap in the finances at the end of
the year that £103,000 would have filled ! Also
think of the increases that could have been given
to the men in the lower-paid branches of the
civil service with that sum. Then if Alex.
Peacock and Bill Trenwith want to live up to the
grovel which they have let loose on the community
they will have to get into Windsor uniform. If
that is not enough to end their political careers then
the democratic spirit in Victoria is dead indeed.
Here you have a Democratic Government(in Wind-
sor uniform) spending thousands on tinsel and
flummery while the educational vote is cut to the
bare bone, and while the Schools of Mines and
technical educational institutions are rendered
useless for the want of an extra few hundred
pounds to buy apparatus.

The Cremation Society of N.S W made
a start recently with a meeting at its rooms, 82
King-street, Sydney. The stated “Object of the
Society is to substitute for inhumation some
method of corpse-disposal “which shall rapidly
resolve the body into its component elements in a
manner which cannot offend the living, and shall
render the remains absolutely innocuous—crema-
tion being at present the best available means to
such end. Any information on the subject can
be obtained from the secretary, Mr. A. H. New-
man, at the above-mentioned address.
Remarks from a Barrier miner serving
Teuth C (l5/3/’1 ®™ bant s Horse > printed in Barrier
We are supposed to get five biscuits, a pound of meat,
af Bu B' ar » and a quarter oz. of tea as a day's supply.
For 18 days we had only three biscuits a day 7, and no tea
or sugar. My Christmas dinner was one biscuit, some
maize I ground up myself, and four hours’ fighting VVe
are on the move sometimes 18 hours a day. We are
v, r th „ ree , months I have not slept under
cover, and I have been wet for days at a time. The man
who comes here soldiering is a fool. As for work,
m? e „ re n ° ne ’ and . Wlll he none for another 12 months.
The poverty here is really appalling. You can buy a
woman for a tin of jam, and .many good women have
Thl r l U m^ Se their virtue to feed their children,
these are Dutch women whose husbands are in Ceylon or
prisoners elsewhere. J
This surely can t be the kind of thing extolled by
bhiny-billy Inglis in his verses recited at N.S.W.
.Highland SociGty , s concert, telling how
Our brave Bushmen once again the flag unfurled,
And splendidly, beneath the sultry Afric sun,
Compelled the admiration of a wondering world
Honor our brave—returning battle-worn—
Our annals to remotest times shall tell
POW |)orne ” hail of death” our victor flag was
And how Australia’s choicest spirits fought and fell.
I ought and fell to make “ the world wonder ”
among other tilings, at the spectacle of women
selling themselves to buy their children bread !


VITADATIO.
HOW IT WAS DISCOVERED.
It is frequently a matter of wonder upon the part
of the public how particular remedies, classified
generally under the title of Patent Medicines,
have been discovered. It is well known that this
has more often been effected by chance than by
the more patient analysation of the properties of
herbs. And let not the reader of this article sneer
at the use of the word “Chance.” It would be
extremely difficult to say how far this same
“ chance ” has been instrumental in the discovery
of much that goes on in the scientific world,
despite the dictum of the Philosopher that what
is discovered by chance is seldom of any value.
Chance, we are told, led to the discovery of the
Rontgen Rays in Photography, and it certainly
led to the no less important discovery of Vita-
datio. Under what circumstances this actually
took place in the first instance is, and will ever
remain, a mystery, but the events which led up
to its wonderful properties becoming known and
being presented to the public are too fresh in the
memories of its proprietors to be speedily for-
gotten. A certain gentleman in Launceston,
Tasmania, found himself one day under an
obligation to a gentlem .n of that town, and
offered to place him in possession of a secret
which he had long held. This secret, he declared,
had enabled him for many years to cure his friends
and acquaintances of long-standing diseases, and
had kept him also in the best of health up to what
was a ripe old age. Eventually the secret of a
peculiar blend of certain herbs was revealed, and
the medicine put to a severe practical test, which
test, it may be stated, resulted in the most extra-
ordinary success.
From being the secret fad of this amiable old
enthusiast Vitadatio gradually spread to becoming
the popular remedy of a large number of people
in Tasmania, until, at length, the demand becom-
ing so great, business negotiations had to be en-
tered upon for an enormous supply to satisfy the
wants of its ever increasing number of friends.
From Tasmania it spread to New Zealand, where
it quickly became in such great demand that a
central depot for its distribution had to be estab-
lished. Its history in Australia is almost too new
to need recapitulation here. From a modest start
about three years ago it quickly caught the public
taste, disease after disease was conquered by its
peculiar properties, and it was evident from but
six months working that Yitadatio had come to
stay. The public are doubtless well accustomed
to hearing of cures being performed by some
patent medicine after the medical faculty has de-
spaired of success. But these cures appear chiefly
in the advertisement columns of the daily news-
papers, and will but seldom bear the investi-
gation which a little publicity and enquiry makes.
Vitadatio, however, has this advantage in its
financial and commercial arrangements, that almost
everyone connected with it, from its energetic
Australasian Distributor at its head down through
all the depannents to the lowest office, all are able
to testify what this wonderful medicine has done
for them.- Mr. Palmer, who is the Sole Distributor
for Australasia, will show incontestible proof that
he has been cured by Vitadatio, and by nothing
else from this scourge: known as Hydatids, and
this after his case had been given up by all the
medical men whom lie consulted, and after under-
going a painful operation which was quite ineffec-
tual, Yet after a course of this medicine not only
was he completely cured but he was accepted by a
leading Life Insurance Company as a first-class
life at their ordinai'y rates. A similar story can
be told by others now employed by Mr. Palmer at
his many branch establishments for the distribu-
tion of this medicine, so that it is quite easy for
the public to satisfy themselves that all the cures
claimed can be substantiated. One peculiarity of
Vitadatio is to be found in the fact that the
various herbs of which it is composed of grow in
Tasmania only , as far as is yet known, and are
indeed peculiar to that healthy little island. It is
perhaps in this fact that the reason may be found
why Vitadatio has not been discovered before in
this very old planet of ours, where, it is stated,
there is nothing new under the sun. Tasmania
is about as old as Sydney, that is, about 100 years,
since our settlers have occupied it, so that it is not
surprising that this remedy has only within the
past few years been discovered, An advantage
to our colonial population in purchasing this
medicine lies in the fact that the manufacture and
distribution of Vitadatio employs many hundreds
of people in all parts of the colonies, and that the
money spent by the public does not have to be sent
to other places, but stays here. This, of course, is
but a minor point in comparison with the far
more important fact that, no matter what your
disease may be, Vitadatio has proved itself to be
worth a trial. This is the main fact which
justifies the confidence all people feel who have
once given it a trial, and far outweighs any other
argument which may be used in its behalf.
For further particulars,
S. A . PALMER ,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney.

Legal Notices.
OTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that a
Petition has been presented under “ The
Presumption of Life Limitation (Scotland) Act,
1891 ” to the LORDS of COUNCIL and
SESSION in SCOTLAND (First Division—Lord
Stormonth-Darling, Ordinary ; Mr. M'Caul,
Clerk) at the instance of Mrs. HELEN SPEIRS
or HOOD, residing at Devonport Place, Millport,
widow of Archibald Mason Hood, Butcher, Glas-
gow ; Mrs. MARGARET SPEIRS or REID,
residing at No. 96 Clyde Road, West Didsbury,
Manchester, widow of John Reid, Millstone,
Builder, Glasgow ; and ALLAN SPEIRS, Baker,
Millport ; setting forth that their brother,
ANDREW SPEIRS, Junior, son of the deceased
Andrew Speirs, sometime Baker, afterwards re-
siding in Millport, and of Mrs. Helen Gibb or
Speirs, his wife, was born in Millport on 10th
January, 1849 ; that after he grew up he was in a
situation in Glasgow ; that subsequently —about
1874 —he went to London, but almost immediately
proceeded to Christchurch, Canterbury, New
Zealand, or the neighbourhood thereof, but did
not remain there as he was employed after-
wards in a store in Apia, Samoa, and there-
after had employment in the Caroline Islands,
and latterly in Melbourne, where he was in
1890 ; and that some months thereafter he
was last heard of by letter from Sydney or
Melbourne saying he was there. In these cir-
cumstances the said Petitioners ask Decree that
the said ANDREW SPEIRS, Junior, has dis-
appeared and that it should be found that in or
about December, 1890, was the date on which he
was last known to be alive, and to find that he
died in or about December, 1897, or if not, to find
that the said ANDREW SPIERS, Junior, must
be presumed to have died exactly seven years
after the date on which he was last known to be
alive, and to find therefore that the said
ANDREW SPEIRS, Junior, must be presumed
to have died in the year 1897. In the said
Petition the following Interlocutor has been pro-
nounced : —lsth February, 1901.—Lord Stor-
mouth-Darling—Act A.S.D. Thomson. The Lord
Ordinary appoints the Petition to be intimated,
advertised and served as craved, and allows all
parties interested to lodge answers, if so advised,
within three months after such intimation, ad-
vertisement and service.
(Signed) MOIR T. STORMONTH-DARLING.
Of all which intimation if hereby given.
MACRAE, FLETT & RENNIE, W.S.
Petitioners’ Agents.
57 Castle Street, Edinburgh,
15th February, 1901.

According to the just-issued N.S.W.
census-papers : “A Chinese is to be set down as
never married, unless lie has or has had a wne in
Australasia.”
He was a IHaorilander, a down-souther,
and all the energy of the Plutonic Isles, in his
case, had gone to thirst. He had, with Maori
foresight, invested his remaining cash m board
and lodging. Dodging the George-street tram on
his way home one day after a drouthy search for
“ a chance next week ” in the wool stores, his
thirst surprised even himself, and his tongue hung
down on his waistcoat and served for a watch-
pendant. Suddenly a man in a heavy suit of
Roslyn tweed crawled out from between two
trams and addressed him in a familiar tongue:
“Hae mon, d’ye ken the whurraboots o the
place th’ call th’ Marble Bar ; aam gae anxious to
see yon ! ” Dropping back into the lingo of his
own youth the dry one responded; “Dee 1 ken
it! Dinna fash yersel’, but just gang after me.
And he led to the Marble Bar at top speed. All
the way delightful visions of long, deep quarts
floated on in front through the haze, and disap-
peared round the corners till they vanished into
the haven of wet. They entered the Marble Bar.
The cool, placid .shimmer of its walls was like the
sound of a grand amen, and filled his soul with
peace, and while the stranger gazed round, and
touched the walls with* his finger, and gasped at
the, goddesses and nymphs, the thirsty one stood
drinking his long quart over and over again m the
great stomach of his mind. At last the stranger
cast round one sweeping look, and remarked in a,
whisper, “Mon, whusky is a verra wonderful
thing ! ” The dry man agreed with him, and tired
of anticipation and hankering for realisation, he
edged towards the bar. Again the stranger looked
round, and exclaimed: “Eh, but laddie, its a
rael monument, dunno ye think; ’aam rael prood
tae hae sin sic o’ place afore ah dee. We 11 just
gang the noo’ an’ see if thar’s ony ither remairk-
able public-hooses like this that ye ken ! Anri
he drifted out into the sweltering street and lett
his helpless compatriot so utter a wreck that he
tottered feebly into the lavatory and drank from
the tap.

a well and strikes a gold mine is no doubt a

store for Mrs. Harrison, Alexandria-terrace,

truth is found at the bottom of a well. In that
case, Mrs. Harrison went down a well, for she
undoubtedly grappled with the truth, and fortun-
ately for herself, struck something better than a
gold mine. What she gained was health. She
writes “ About six weeks ago I first tried Dr.
Morse s Indian Root Pills. I must state that I
found them a most superior medicine. My
complaint was _ peculiar to women, languor,
debility, and bilious attacks, which combined to
make my days perfectly wretched. Indeed, there
were spells when I used to wonder in the morning
how I could manage to set about my household
duties; but they had to be done, and I would
e through the day when I should have been
in bed. You can imagine by all this how agree-
ably surprised I was to get such a valuable
medicine, especially after the failure of so many
others. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have
made me vigorous and well. I noticed the change

The new M.L. owner of Melb. Cup-winner
Merriwee (Mr. E. J. Watt) is dabbling in the great horse
sport in no uncertain fashion. Rolling in wealth, he
can indulge his ardent, youthful passion to the top of
his bent. He fell into a common error lately, however
by asking stewards to hold an enquiry as to w'hv Nobility
should be given 8.2 in Wanganui Cup. Officials refused,
and next day Nobility smothered his field.

Apropos turf nomenclature. Someone has found
that there are fully 20 instances of four Australian na-s
owning the same name, and 30 more where three have
been similarly dubbed. Eleven strut under the appella-
tion of Jack, eight Starlight, seven Federation, seven
Larrikin, seven Ruby, six Doreen, six Daisy, six Lady-
smith, five Shamrock, five Mermaid, five Planet, five
Trilby, five Victory, five Vanity, five Chester, five Glen-
coe, and five Lyddite.
» « * *
Somewhere or other in this hemisphere t’other
day, a well-known co.’s secretary and accountant was
found, on audit, to be badly “ out ” in his cash balance.
He had used practically the whole of the shortage in
betting with bookmakers. The man (married) had no
money or property, and made no attempt to clear out or
conceal his deficiencies. He just waited till the fusty
auditor tripped over a deficit of about £lOOO.
The only offer the sorrowful embezzler could make his
astounded directors was quite seriously a £lOOO
double,” which, if it came-off, would cancel the short-
age. The first part of said “double” did come off but
as khe second event went ver3 r wide, the position is as it
was. The man still gnes about as perky as ever, however
expecting sympathy for his bad luck.
