Ranji s definition of a throw is: “ You use the
elbow as well as the shoulder and the wrist in propelling
the ball whereas you could only use the shoulder and
the wrist in bowling proper.”
* » » •
T other day in Adelaide Yankee wheelman
Martin clipped all Australian bests from sm. to the hour.
He got over 31m. 1425 yd. inside 60min. Previously none
had reached 30 miles. Before this Britisher Relph owned
the hour record (29m. 450 yd.), done on Sydney Cricket
Ground. The world’s speediest exceeds 40m., and belongs
to Stinson. Fact that Martin had motor-pacing, while
those who preceded him could get nothing better than
multicycles, was much in his favor. The Murkan’a figures
for sm. are 8.57 1-5, and for 10m., 17.59 J. Former is
11 l-ssec. and latter 44 4-ssec. less than those knocked
out.
* * • .
Saturday, at Adelaide Cycling Carnival, Martin
further showed his present wonderful form by cutting 1
4sec. off lvalue's Australasian mile record (lmin. 30 l-ssec.),
and brilliantly appropriating the final of Australasian
five-mile supremacy from F. O. Beauchamp and J. M.
Chapman.
* * * *
Up to date Yankee riders have won two of the
three Australasian cycling premierships decided. Lawson
got the mile and Martin the 5 miles. The 10 miles is the
only one uncontested, and this looks a fair thing for
Hugger Bill, who has won upwards of £lOOO prize money,
and knocked out all previous principal Australasian re-
cords. So much spoil never before came to one rider in
this section during a single season.

Re par. herein 13/3/’01: N.S.W. League of
Wheelmen got a nasty knock from the M.L. body, over
Mother-State representation in amateur events of coming
M. L. Championship meeting. League entries were re-
fused and N.S.W. s Union’s accepted. So Kershaw and
Turnbull will ride for N.S.W.

Westralian “feather” fighter Tommy Cox had
a ba, r ralS go wifch Californian Herrman at Oakland
(U.S.), Feby 19. From end to end of 13 rounds each
poured in hot shot, closed, wrestled, roughed, and
thumped while 1500 spectators howled like maniacs. Cox
maiie pace from the jump, never let up an instant, and
did the Cribb act with vim and vigor. Ultimately the
thing became so cruel that the police chipped in Cox
was declared winner on points.

, . * * *
In ’Frisco they do the thing properly. Boxing-
promoters must get a permit from the “ police committee ”
b ® f °f® . they may bring off a fight. Only one match a
month is allowed in the city, the pros and cons being first
well sifted If either principal has a crooked reputation
there s no hope for him-the controlling body insists that
he must be dropped or sanction is withheld. Sydney
badly needs something like this. J y
* * * ,
Balmain Swimming Club for years held the
water-polo premiership of N.S.W., and proved so much
better than others that all interest in the annual com-
petitions ceased and they were allowed to drop. Early
this season the newly-created rival local body-Mort’s
Dock S.C.—sprung a surprise on followers of the game by
putting down the previously unconquerable team. “ Lack
of condition and “ out of form ” were excises advanced,
and a second match saw Balmain secure a bare win As
it was now one and one, a third had to be arranged,' and
this took place last Saturday, when Mort’s Dock won bv
3 goals to L Secret of Balmain’s continued success was
that they practised often together and almost every
morning during two or three weeks immediately preced-
ing convincing date. No other club hitherto prepared
anything like as systematically, but Mort’s Dock swim-
mers did all through this season
* * « .
xr P?/ Uick Cavill, in 500yds. supremacy of
N.S.W. at Elkington Park Baths, Balmain, Saturday
afternoon, topped everything before done by him. Time
recorded was 6.29 1-5 —13 1-5 speedier than the previous
best (6.42 2-5), which belongs to Englishman Jarvis. Un-
fortunately Cavill’s figures won’t stand, owing to the silly
blundering of officials who made a mistake of 4yds. lOin.
in reckoning up the distance. It was a simple sum in
multiplication that the smallest schoolboy might be
trusted with. Second man (Read), 40yds. from-Cavill,
also got inside Jarvis’ performance. Cavill could have'
nearly covered thrice the difference between the distance
actually gone over and that required in the time he had
to spare. Unfortunately this fact doesn’t help him, and
Jarvis’ achievement still remains on the list as the world’s
absolute best, though, but for the bungling referred it, it
would surely have been reduced by seconds. Prior to
the race it was reported that the world’s record would go
if Cavill won, as Read had shown something much above
the fastest Dick was suspected capable of.
M.L. swimming rep. (Champion) at recent Aus-
tralasian championships in Victoria, upon returning home,
disabused his fellow-swimmers’ minds of the notion that
the cold water of M.L. was much less conducive to speed
than the warmer ditto of Australia. Champion said he
noticed no difference whatever.

* * » *
Homing-pigeon liar takes a turn. “ But you
can t beat the homer for instinct. I gave a pair of my
breeding to a friend in Bathurst, telling him to be sure
and cut their wings close, as, if they got out, they were
bound to come home. About a week later my wife and I
were sitting at breakfast when we heard a scratching at
the front door, and, upon my opening it, in fluttered mv
pair of beauties with corns on theirfeet as big as walnuts.
They had walked home from Bathurst! ”
* * *
Bondi sewer is a great resort of fishermen.
The turbid, oily stream flowing from it can be traced out
in a long curve for over a mile before it appears to merge
into the ocean. When the sea permits, a fleet of boats
can be seen anchored in the stream, some within a few
yards of the mouth of the sewer, while the rocks at the
entrance are . also crowded with fishermen. The fish
caught are almost entirely black bream. The Board of
Health officer some time ago declared these fish quite fit
for consumption, but it is hard to imagine that anyone
who has ever seen, or, what is worse, smelt that sewer at
close quarters would ever eat another black-bream.
* * * *
Bridge-jumping crank Brodie, who originated
the craze, died in the U.S. last month. Modus of bis
business was to don heavily-weighted boots for preserva-
tion of equilibrium and cork jacket for floating purposes
then hang from the edge of a bridge by the hands till
steady, and drop. Waiting boat, with newspaper reps,
aboard, would pick the crank up, and columns of matter
re the mad act would be wired from one end of the Con-
tinent to the other. In his later days he ran a gilded
saloon noted for its extravagant embellishments, where
money was raked in and squandered at a great rate. He
died of consumption.

A recent fresh in the Brisbane River
carried acres .of the water-hyacinth from the
Bremer and tributaries down to Brisbane, where
ferry traffic. was completely stopped for some
time. Ipswich people smiled in a gratified way
when they heard about it.
A side-light upon the quality of what
water was left in the Broken Hill reservoir before
the February rains. In spite of its droughty
situation the silver-town is fairly provided with
small gardens; the chrysanthemum especially
thrives there and throughout the inlands. But
the local society has been compelled to “pass”
this year’s autumn show (for the first time for 10
or 12 years), as the water had become so mineral-
ised that it killed what plants other plagues had
spared.
A hideous pig- story is reported from
Nathalia (Vic.). A housewife left her baby in
the yard to answer a call in the front, and when
she returned it was being attacked by three pigs
Ihe animals were beaten off with difficulty, and
the baby was found shockingly mutilated—flesh
torn from face and head, and one hand badly
chewed. That horrible scene of the pigs amongst
the wounded on the battlefield, drawn by Ambrose
Bierce, in hi.s story, “The Coup-de-Grace,” was
probably close up to truth. The terrible pig takes
his own where he finds it.
The “ business-juryman ” at the Coning-
liam divorce trial which as The Bulletin went
to press was fast drawing to a close :
If the heart of one juryman had bean opened at anv
time during the trial, the word “ business ” would have
been found written there in large letters. On several
occasions this juryman has pathetically appealed
to his Honor as “a business man who was being
kept from his business.” On Tuesday, the man
of business broke forth into a tearful oration from
the box. This is what, in a deep, guttural voice,
he said : Your. Honor, speaking for myself as a
business man, I think it is not right to keep businessmen
away from their business so long. If this case keeps on
like this we 11 be kept away—l am speaking as a business
man—from our business for a year. I speak as a business
man, your Honor, as your Honor will understand that
this means ruin to a man’s business. I’m only speaking
as a business man, your Honor. The men sitting here
and laughing are not business men. It’s more like a
theatrical performance than a trial, your Honor I don’t
mean Mr. Coningham, your Honor. But I’m a business
man, your Honor, and speaking as a business man, I’d
like to know, your Honor, when the trial is to finish as I
want to get back to my business, your Honor. As I said
before your Honor, speaking for myself as a business
liian, I d like the ease to be cut short. My business will
be ruined if I stay here for ever, your Honor.” Judge
Owen (the one man in Court, save the speaker, who had
not rocked with laughter) said he “quite agreed with
every word the gentleman had said. It was very hard on
himself, although he was not a business man.” —Truth.
Can anyone reconcile Gen. Roberts’
two books, “ The Rise of Wellington ” and
“Forty-one Years in India,” with each other,
and with his conduct in S’Africa —except
on the hypothesis that there are two or
three Robertses, as there were two Nelsons ?
day it will be necessary to analyse Roberts’
character as displayed in his writings and actions,
and the result will probably be surprising to
the little man’s admirers. The small military tin-
gods of the British public have a way of getting
knocked out of shape like that. Wellington—
about the most unpopular general the British ever
ha-d is the only one whose reputation has stood
the test of time.
The return of that awful Tory, the hide-
bound bigot Simon Fraser at the top of the Vic.
Senate poll, was a spectacle to make democrats
weep (it paralleled the return of nonentity
Gould almost at the top of N.S.W. Senate
poll). Fraser was favoured by inclusion in the
lists of both Age (he is a personal pal of D. Syme,
ye ken) and Argus —neither of which papers
think enough of him to report his speeches in the
local Leg. Council. He is merely an incompetent
reactionary, utterly incapable of finding reasons
for his obstruction—a man who steadily blocks
progressive legislation with the same intelligence
as a mule blocks traffic. Why, if the world is
controlled by an Omniscient, Omnipotent, and
Beneficent Power, was an artist like Millais
allowed to paint on canvas so ridiculous a politi-
cian as Simon Fraser ?
The power of scientifically-used printer’s
ink was never better shewn than by the respective
positions held by Labor candidates Barrett and
Barker on Vic. Senate poll. Both are adherents
of the Labor-platform in its entirety, and both
personally are obscure bores of the most tiresome
sort. Neither is well-enough-off to canvass the
State properly ; and, as the Labor-ticket included
both, there was no reason to show why one should
receive a preference. There was not a ha’porth
to choose between the pair, save that Barrett
has mutton-chop whiskers, while Barker wears a
beard ; but when D. Syme ukased that the public
was to vote solid for the man with the mutton-
chop whiskers, it obeyed with the docility of a
well-trained poodle. Barrett was returned in the
favoured six ; and Barker, half-way down the list,
scored half as many votes.
i The brightest all-round joke of the Federal
Elections in Victoria was old johnny Wallace’s
advt. circular, which appears to have been dis-
triouted by the million :
Dear Sir, --If you think lam worthy of your vote and
interest I will be glad to get it. I have been nearly 50
years in the colony, and over -j years a member of the
Leg. Council. I was elected without opposition and never
had a contested election. I have continued to do my
duty and have never had a mark against my name, so
please, when you are drawing the pencil through the
names of those whom you do not want to vote for, do not
mark my name out so as I can read it after the Election—
said the circular. This awful literary production
ought to be posted outside Melb. Parliament
House to show the world what sort of intellect
serves to satisfy a Leg. Council constituency for
25 years. A vignette portrait of the uncomely
old squattah tells its story in the top left-hand
corner of the document. There’s the man—the
John Alston Wallace, whose sons a while ago
ousted him from his arrogant bull-headed trustee-
ship of the family property. Wallace, the un-
grammatical autocrat, who used to flourish a stout
walking-stalk at his offspring when they ventured
upon a mild demand for reasonable treatment.
How pitiably stupid seem the scoffs of Austra-
lian Democracy at the hereditary legislation of
a British Peerage whilst such as Wallace can
*u Ul ?t iemse^ves , on having been elected to
the Upper House without opposition for a quarter
of a century ! The circular which set forth Wal-
lace s pretentions to a seat in the Senate was a
joke at the expense of the Vic. public. His
claim to compose it by the sweat of his own brain,
and issue it with his portrait in the corner thereof,
was based on the solid merits of political achieve-
ment. lor all that Wallace can be expected to
know to the contrary, he has done his duty well.
He is a valuable legislator, else why do his con-
stituents endure him from generation to genera-
tion ? And if anybody “marked his name out
(which somebody certainly did) so as he couldn’t
read it after the Election,” the Bulletin considers
it a very unwrong act.

The inconvenient time at which the jury s
decision in the notorious Coningham v. O’Haran
divorce-case was given precludes extended com*
ment in The Bulletin’s present issue. The
verdict, which was in favor of Dean O Haran,
and was “quite concurred in” by his Honor
the presiding Judge, is the only one which could
possibly have been arrived at on the evidence by
any jury of sane and dispassionate men, inas-
much as Mrs. Coningham’s practically-uncorro-
borated story, however convincingly circumstantial
it may have looked when first presented to the
public was not one which would have enabled her
to get an order for three half-crowns a week from
any sober Police Magistrate had sne come
forward, not to aid her husband in his suit
for divorce, but as a single woman claiming sup-
port for the alleged illegitimate offspring of the
co-respondent. The co-respondent is, after all, to
be congratulated on his luck in failing to secure a
verdict at the first trial, in which the jury was
grotesquely allowed by the Court to wander off into
the consideration of a number of theological ques-
tions quite apart from the point at issue ; for had
he succeeded on that occasion he would have been
deprived of the benefit of the startling light
thrown upon the case by the convincing docu-
mentary evidence produced at the second hearing.
The Melbourne firm of Younghusband and
Co. is about to absorb the Co-Operative Wool and
Produce Co. and all business appertaining there-
to.*
Melbourne shopkeepers, with characteris-
tic’cuteness, have not only insured their stands
and decorative gear against loss by fire, but have
also effected insurances on the life of X orK, so
that, in the event of H.R.H. not materialising as
expected, the whole gaudy outlay won t be a dead
loss.
Broken Hill, the one big town towards
the heart of Australia, is surrounded by such
utter desolation, in spite of recent rams (which,
of course, haven’t had time yet to improve condi-
tions), that to supply its meat-market buyers have
had to travel as far afield as Hay, 320 miles south-
east.
The dodges of political canvassers are
many and weird. A distinctly refreshing circular
was issued by the doting papa of a gallant Colonel
who strove vainly fora N.S.W. seat mthe Senate.
Assuming the air of a confidential communication,
it expressed the opinion that “we should be
represented by men of ability and mtegn y,
having a stake in the country. Then it went on :
“My son has proved himself to be a man of
marked ability in everything he has undertaken,
and I am proud to say his straight-forwardness is
beyond question.” If parental certificates were
worth anything, who would not be a Senator .
The treasurer of Sydney Cabmen’s Union
calls attention, in the Evening News, to the
hard measure meted out to ’bus-drivers in the
matter of small breaches of the traffic regulations.
A ’busman, he points out, will be promptly and
heavily fined for having even a couple of passen-
gers over his number, whereas a tram-conductor
may with impunity overload by half-a-hundred or
more.
“ Yiking ” : Discussed N.S.W. Senate
elections with 11 men last Saturday. Six of them,
apparently sane, admitted R. E O’Connor to be
the best of the candidates, but said that, owing to
recent events, they could not vote for a R.C.
“In good sooth, sir, your principles are merely
prejudices.”
W H Woodfall, the defaulting teller of
the Union Bank’s Grenfell (N.S.W.) branch-he
was charged with stealing £4lOO-was found gm ty
and was sent to gaol for 18 months. What luck .
Not long ago a N.S.W. bushman got three years
for “ stealing ” ninepenn’orth of baking-powder
—which he took when he and his mate quarrelled
and broke up camp.

Melb. Age made two glaring “ bloomers ”
out of five mistakes in its 19 commands to the
people of Victoria. The finger of King David
Syme pointed to Parson Ronald as a traitor
unworthy the vote of any respectable working-
man in Southern Melbourne. All the same 3211
disloyal subjects returned pro-Boer Ronald at the
top of the poll. The selection of L. L. Smith was
another inexcusable error m tactics. Even though
he had been a “dead-bird ” for the three-cornered
contest, the Age could have afforded to remark
that the House didn’t want a Protectionist from
Flinders if Smith was the man. And the aged
“ L L.” was not a “ dead-bird.” He finished 300
votes in the rear of the Argus candidate, A. G.
Groom.
Election results in Victoria, as in most
other States, upset all calculations. For the
Senate three Fat Men were returned, two
Liberals, and a Laborite. The elector seems to
have been unable to shake off the ingrained habit
of regarding the Upper House as a sort of club
for large, stodgy men of great social tonnage ;
also he seems to have been too tired to consider
things for himself, and so took the first “ticket
shoved before him, as evidenced by the fact that
the Age got five of its nominees in, the other being
seventh on the poll. The Akgus pulled through
three of its nominees, two of the three being in
pets, Fraser and Zeal; the third, Sargood, is the
only Freetrader. For the Representatives but
three Dry-Doggy men got home, two through tne
splitting of votes. Only in one sohtarycasedida
Freetrader win a straight-out fight, in the Fat-
Man constituency of Kooyong, where Fat-Man
Knox beat Fink as easily as he would had the
latter posed as a Socialist or a Prohibitions .
Curious that, though the democratic metro-
politan electors, as the detailed returns show,
took Syme’s Senate ticket, they turned and
smote him heavily when he attempted to dictate
as regards the Lower House. For days the Ag®
had savagely vilified the Labor candidate in
Southern Melb.— Presbyterian Parson Ronald—
but the workers rolled up m force, and shot their
man in by 3211 against 2256. Democratic
“Yarra” also sent Syme’s selected about his
business ; Tudor—without the heavy Catholic
vote—beating him by 423. And
it was doubtful whether Laborite, Liberal,
or Freetrader would win at Bourke. Unly
Cook’s personal popularity and his demo-
cratic programme pulled him through, lhe
Akgus was also rebuked in no tender fashion for
interfering in the selection of the people s Cham-
ber. In large letters, day after day, it placarded
Cook, M‘Cay, Higgins and Ronald as Pro-Boers
and disloyalists—and every one of them got home.
That dog didn’t fight! But all this is understand-
able. The one incomprehensible fact is the posi-
tion of the Tory Simon Fraser. What he has
ever done that he should get a vote from every
second elector in the State, not even those voters
can say. Probably Fraser himself is as much sur-
prised as anyone.

At Poverty Point.
The picture on the wrapper of the published “ Floro-
dora ” music shows Dolores garbed in tights during the
“ Prapses ” entertainment. Dolores was therefore a leg-
part in the original London production ; and the only sup-
posable reason for the change here is that Carrie Moore
has been growing beautifully thinner since her last
appearance in a boy’s part. Carrie, as The Bulletin has
a dozen times protested, needs a Rest, and the best thing
that could be done for such a bright artist is to send her
to a country farm in the Mallee for 12 months, and let
her, after" she has stayed in bed for the bigger
part of a month, gradually slide into some open-air
manual work, milking quiet cows for preference. The
same advice applies to Joey Cassellis, one of the most
promising dancers Australia has turned out for some
years—who has already sacrificed some of her undeni-
able beauty of face and figure by overwork. Both these
girls are little more than children, and have been doing
the work of grown women for years. Are you listening,
Carrie Moore ? The Bulletin hates attending funerals.
The pretty Nina Boucicault, whom Australia knew a
dozen years ago, was married some time since to Mr. K. H.
Kelly.
Melba, it is now quite plain, isn’t coming. Why should
she? Australia is only meant for people gravitating
towards crutchhood. Nellie M. will be here before the
year 2000 and fast asleep. Irving at 70 will have plenty
left to dazzle us with. Naught can kill an actor in these
advertising days—except Death. It is premature for
anyone to go buzzing round Ellen Terry. Wait till she s a
great-great-grandmother. Then airy, fairy Olivia will
come bounding on to our stage. Jefferson notes a pathetic
confab with Charles Kean in Albert Park, Melbourne.
Kean said, “ I’m too old.” It took a lot to make him find
that out. Barry Sullivan, as a charming young man of
50, had to caper round him with rapier logs. But C.
Kean wouldn’t be too old now, if he had lived right up to
this time. A man’s (or a woman’s) histrionic ability has
such a tremendous comet-tail!
Announced somewhere that bright, clever Peggy Pryde
was lately married to a Melbourne youth named Pearson.
Then the step-father of Peggy’s hopeful son must be her
third husband. Anyhow, The Bulletin always under-
stood that the Mr. P. P. who accompanied the lively little
woman to Australia was her second experiment in matri-
mony.
Mr. Guenett, many years musical critic for the Argus,
has mercifully retired from the field of journalism, and
will henceforth teach music to his private pupils only.
Morrison, the self-satisfied “ Nono ” of Melbourne’s even-
ing ’Erald, is to take Guenett’s place ; but meanwhile
Argus readers are being refreshed by some conspicuously
sane and judicious criticisms, for which Carlyle Smythe
gets the credit. Seems that Morrison’s successor in the
Herald chair of music will be Hartung, chief apostle by-
appointment to Marshall Hall, and an exponent of scien-
tific warbling on the serious German system. These
changes are somewhat awful things to contemplate.
They apparently mean that Melbourne will have to put
all its faith in Wagner and the prophets, or be d d
everlastingly.
Long, long ago in the days when the local Herald was
a morning journal, R. S. Smythe (he was not then the
Much-Travelled) gave Melbourne its first criticism of “II
Trovatore.” It would be interesting to read some of
those early notices on singers who were absolutely bril-
liant in some cases, and compare them with the nonsense
written about third-rate voices in the grand (!) opera of
to-day.
Hazon and Geo. Allan had to make their round of the
Italian opera-houses (starting from Milan) on the right
side of Feb. 20th, when the shows everywhere close for
Lent. Their trouble now is to arrange reasonable terms
with the artists they are most anxious to secure, and the
most particular trouble no doubt is to get the right
woman whose contract doesn’t involve the engagement of
an undesirable husband, or vice-versa.
Snazelle, who has Charles M'Mahon for business agent,
is putting in a while in Adelaide with “ Our Navy.” By
the way, Snazzy must be praying devoutly for an out-
break of naval war to stimulate public interest in the
Handy Man afloat and ashore.
Harry Monkhouse, who visited Australia with a Gaiety
co., died in London of acute pneumonia Feb. 18. The
stout man left the Gaiety crowd for the Lyric Theatre,
and was playing in “ Florodora ” until a month before his
death. He was only 47.

The story that Lady Hall 6 had decided to turn her
back upon England and end her career in Germany
turns out to have been as false as the report that Edward
Lloyd was going to make a tour round the world before
settling down upon his farm.
Tyrone Power and Edith Crane will appear shortly at a
leading Sydney Theatre with an entirely new company
and a big new repertoire, including “ Nadjezda” (a Polish
play), “The Charity Ball,” “The Wife,” and “ Taming
of the Shrew ” (including the Induction).

to his old part of Tweedlepunch in the London production
of “Florodora.” Re the London Florodora, George
Allan writes that if it had been produced in the same sty e
in Melbourne it wouldn’t have lasted three weeks. But
then the patriotic Australian abroad ever writes in that
strain.

Rickards’s in the first throes of Australia s jingoism, and
used to exhort us to
“ Fight for Freedom’s cause,

has not yet been called to his long account byastern

“ God save the King !

Well-beloved Queen and Mother 1 Mother more than

Barrett’s announcement that the deceased monarch gave
peace to the Chows, the Boers and humanity in general,
was so well received by the Jingo press that he broke
out again in the Morning Post with a long poem to the
King, requesting His Maj. to “ Take up the Sceptre and
reign abundantly.
George Rignold is due at Pertfi Theatre Royal for
Easter, and will tour the goldfields later. George follows
on the track of Johnnie Sheridan, who got such a warm
Westralian welcome in “ When the Lamps are Lighted
that first performance had to pause whilst he fired off a
speech.
“ Stager ”: Satisfactory to hear of an Australian
actress touring in a number of “ star” characters, bean,
Camille, Juliet, &c. Miss Ada Lawrence is declared by
some people to be tbe brightest and most versatile
tragedienne Australia has produced, not even excepting
Essie Jenyns. I have it from a man who lately saw Miss
Lawrence playing in Tasmania, that she is a most inter-
esting artist, strong, unaffectedly emotional, and
possessed of a natural humour. She is the daughter of
Bob Lawrence, who supported G. V. Brooke in the early
Melbourne days. As a child she got her first Shakespenan
experience with Oreswick, playing one of the princes in
Richard the Third.
“C. C. ” : The “ Highland Legacy ” at Sydney Palace
proved once for all that a Scotch accent is not to
be attained merely by calling a cow a “ coo,” or remark-
ing that anything the speaker approves of is ‘ braw.
A genuine Irish brogue may be heard ten times on the
stage for the once that you will listen to a correct in-
tonation of the Scotch “R” or “Ch.’ The reason is
that the Scotch do not take readily to a histrionic career,
their natures being too open and sincere—writer is
Scotch, so believes this must be the reason. A genuine
criticism was given of the “Tom, Dick, and Harry . in-
volvement by a nice girl in the D.O. who was perceived
at each fresh joke enthusiastically slapping the knee of
the man beside her—a man who was a perfect stranger to
her. The real finale of “Tom, Dick,” &c. took place
that night after the curtain descended when the gentle
maiden realised the unauthorised chastisement she had
given to an unknown person, and fled blushing from the
theatre.
The 100th performance of “Florodora” drew the abso-
lutely biggest crowd on record at Her Majesty’s, Melb.
And it looked as though the occasion had more to do with
the rush than the Souvenir, for the Souvenir (a tastefully
wrappered set of “Florodora” photos by Talma) was a
throw-in for “ ladies only.” The proportion of men who
came early on their own was just about up to the average,
and many more were turned away from the doors.
Marie Lloyd enjoyed her 31st birthday three days
before she started for Australia. Seems that Mane was
taken suddenly ill on her birthday night, and had to miss
her turn at one of the London halls. Next afternoon,
when she came to be farewelled by her admirers at the
Tivoli Restaurant, the sprightly little woman burst into
weeps. But a presentation silver coffee service had a
consoling effect, and, in the words of the historian,
“ Marie bucked up.”
Tyrone Power is at least a foot too tall for Napoleon,
otherwise he might have made the part as great a success
as he did Sydney Qarton in Melbourne.
An eminent Australian actor-manager once had some
business with a Brisbane firm of lawyers. Rushing
up stairs, perspiring and puffing, he ignored the office boy
and the office etiquette and in his stage voice announced
himself to the senior partner thus : “My name is George
; I am an act-orr.” “ The devil you are, said the
s.p., “ then go out and wait until you’re announced.”
“Stroller”: Compressed drama as advocated by the
“ B.” is very much in evidence now at the London hails.
Sketches —original or boiled-down adaptations of other
plays—are regularly produced; running from 30 to 40
minutes. The writer played Macari in a4O min. sketch of
“ Called Back.” All the scenes were in it, the compres-
sion being brought about chiefly by omitting the comedy
and leaving only the “situations.’ It was on Boxing
Night, to the roughest, drunkest audience in .London.
Previous to our sketch a pint-pot was thrown on to tne
stage. But they listened with surprising attention and
despite the rattle of coin and glasses, the smoke, and an
occasional beery scuffle, it was on the whole an enjoj able
experience. .
A Woman’s Needs.
A very eminent surgeon lias declared tnat he
never lets a day pass without thanking Heaven
that he was not born a woman ; and this dis-
tinguished man probably knows more of woman s
trials than any living person. Yet how sweet and
gracious is the gift of womanhood ! To woman
belongs the joys of girlhood, love and beauty—ot
wifehood and motherhood. “ Angels are painted
fair to look like thee ! ” But the penalty of her
sex has to be paid, and the tribute it exacts is
heavy with pain and weariness. Too
payment leaves her ruined in health, bankrupt m
happiness—a lifelong invalid, for want of a little
timely help, perhaps a little timely knowledge.
For the sex thereof is the life thereof, is the case
of women. When childhood is over and girlhood
begins, the burden must be taken up and the first
crisis of a woman’s life met. It should make the
blossoming of womanhood into the lovely strength
of early maturity ; too often it is the beginning of
a lifelong struggle with pain and ill-health. At
this and every critical point, a little help may
make all the difference between happiness and
misery—between health (in short) and disease.
The purpose of this article is to suggest the
tried means of help. The one thing which will
tide over the difficulty and give health and
strength just where they are needed. A lady s
experience and advice is a safe guide for ladies,
and the following letter from Mrs. Annie Harris,
of “Charlton,” George-street, Norwood, b.A.,
gives both:—“About seven years ago I con-
tracted a severe internal complaint and was in a
very critical state. The doctor attending me told
me that if I lived I should be a sufferer all my
life. His treatment was quite useless. It gave
me no relief. After two or three years suffering
I got tired of paying doctor’s fees and deriving no
benefit, so, hearing of Warner’s Safe Cure, I gave
it a trial. I took, in all, five bottles, and am now
thankful to state that lam perfectly cured, lhe
medical man was genuinely surprised at such a
wonderful result. lam now as well as it is pos-
sible to be, and give Warner’s Safe Cure the
whole credit of accomplishing what doctors
deemed impossible. Hoping that ladies suffering
as I did will profit by these few lines I am writing
to you now. My mother considered that her life
was saved by Warner’s Safe Cure, she
taken it for a somewhat similar complaint.


One Taste Is Enough.
Th ' (vriter never had a taste of rheumatism but
once—about four years ago it was—and it laid
me up and made me groan for six weeks. And I
am not praying for it any more. I can get a heart-
load now, just by thinking how it felt. But, oh,
what a lot of folk catch it worse than I did.
Here is Mrs. Annie Hill, she is one of them.
Or rather she ivas ; she is right enough in these
a} s. Her idea of talking of it is to cheer some
other sufferer; and show him the way out. And
we thank her for that. It’s the proper feeling to
have towards our fellow-travellers through this
vale of tears—and pains.
„ ‘ Some eight or ten years ago,” says Mrs. Hill
1 was a perfect martyr to rheumatism and indi-
gestion. As if they were not enough for one poor
woman to bear I often had frightful pains in the
chest, with weakness all over my body. It was

Bunny has a poor time of it now—quoted
in the London market as flat at 7id., he is bundled
out of existence in Victoria with the assistance of
M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., the big iron-
mongers in Melbourne. That firm supplies guns,
ammunition and traps, and, in cases of dire need,
can turn on a full blast of destruction with Dixon
Gould’s Vacuum Destroyer. This machine is the
Jim Dandy of its kind, is beautifully made,
certain in operation as a Waterbury watch, and
counts its victims by thousands. Poor bunny !*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-at., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.
The workers haven’t always the short
memories they are credited with. It would hardly
bethought that the bucolic John Kidd would appeal
much to a radical mining community; and yet,
outside R. E. O’Connor and the Labor-men, there
wasn’t a candidate for the Senate who got more
support at Broken Hill than he. The explanation
is that during the great strike of ’92 Kidd, then
Postmaster-General, got at variance with his col-
leagues and the newspapers for speaking out on
the men’s side, just then the unpopular one, and
for franking telegrams between the strike com-
mittee and the city people who at various times
tried to bring about a conference between the
parties.
Baronet Rupert Clarke surprised Mel-
bourne by taking the chair at one of Dr. “ Billy ”
Maloney’s city electioneering meetings just prior
to the Federal elections. The titled rich man
spoke of the Radical Labor-member as “my very
old and dear friend,” and mutual expressions of
respect and affection were exchanged freely to the
wonder and delight of a large, horny-handed
audience. S’Rupert of Rupertswood is posing of
late as an “advanced” landlord with Socialistic
sympathies, and Dr. Billy’s Radical friends accept
his patronage as a hint that capital is not going to
be scared out of the country by politicians like
“ the great little man ” of West Melbourne.

This valuable paper rises to acknowledge the
receipt'from the publishers, Messrs Gordon and Gotch,
of the Australian Handbook for 1901. The Australasian
Handbook would be a better title, seeing that the volume
includes the statistics and all other particulars of Maori-
land, Fiji and New Guinea. The present number is
quite up to the usual high standard. In addition to
Australasian information there is a considerable fund of
knowledge relating to Britain and its metropolis which
is always useful in a newspaper-office when the tired
editor wants to fill in space with comparisons between
the Sydney Municipal body and London County Councils.
There are maps of all the States of Australasia, and every
town in each State is taken individually, and carefully
described, in detail. When you wish to know if your own
particular little sect has a mothers’ meeting in any little
out-back one-horse show that is dignified by the name of
a town, you can find out at least if the sect has a place
wherein to meet in that remote hamlet. Particulars
are (.iven of all the tariffs, and maps to show
one where he may find gold, iron, copper, antimony and
mica, and many other valuable minerals. Whole sections
are devoted to Australasian Banks and Govt. Securities
and the comparative statistics of the various States and
dependencies of Australia.*
The Modern Art Jewellery Co., of 187
Pitt-street, Sydney, have a splendid and extensive
stock of watches; the principal line being the
famous Rotherham and Sons’ English levers,
which have been made expressly for the Modern
Art Jewellery Co. and bear the firm’s name.
These watches combine durability with accuracy
and are a pleasure to the wearer. Bangles, the
firm has in endless variety—Nellie Stewart
bangles; gold and silver bamboo bangles; bangles
for ladies, girls and babies at moderate prices.
Other lines are gold lucky bells, hearts, elephants
and envelopes, Federation charms, and hundreds
of others in most novel and dainty designs.
Country friends are cordially invited to inspect
the stock.*
That most welcome mine of information upon
all matters Australian, the Year Book of Australia for
1901, is now to hand. The amount of brain-sweat that
the Year Book of Australia and Publishing Co. has put
into this work deserves to be rewarded with an over-
whelming demand. Any fact about the Commonwealth
of Australia which cannot be found within its covers
must be a very small and remote incident indeed. All
the principal events of last year are arranged in calendar
order. There is an opening sketch in which the Federal
movement is outlined, and the different tariffs of the two
great parties are held up to the dull, fishy eye of the
general public. There are vast heaps of statistics from
which the general public can dig out news as to the con-
jugal condition of the great Australian nation—and find
out how much the great nation aforementioned owes the
British and local creditor. It is also carefully states what
the money was borrowed for, and he who wishes to do so
may read and see if the country got its money’s worth.
This is a very important portion of the work indeed;
but, more’s the pity, it won’t be read much, as the
Australian has a horror of seeing his liabilities walk about
in their naked hideousness.. Even the Sportsman (five
sixths of the community is a sportsman) can get par-
ticulars of matters equine, the scale of weights-for-age,
principal courses, comparative’racing time-table, winning
sires, winning owners, and a whole cartload of informa-
tion about records and cricket, cycling and every other
recognised form of sport. Section XIX, relating to In-
dustries, should be read by everyone ; it talks with a
louder voice than all the candidates for the Senate in one
breath. Altogether, the Year Book is an indispensable
volume.*
The Jingo Australian press, which insisted
on the allegation that Queen Victoria’s death was
felt as a “personal loss” by everybody in this
far-off country, seems to have gone further in its
grovel‘than the London County Council, or any
other purely British institution. An address from
the Lord Mayor and Aldermen to the new King
merely expressed a “feeling of the deepest
regret’on the lamented demise of our late, &c.,
&c.” The Co&nty Council said “to us, as indeed
to all her late Majesty’s subjects, the national be-
reavement appears to he almost personal.” The
pretence of national “grief,” “affection,” or
“distress” on account of the Queen’s death was
mainly a journalistic fraud.

Ed. Bulletin, — R e delivery of Wednes-
day’s milk on following Sunday (8., 16/3/’01). No
need to go to the police-court to learn that —our
“milk-oh !” told us himself. Wednesday is a
half-holiday, and the milk which would ordinarily
be delivered that afternoon is cold : stored until fol-
lowing Sunday. Have seen it afloat with lumps
frozen solid, and, so far as could be judged,
not ling wrong. I presume that Sunday’s supply
is frozen until Wednesday, to keep matters
craight.— Fattt.'

A RATIONAL REFORM IN TEACHING
FRENCH.
Teachers must bear in mind that it is a gross mistake,
as well as a mere waste of time and labor, to land pupils
on the grammatical and literary stages of the study before
the first stage (Phonetic and Mechanical) has thoroughly
been mastered. (Dr. Henry Sweet, Oxford.)
Therefore use PERIER’S FRENCH METHOD (Is. 6d.)
and its PHONETIC COMPANION (Is. 3d.). All book-
sellers. Copies are sent, post free, by sending postage
stamps to A. 0. GAERTNER, Sole Agent,
36 Norwich Chambers, Hunter-street, Sydney.


An Engineer in
Difficulties!
TRIES, LIKE MANY MORE, HEAPS OF
SO CALLED REMEDIES
Meets with Final Success.
The Case of
MR. JAMES BURNS.
fU;/ Our Reporter.)
No invalid is so difficult to deal with as
the one who has “ never known a diiy’s
sickness." For the most part he resolutely
puts away the suggestion—“ That his time
has comeat last, that his smooth wot king
machinery has broken down, and i hat active
repair is at once required.” No ! the
chances are the sufferer battles on, getting
daily worse and worse, till final collapse lays
him on his back.
“My husband never would have that
anything was seriously the matter with him,
that he was bilious, or that his liver wus not
as sound as it ought to be,’’ said Mrs. Bums
as our reporter interviewed that lady and
Mr. Burns. “ Not till he was really bad
and desperately ill would he give in, and
admit that something was radically wrong
with him.” Mr. Burns, it may be men-
tioned, is an engineer by profession, living
at 107 Stanley-street, North Melbourne,
and well known in that locality. Some time
ago he was engaged in a very arduous
undertaking, where he was exposed to all
kinds of weather, continually knocked up
by excessive travelling, and left for long
periods with poor and irregular food. In
the pride of good health and an iron con-
stitution, Mr. Burns thought nothing of
these hardships at the time ; but later on,
like many more before him, he had good
cause to recollect. “ I fell,”said Mr. Burns,
“into a low lethargic condiii n of body
and mind. I had pains all over me. Ido
not think there was a pait of my frame free
from an ache of some kind. I was bilious
with an excess of bile that seemed to numb
all my faculties. I did not care to work, to
move about, to do anything. Yet when I
braced myself to active exercise I felt better
—that is, while the work lasted ; but when
the excitement and stimulant of exercise
was over I was done, prostrate and desti-
tute of all hope and energy. I suffered
from & pain below my neck and between
my shoulders, that cramped up my body.
At the least excitement my heart was
deranged, and went thump, thump, thump
against my ribs like the beat of a hammer.
My stomach and digestive organs behaved
very strangely, as though they had gone on
■trike and quite given up work. No sooner
had I eaten a meal than I had to run to the
back yard and vomit it all up just as I had
swallowed it; so that my assimilative pro-
cess was absolutely paralysed. At times I
would go giddy in the head, like a child
that turns rouud and round; I could not
stand on my feet, and felt the sick deadly
sensation of one about to faint. If I had
been resting any time in one position, and
went to get up, I was held fast by a stiff
gripping pain in the small of the back
Gradually I lost all taste or inclination for
food ; I would go for two or three days con-
tinuously without eating. This starvation
greatly added to my weakness, and at the
same time I rapidly lost flesh, and became
reduced to a shadow of my former self. My
nervous system suffered with the rest of
my body, and I became morbidly sensitive
to the least sound. A loud knock at the
door would make me start and jump as
though a camion had suddenly been fired
-close by. My temper became very irritable,
I was vexed and upset at the least thing.
1 slept well, in fact I was always drowsy ;
but the sleep app a red to do me no good. I
was never rested, bleeping for one hour
was aa good as eight for all the benefit I
derived, and in the morning I would be
more tired than at night. A tenor that
haunted my night’s rest was nightmare.
Even when I awoke, or, what won d often
happen, my wife woke me, I soil for a time
experienced the cruel pains of suff cation
and strangulation. This general upsetting
of my whole system affected my eyes ; there
were times wh»u they were quite dull, my
sight becoming blurred, and it was with
difficulty that 1 could see to read. When I
stooped down there followed pains aero s my
loins like the piercing of sharp instruments.
My palate always iiad a nasty flavour, my
tongue was parched and dry. I became
wonderfully chang d in outward appearance
and was depressing, to look at, thin, worn
and emaciated, a skeleton of what I had
been.”
“ What remedies did you try, Mr. Burns?’
enquired the pressman.
“Heaps of things, various makes of bitters
and blood purifiers, all warranted to cure
cases like mine and all useless, also a
uutnber of patent and much 1 aided cure-alls,
with a like result. None did me the slight-
est good, iu fact I became day by day
steadily worse. It was about this time,
when 1 concluded that there was no medi-
cine in the world that would relieve my
ufferings, that a book was left at the door
descriptive of the virtues of Clements Tonic.
“I wonder,” said my wife, “ if that would
do you any good, let us have one more try.”
She went over to our chemist and asked
about Clements Tonic, and that gentleman
told her she could not go wioug in buying
it, that it was :lie best remedy be knew of,
that Ins sales of Clements Tonic were large
and increasing, and he had ieceived the very
best reports from his customers who had
purchased it, and that there was not the
shadow of a doubt that Clements Tonic did
all that it claimed to do. After that, you
may be sure I purchased a bottle of Clements
Tonic, for I know that chemists are not
naturally inclined to give credit to propiie-
taiy medicines without good reason. My
health began to mend from the very first
hot tle I took. I gained a good appetite, and
my stomach retained the food. My sleep
refreshed me, the pains all over my body, in
my back, head, and heart were all subdued.
I regained energy and flesh and lost my
sallow appearance, my nerves were strength-
eued, and at night 1 slept soundly, undis-
turbed by dreams ; and very soon my cure
was complete.”
“I report this for the papers, Mr. Burns,”
said our representative.
“ I am glad to hear it, and I hope it will
be read and do good ; it’s all true, and you
can publish it how you I ke.”

KUTNOW’S POWDER Restores Health and Activity to the Liver, and Drives Away
the Diliousness, Headache, Hervousness, Dyspepsia, Constipation, Low Spirits,
Insomnia, Lack of Appetite and Energy, &c., which a Disordered Liver causes.
An active, healthy liver means pure blood, a stomach and kidneys in good working order, a healthy appe-
tite for food ability to sleep, capacity for work, and freedom from headache, nervousness, biliousness, dyspepsia,
constipation, and a hundred other ills that make life miserable. Those persons, however, who are engaged m
sedentary occupations, or are in the habit of indulging too freely in rich foods, or even plain foods, seldom have
bodies that are in this healthy condition, and hence a very large proportion of the people of the. well-to-do, well-
fed, brain-working class suffer from a disordered liver.
It is useless to attempt to remedy liver derangements and their symptoms by taking malt, pepsin, and
other preparations, which give nothing more than mere temporary relief. In order to restore the liver to healthy
activity, it must be freed of the impurities which weaken it and hinder its action ; and nothing does this more
effectively and less unpleasantly than Kutnow’s Improved Effervescent Carlsbad Powder. It is the most speedy
and certain remedy for all derangements of the liver.
Everyone is aware that when a sponge has been in use for some time the pores become clogged with soap
and other matter, which make it almost useless. When, however, it is soaked for several hours in a solution of
sa j t or> better still, in a warm solution of alkaline sulphated water —the soap and other impurities are dissolved
out and the sponge becomes clean.
Exactly the same thing occurs when a sufferer from a disordered liver takes a course of Kutnow’s Powder.
It increases the action of that and other digestive organs by freeing them of the waste matters that cause their
derangement. It renders them, as it were, like a clean sponge. The moment this derangement of the organs
has been rectified, and the liver has been restored to a healthy condition, the sick headache, biliousness, nervous-
ness, dyspepsia, constipation, low spirits, insomnia, loss of appetite, &c., which liver disorder causes will disappear,
and renewed health, strength and vigor follow.
After using Kutnow’s Powder the digestion becomes stronger, the excretion more prompt, and the
assimilation more complete.

Father’s Diamonds.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Father’s fad was Co-operation. That was the
reason he gave up his work as a baker and took a
contract for breaking stones. You see, he had a
family of five boys strong enough to use a hammer,
and there were others coming on.
Father appointed himself Treasurer.
Stone was stone in those days, and the co-
operative company was a success. So much so,
that father began to develop a scorn of all white-
handed occupations.
“You begin when you like, knock off when you
like, have a holiday when you like,” said father,
“and there’s no boss to come interfering.”
Father always spoke like that a day or two
before the monthly pay. No doubt he was think-
ing of the fortnight’s holiday he always took after
the settling-up. It would have been longer,
father said, if the surveyor hadn’t cheated him.
His opinion of surveyors was unprintable.
“ And it’s a healthy occupation, too. Gives
spring to the boys’ muscles, and makes ’em indus-
when he returned to work, and noticed the
growing heap of stone from the boys’ hammers.
6 The boys’ did not dare to take holidays them-
selves Father was against it. Jack tried to
rebel’once, but father quelled the insurrection
with the hammer-handle.
Then the record-pay came —sufficient to keep
the old man on the spree for three weeks. He
even omitted to hint that the surveyor was a
tl 'wiien he turned-up again the supply of coarse
stone was nearly exhausted. Father always did
the quarrying himself. He reckoned he knew a
thing or two about stone. “ You can’t trust
them boys,” he said. “ Like as not they’d pick out
the hardest. Catch me, though ! ”
Father was pleased about something when he
got home that night. lie talked of becoming the
richest man in Australia before the end of the
year Father’s talk didn’t make much impression
on the rest of the family. He was of optimistic
temperament, and usually had a scheme that
would enrich him some time in the future.
“ I’m the man the slianty-keeper refused a drink
to yesterday—and, to-day!” Father stopped
short and chuckled. . , , T ,
Suddenly he ordered the boys to bed. lie
wanted to be quiet and think.
Father did nothing but quarry, and grew hap-
Next pay was the smallest in the history of the
co-operative company, and father’s credit was
gone at the end of the first week.
He sulked for a day, and then went off to the
shanty-keeper. ~ ,
The shanty-keeper frowned, but father called
him aside, and drew.something wrapped in paper
from his pocket.
The shanty-keeper was interested, lie sus-
pected half-a-sovereign.
Father tore away the paper and displayed a
glittering quartz crystal.-
“ A diamond ! ” whispered father. ,
“Diamond be hanged ! I’ll get you a handful
for sixpence. Get out! ”
Father got.
JACK ASH.
Our Boarding-house.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
N.'S.W.
There were six of us fellows ; all young, in good
billets, and with fine, healthy appetites.
We had engaged with Mrs. Cluny on the under-
standing that she took no other boarders, and
then, when we were settled, she went back on us,
for one night, at dinner, we found another pay-
ing guest,” and, worst of all, a lady.
We called a meeting upstairs, in Smiler’s bed- :
room, and decided she would have to go. This
was the first night, and, after we had stood her
for a week, we made a deputation to our landlady.
She was very sorry, etc., but the lady-boarder was
a “monthly-paid-in-advance,” therefore it could
not be done, and we must put up with it. We
called another meeting, to lay our plans. Our
first grievance was that, at breakfast every morn-
ing, she had two hot, buttered rolls while we had
the’ plain, or garden, bread. One morning, the
plate was seized by the literary man, and handed
to Fatty, who had to eat them both. He did not
seem to relish his breakfast as much as he thought
he would. If the murder in the lady-boarder s
eye could have taken effect there would have been
six corpses for the cold-storage chamber.
When we all met at dinner there was a frigid
silence. For the first time since her advent, the
lady-boarder did not tell the story of her great
connections at “’Ome.”_ After dinner the Cynic
kept muttering about poisons that killed and left
no trace. The Dazzler told a story about a man
he knew who had married young and then re-
pented, and had lured his wife to the edge of a
cliff and pitched her over. Some of us could not
quite see how this conversation could help us m
our dilemma, but at last, with a knowing wink,
Fatty invited us up to his room. He had dis-
covered that the “She’s” bedroom was exactly
underneath his. We all set to work with a wilf.
The carpet was taken up, furniture moved to one
side, a chalk ring drawn, and a boxing contest
begun, which was to last indefinitely from 9 to 12
every night (she went to bed at 9).
The old girl stood it like a brick and made no
complaint. We had nearly tired of our own
sport when, on the third night, we heard a
timid knock on the door. To our united yell of
“ Come in,” entered Mr. Cluny. He was a little,
knock-kneed man with an immense idea of his
own prowess. He uttered a poor little squeak of
remonstrance —“ Gentlemen, gentlemen, there
are ladies in the house!” We promptly caught
and stripped him, clapped on the gloves and
started to play football with him. Told him he
was a Hercules in the buff, and to put em up.
He did and so did Billy. When he had one eye
bunged-up and his nose streaming with gore,
there came another knock on the door. We stood
paralysed as to what would happen. Mrs. Cluny
opened the door suddenly, gave a fearful scream
and fled.
The next night we lolled on the floor at ease,
smoked our pipes, and rolled dead marines up and
down the floor. They made a lovely row, and it
was not nearly so tiring as boxing. Suddenly the
gas went out. Wo went to investigate with
matches and found it turned off at the'meter, and
the key nowhere to be found. Held a meeting at
midnight on the roof and made the night hideous
with our suggestions for revenge. We finally de-
cided that we would all give notice to leave. We
drew lots as to who should be the first to speak.
Our Artist was the winner. _ He was a man who
went in for wild bohemianism, and only shaved
when he was asked out to dinner. In the morn-
ing we were all out of bed at the first stroke of the
gong and hanging over the bannisters awaiting
our turn. We heard our Artist pleasantly pass
the time of day, and then, ‘‘ Er Mrs. Cluny,
doosid sorry y’ know, but —er —I shave each night
before retiring, and—er —I find it quite impossible
to do without the gas y’ know, so l am reluctantly
compelled to give you a week’s notice; too doosid
risky y’ know to shave by the light of a candle.
We could not hear her reply. Then the Beau
went down and in exactly the same words (only he
always varnished his boots at night) also gave
notice. When five of us had been represented
Mrs. Cluny got hysterical and burst into tears.
She said we could have the gas-meter taken up-
stairs to our bedrooms —we could have the whole
house, anything for peace. As we felt we had
won a victory we graciously consented to stop, it
only the lady-boarder left. She promised, and
home was once more “ Home.”
The following week the lady-boarder’s month
was up, and she left for Maoriland. Mrs. Cluny
thought she would like a change and went with
her. She gave Mr. Cluny particular instructions
to look after our comfort, which he did m a bitter
spirit by reason of the famous boxing-lesson.
One night we gave a party. We sent in two
big bags of oysters and several dozen of beer, w e
carried them into the finnicky man’s room, in-
vited a few fellows in and made things hum We
put our visitors out with difficulty at about o
am., and then started to clean-up. _We stacked
the oyster-shells on top of the stairs, and then
sliding dexterously down the banisters we went to
our several couches and slept the sleep of the just.
Eatty, who was too fresh to get downstairs, we
left on the landing sleeping like a cherub. Me
woke about 4 a.m., feeling cold, and started to
erope his way down. In the dark he fell foul ot
the oyster-shells. They went clattering down,
and so did Fatty, and all night long was heard the
drip, drip of the shells falling into the hail.
We went down to breakfast feeling very happy,
and Mr. Cluny growled out “ that if we were
going to chuck our boots up and down stairs all
night we could chuck ourselves out of the house.
We took him at his word and decided to
That night we drew up an advt. for the
Herald :
q IX Young Gentlemen, good positions, desire Board
O an d Residence in good house with quiet family ;
good table and absolute quiet essential. Apply by
letter or personally, after six, to Integrity, Dash-
street, Potts Point.
For the next three days bell and knocker were
never still. In three days we received 197 letters
■and fifty personal applications. Some ladies were
so kind as to bring their daughters for inspection,
and in one instance the old lady brought three.
We took it in turns to interview them, and we
never had such a roaring week in all our lives.
The people sent in repeatedly from next door to
complain about us, so we climbed on the roof m
the dead of night and dropped lighted crackers
down the bedroom chimneys. I forgot to men-
tion that the house next door was a ladies school.
We found a place to suit us with two old maiden
ladies, and Saturday being half-holiday we hired
two carts to move us. We condoled very much
with old Cluny, and recommended him a good
hair-restorer. We also clubbed and presented
him with a set of boxing-gloves.
The two old maids were taken away to Callan
Park last week, and,"Mrs. Cluny having returned
from Maoriland, we all came into our own again
last Saturday, . . , ~ •
r Oluny is now sleeping m the coal-cellar.
ELESE ESPINASSE.
Queensland Salvation Army offers a bed for
a shilling a night; a bunk for 4d. or 3d. a night;
refreshments, all prices down to a penny - -Not
much chance for a working-man’s eating-house
that has to compete with the Harmy, which pur-
veys at such a price victuals of ten procured gratis.
N.S.W. compositors have had a long run
of lean years, and, taking them as a body, they
have borne-up spiritedly enough. Labor-saving
inventions they do not rail against; but if there s
a kick in them it ought to be delivered at a com-
paratively new, but rapidly-growing, system under
which the.matrix ” of columns of matter set-up
in cheap dens in England is received m Sydney at
a trifle a column and issued in N.S.W. news-
papers.
“ J.S.” to The Bulletin
That- Montenegrin youth who can see certain satellites
of Jupiter with the naked eye, is one of a small number
of visually-gifted persons possessing an optical peculiarity
which overcomes the glare from the adjacent planet.
Such cases are occasionally recorded. A
tailor of Breslau, who lived in the early part of this cen
tury, could distinguish the third satellite at a glance, the
first with great clearness when at its maximum depres-
sion, but never the second or fourth alone. As P oin ted
out by a correspondent (8., 2/3, ’01) Jupiter really has five
moons, but the smallest, discovered by Barnard in 1892
with the Lick telescope, is only visible to telescopes of
the greatest power.
Certain stars are also to be seen only by persons pos-
sessing exceptionally strong sight—one of the Pleiades
for instance. The group consists of one star of the 3rd
magnitude, two of the 4th, three of the sth, and two be-
tween the 6th and 7th, besides numerous smaller tele-
scopic bodies. Of the two last-mentioned, one is too
close to Atlas (4th mag.) to be seen but the other,
Celseno, is visible on clear nights to those possessed of
strong sight—the present writer is an example. Com-
paratively few people seem to be able to see this seventh
Pleiad with unaided vision. Alcor, in the tail of the
Great Bear, was called by the old Arabian astronomers
Sadak— “The Test”—since, from the fact of its being
overpowered by the adjacent large star Mizar, it formed
a good standard for sharpness of sight in pre-telescopic
dl The new star lately reported in Perseus is an instance
of another rare phenomenon. About 22 instances of the
kind are recorded in the last 2000 years, taking the
ancient Chinese records into account. Tycho Brahe in
1573 observed a new star of extraordinary brilliancy and
various colours in Cassiopeia, which gradually faded away
after 17 months, and a pupil of Kepler in 1604 discovered
a big new star in Ophiuchus. Other stars have
shown periodical variability, suddenly flaming up
into unusual brilliancy. Some stars change color, Sinus
being the best-known example. In Ptolemy’s time it was
described as a “ fiery-red ” star, but by the 16th century
had become pure white, remaining so to this day.
Astronomers cannot explain the phenomenon, but the
best accepted theory implies some alteration of the clouds
surrounding it, which has affected the passage of its light-
rays The appearance of a “ new ” star probably depends
on the sudden ignition of a dark oosmical body. Many
of these “dark stars” are known to exist, but they
are of course, invisible to the telescope. Perseus, the
constellation in which the latest example has appeared,
has no previous pranks of the kind recorded against it,
but is somewhat famous on account of the extraordinary
number of shooting stars it has contributed to the
November displays. The new star will probably fade
and disappear as its predecessors have done. In ancient
days some catastrophe would have been fathered on it,
but Man is becoming less magnificently egotistical

HEARD IN TRAINS, ’BUSES—EVERYWHERE ARD ALWAYS- DOAN’S.
When incidents like the following occur here in Sydney, and
Sydney men and Sydney women relate their experiences in a
Sydney paper for the benefit of Sydney people, the genuineness of
the statements cannot be doubted. They have the ring of truth
about them that there is no getting away from.
Here is the case of Mrs. Fred. Russell, 7 St.
Andrews-street, Balmain: —“My husband has
been bad three or four years with kidney trouble.
He was such a sufferer that he could not stand up
straight, and all his movements were fraught with
great pain. Unable to sleep at night, he used to
try all manner of positions, and would get up next
morning thoroughly done up and unrefreshed.
The best position for him to lie in was face down-
ward, but he would soon have to change that.
Sometimes the pains were a lot worse than others.
To stand up straight was wojse than hard work.
When well, before he got bad I mean, he used to
do heavy work, but he had to give up, his back
having well-nigh crippled him. He went to hos-
pitals one after another ; and when they failed to
give him ease from his terrible pain he tried all
manner of remedies advertised, excepting one.
We spent a lot of money in plasters and liniments
and other things for outward application,
but he was still as bad as ever after using
them—aye, worse. It is now over 12 months
since that lucky day when we got Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills at Newman’s Pharmacy,
King-street. At that time he was unable
to sit down or lie down, and when he walked
he held his back all the time. His pains were
frightful. Well, the first few doses of Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills rid him of those terrible
pains, and after a time he became much improved.
In all he took six boxes. Then he left off the
medicine, and has taken none since, and that is
over six months ago. A few days ago he said to
me, ‘You never hear me complain of pains now ;
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills are simply grand.’ ”
Then there is the case of Mrs. Spear, of 32
Goodsell-street, St. Peters, Sydney, who suffered
for a good while with her back, a constant dull
ache about the kidneys, who was at last cured by
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.
And there are the cases of Mrs. M. Isaacs,
21 Albion-street, Surry Hills; Mrs. Gregg, 9
Enmore-street, Newtown ; Joseph Grieve, 18
Albion-street, Surry Hills, Mr. E. Hansen, 41
Rosser-street, Balmain ; Mrs. F. May, Excelsior-
street, Woollahra; and hundreds of others, all
cured by Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills of illness,
the full facts of their cases having already been
published in the Sydney papers.
WHY?

in Sydney straight out, honest statements from Sydney people ? No j they publish names of people
from some far away town.

Itchiness of the Skin.
Ever have any irritation of the skin ? There are
many forms'of it, any of them bad enough to tax
your patience. Hemmorrhoid, a plague of the
night; no rest for the sufferer from that com-

A Love-Song.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Now that our lives are one in daily use
And household duties claim our mutual aid,
Existence is too level to induce
The rain of song,” she said.
“The unwedded lover must unpack his heart
Of words fire-purged from dross of common speech;
The husband needs no ardour and no art
To impassion and beseech.
“ Week after week the little temple bells
Tinkled and called me to devotion due,
The while you prayed in faltering syllables,
And bade me kneel with you.
“ Here am I, kneeling by your side, and here,
With downcast eyes, my crimson beads I tell;
But shall I, at the accustomed season, hear
The tinkling of the bell ?.”
* * * *
0 fragrance of my life, my spirit’s light,
Eacli act, each word, each heart-beat is a note
In song as wide and wonderful as Night,
Though I sing naught by rote.
My very self is music. Through your eyes
I see a universe by Love made whole,
And splendour shining softly through them lies
On my Memnonian soul.
N.S.W.
J LE GAY BRERETON.
My Valley.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last week I was out walking up the North
Shore line, when I came to the bottom of a steep
path suddenly, and—it all seems like a dream.
Half an hour from the city, and you leave the
red red road, and turn away westward.
The path falls steeply, falls, falls, falls ; all in a
moment you are there. The Valley lies at your
feet, the mysterious Valley, strange whispers of
which have come floating to the outer world. I
have often heard of the valley where everybody
marries everybody, but I never believed it existed.
And here it lies, a wide, green, laughing place,
miles wide, miles and miles long. Here and there
on the hillsides, and up the valley itself, are
houses ; they look like homes. They rear them-
selves so gently, with such fronts of woodenness
and peace, from luxuriant melon-slopes and
orchards. Their golden smoke goes curling up
through the afternoon sunlight, and you long to
go in and become part of them, even as they are
part of the beautiful closed-in world outside them.
Low hills lie all round. The world is shut right
off. You are out of everything altogether.
Here in this valley the ordinary ways of life—
Australian life as we know it—cease ; something
foreign, something French, a trace of the Maupas-
sant or Flaubert steals in. They do as they like
here. You see two women with bare feet, dig-
ging in dull brown dresses on a green slope.
Their blue sun-bonnets are bent till the field of
peas throws up an emerald background behind
them. You come to a little grave with a rail-
ing round it at the bottom of an orchard. She
was a daughter who died thirty years ago, but
fresh flowers are in the bottle at the foot of the
grave. You pass wooden oases, the lids open to
show the gleaming scarlet of ripe tomatoes, or the
dull amber and pink of peaches. You feel an in-
tense, exquisite silence in the midst of work and
life. You watch men picking plums, and a woman
washing at a stream away on the left hill. She
works as if life were all before her and yet throws
an agility into her movements, and hums happily,
while her little crippled son lies on the grass near.
You look all along the valley and you see a cottage
up the slopes, and you are told how that was the
house Grandfather was born in. Fancy a grand-
father being born in a land where most- grand-
fathers belong to a prehistoric age, and are, in fact,
merely conjectural! Great-Grandfather lived there.
Mother, and uncles, and aunts, were born there.
Everybody is related to everybody.
Is it Poe’s valley—that “ greenest of our valleys
by good angels tenanted,” we have stumbled into,
five minutes from the station, half-an-hour from
Sydney. Years ago—9o years ago—Great-Grand-
father Owned the valley, Just fancy a great-
grandfather ! Years went on, and Grandfather
married, and his children married, and cousins
married cousins, but they all clung to the valley.
The sons brought their wives, the daughters their
husbands, and they built new houses on its sloping
sides, and it began to be a hive of homes. Aunts
and uncles, brothers and sisters, they went on
marrying among themselves until relationship got
lost altogether. They are all mixed up now.
They don’t know what they are themselves. But
they stayed on there in the Valley; and they stay
there still, and one name predominates.
In the Valley they live by fruit. They rise
before dawn, they dress by candle-light, they
breakfast by the earliest rays of the sun. Men
and women alike, they begin their picking and
packing, peaches, apples, melons, tomatoes, plums
and pears, and when it is half-past 10 in the
morning they take a rest and sit down to their
mid-day dinner of vegetables and a peach or
apple pie.
They work on after that till five. Then they
take their tea. And early, almost with the sun-
set, they go to rest—the sweet still rest of a harmv
valley.
The utter peace of this valley of orchards creeps
into one’s soul. And these relatives live in har-
mony, and love each other. They go in and out
of each other’s houses all day long, and at Xmas
they gather in the old homestead, a stone’s-throw
away. If you try to realise where you are you are
lost. You cannot do it. You may not convince
yourself that you are not in another world. You
are in another life altogether. And again the
women in brown, digging peas, flash before you,
and Great-Grandfather’s cottage, the house Grand-
father was born in, and away to the left, a little
white place where the valley buries its dead. For
they die in the happy valley. What else would
you expect ? They marrv and intermarry, and grey
mists rise from the creek where the willows shed
red needles on the banks, and their roots turn
yellow like sponges under the creek waters, and
they die, many of them in early youth.
But in death, as in life, the valley is not divided
It dies, and is buried, here among its own It has
its own little cemetery, its own pastor to bury
aU d who r ik herJv!f rS ’ mothers - little children,
them 1 here belong to some one who lovei
them, close at hand. Over the graves grows such
a luxuriance of violets that I think to lee the
place in spring, and the blueness thrills through
my senses. Headstones of purest white mark all
the graves, little and big. The quiet place is full
fence Ap ' A f ’ ~t e £ um looks ov er the
tence. Acacias droop silently all around. And
in the winter, when the frosts come, loving hands
p °™ and ky Ws across the graves—for the vio-
AnH in +1 ? ay ~ to keep the frost from the violets !

LOUISE MACK.
He took to the Track.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We all knew there would be a row soon between
the old man and George; and when George re-
fused to help in some chaff-cutting, saying it was
too hot, the storm came. The old man bucked
about and swore he was going to kill the lazy dog.
George grumbled, and said he wasn’t going to
a of himself for anyone.
1 he old woman cried and tried to mend matters,
even offering to take George’s place ; but the old
man wouldn t listen to it. At last he said, “ This
is wot it amounts to : you either give us a hand
or take to bluey.”
George, amidst crying from the old woman and
gaudy language from the old man, took up his
swag. I hat night he got out as far as Whelan’s
(three miles from our place). Next night he
-j past EWncl ge’s in a spring-cart. The
hid ridges live two miles past Whelan’s. George
was taking to the track in earnest. Five miles in
two days !
Ihe next night, as Jack and I were sitting on
the doorstep, we noticed someone sneaking around
about 40yards from the house. We were watch-
ing the figure when the old man came out. On
s 'eing it lie called out, “ Hey ! you ! come ’ere !
\v ot yer want? ”
1 he person addressed sidled up, when what was
our surprise to find out it was George ! Shame-
• and in a plaintive voice, he asked,
‘ Could I have a clean shirt, Dad, an’ another tie ?
I only took one of each with me.”
Come inside,” said Dad. “You was never
cut out for the track.”
“I’m off again in the morning,” said George,
hnng-up.
“ We ll see ! ” remarked Dad.
In the morning he went to George’s room. It
was empty. “By Gum! he’s gone,” bethought.
,1 ust then the ring of the axe caught his ear. He
went out. There, at the wood-heap, stood George
working- away for deal life. “Father,” he said,’
j r eckon y ouie t°° eld fer too much graft, so
111 help yer.”
So George stayed.
But what a change has come over Dad ! He
seldom works now, so George has to do his work
too. But he doesn’t grumble much.
• Yesterday Jack and I suggested that he should
give Dad Ins choice—the bluey or a bit of fencing.
Wy leg bears the mark of a pick-handle, and
Jack’s nose the mark of George’s fist.
RIVERINA.
At St. Arnaud (Vic.) they are taking up a
collection for a public fountain to commemorate
the deeds of Australia’s warriors in S’Afriea.
“ There is a Fountain filled with Blood.”
A warder in a certain Australian lunatic-
asylum recently underwent a severe ordeal. He
and a few of his colleagues, male and female, had
arranged for a quiet evening’s entertainment, and
provided suitable and unsuitable refreshments, in-
cluding a small barrel of beer. The festivities
went off splendidly, and when dancing was in full
swing this warder suggested to a female attendant
that they might find in his room further bottled
gladness of a better character than that in the
cask. The lady was nothing loth, but they had
scarcely entered the room when somebody else
was heard approaching. Concealing themselves
hurriedly, they saw an inferior attendant enter
stealthily and ransack the occupant’s belongings,
finally wrenching open a cash-box and abstracting
several sovereigns. The thief left as silently as
he came, and ever since that warder has been in a
quandary. He cannot tell the truth because he
would have to reveal himself guilty of two dis-
missable offences —introducing liquor into the asy-
lum and permitting a female attendant to be in
his room. But the whole asylum knows the offen-
der, and if the latter should be violently assaulted
one night in the dark by a dangerous lunatic
nobody connected with the institution will be a
bit surprised.
The recently-cabled discovery of a new
first-magnitude star in the northern constellation
Perseus is only worth the fuss of wiring-out
because of the brilliance of the stranger. These
new or “ temporary ” stars are not so very uncom-
mon, but as they generally are telescopic objects
they only attract attention in astronomical circles.
The sudden apparition of a star brighter than
Capella or Vega, the leaders of the northern stars,
would naturally hold public attention. In the
southern sky is a star known as “ Eta Argus ”,
Usually of the 6th. magnitude this stellar point
occasionally blazes up brighter than Sirius the
Dog-star. This is known as a “ variable ” star
with “maxima” of greatest brightness every 70
years, the' next flare-up being booked for some-
where about 1910. But for the new star to be of
this class would mean that it had a period of
enormous length for there is no record of its previ-
ous appearance in modern history. So the prob-
ability is that Nova Persei is but a casual brilliant.
Already it is on the wane, and soon will be lost to
sight. As to the cause. Human reasoning, based
on terrestial analogies, can only think of a collision.
Suppose two worlds in terrific impact, or a body
like oar earth precipitating herself into a body
like our sun ! The resultant explosion and com-
bustion would beggar the imagination of even an
O’Sullivan. Still, scientists are loth to think of
such a run-away in the well-ordered thoroughfares
of the interstellar spaces, and only put the theory
forward to account for the instantaneous character
of the appearance of the new star. Though light
travels at the inconceivable rate of 180,000 miles
per second, it has taken years, maybe centuries,
for the spectators on our planet to note the effect
of this supposed collision. Earth-dwellers can
contemplate the risk of such a mishap to their own
little speck in the universe with complacency.
The nearest “ fixed ” star is too far off for us to
observe its motion except with the most delicate
instruments, and it is pretty big odds against
one of them coming this way.
